Interpreting the Proof: Possibility and Origination
Commentator clarifies the intended meaning of 'either by its possibility or by its origination'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator reconciles the phrase 'either by its possibility or by its origination' with the majority Ash'ari position that the cause of need is origination, not mere possibility.

Core Idea
The cause of need is origination, not mere possibility.

This passage delves into the interpretation of a key phrase in the proof of God's existence. The commentator clarifies that the apparent meaning—that need is caused by mere possibility—is not the intended one for the theologians; rather, possibility must be coupled with origination. We will examine the commentator's reasoning and the debate behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key terms and the commentator's argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The commentator discusses two ways to interpret 'either by its possibility or by its origination'. One is apparent (possibility alone), the other is the commentator's preferred reading (possibility conditioned by origination, or origination alone).
3
Understand the commentator's reasoning
The commentator rejects the apparent reading because it contradicts the majority of theologians. He then offers an alternative interpretation of the phrase and criticizes a far-fetched reading.
Consider why the commentator says restricting the path would have made it clearer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إما بإمكانه أو بحدوثه المتبادر من هذه العبارة ومن سياق كلامه فيما بعد هو الاستدلال بالإمكان وحده ولما كان مخالفاً لمذهب جمهور المتكلمين وقد قيد المسلك بكونه للمتكلمين، ولم يسبق من المصنف تعرض لقول بعضهم بأن علة الاحتياج مجرد الإمكان لم يلتفت إليه الشارح، وحمل قوله إما بإمكانه على اعتبار الإمكان مع اعتبار الحدوث شرطاً أو شطراً، وقوله : أو بحدوثه على اعتبار الحدوث وحده فقوله بناء متعلق لمجموع قوله إما بإمكانه أو بحدوثه وجعله متعلقاً بالحدوث فقط حتى يتجه حمل الإمكان على الإمكان المجرد بعيد جداً، والحق أنه لو لم يقيد المسلك الأول بذلك كما لا يقيد في المحصل لكان أظهر.
His statement: (either by its possibility or by its origination). What is apparent from this expression and from the context of his speech later is that it is inference by possibility alone. And since that contradicts the doctrine of the majority of theologians, and he has restricted the path to being 'for the theologians', and the author had not previously mentioned the view of some that the cause of need is mere possibility, the commentator paid no attention to it. So he interpreted his statement 'either by its possibility' as considering possibility together with considering origination as a condition or a part, and his statement 'or by its origination' as considering origination alone. So his statement 'based on' (بنائً) is connected to the entirety of his statement 'either by its possibility or by its origination'; making it connected only to 'origination' so that possibility can be taken as absolute possibility is far-fetched. The truth is that had he not restricted the first path in this way, just as it is not restricted in al-Muḥaṣṣal, it would have been clearer.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage comments on the phrase in the matn: 'either by its possibility or by its origination'. The commentator notes that the apparent reading and context suggest the argument relies on possibility alone. However, this view—that mere possibility necessitates a creator—is contrary to the position of the majority of Ashʻarī theologians, who hold that the cause of need is origination, not mere possibility. Since the matn author had already specified that this first path is 'for the theologians', and since he did not previously mention the alternative view (that the cause is mere possibility), the commentator disregards the apparent reading. Instead, he interprets 'by its possibility' as including possibility together with origination as a condition or part, and 'by its origination' as considering origination alone. He then argues that the word 'based on' (بناء) must refer to the whole disjunction, not just to origination; otherwise it would be strained. The commentator concludes that the truth is that if the first path had not been restricted in this way—like in al-Rāzī's al-Muḥaṣṣal—the intended meaning would have been clearer.

Key Terms
إمكان
حدوث
علة الاحتياج
استدلال بالإمكان
المسلك الأول
المتكلمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of God's existence from possibility works by considering mere possibility alone, without reference to origination.
Correction: For the Ash'ari theologians, the cause of need is origination, not mere possibility. Thus, when the phrase mentions 'possibility', it must be understood as possibility conditioned by origination.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the correct interpretation of 'either by its possibility or by its origination' in the first path?
A) It means two separate arguments: one by possibility alone and one by origination alone
B) It means considering possibility with origination as a condition or part, and considering origination alone
C) It means only origination is considered
D) It means possibility is sufficient without origination

Key Takeaway
The commentator insists that the first path's argument incorporates both possibility and origination, not possibility alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator set aside his own apparent reading because it contradicted the majority of scholars. Is there an area in your faith where you have clung to a personal understanding despite the broader scholarly consensus? What would it take for you to sincerely revisit that position?
?
When you read the phrase 'either by its possibility or by its origination', your immediate reaction might have been different from the commentator's. How do you react when a trusted teacher or text shows you that your first understanding was incomplete? Will you humbly adjust, or do you resist?
?
This lesson reveals that even technical theological arguments require careful attention to context and tradition. In your daily life, when do you tend to take the easy, surface-level meaning of a religious text? How can you cultivate the habit of digging deeper and consulting the scholars before concluding?

Abraham's Proof from the Origination of Celestial Bodies
First of four ways: inference by origination of setting substances, attributed to Abraham.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abraham's statement 'I do not love the setting ones' serves as a rational proof for the existence of a Creator, based on the origination of celestial substances.

Core Idea
Setting proves change; change proves origination; origination proves a Maker.

Abraham's proof is one of the classical arguments in Islamic theology for the existence of God. By reflecting on the setting of celestial bodies, he demonstrated that they are originated and therefore cannot be gods. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of that argument, including the Mu'tazila's elaboration and a key objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation.
Read the Arabic text of the passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; don't worry about understanding every detail on first read.
2
Identify the core inference.
Find the logical chain: setting (change) → origination of substance → need for a Maker. Trace how Abraham's statement 'I do not love the setting ones' leads to this conclusion.
The preamble affirms that possibility is considered with origination as a condition.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila's additional argument and the objection.
The Mu'tazila argued that our own originated actions prove the need for an agent, and analogously the world's substances. Then an objection is raised: could the proof be based on possibility rather than origination? Note how the response defends the origination-based approach.
This section deepens your understanding of the proof's foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في إثبات الصانع الامكان مع الحدوث شرطاً أو شطراً. فهذه وجوه أربعة . الأول : الاستدلال بحدوث الآفلين ( وهو الجواهر) قيل هذا طريقة الخليل صلوات الرحمن وسلامه عليه حيث قال لا أحب الآفلين أن العالم الجوهري أي المتحيز بالذات حادث كما مر كل حادث فله محدث، كما تشهد به بديهة العقل فإن من رأى بناء رفيعاً حادثاً بأن له بانياً، وذهب أكثر مشايخ المعتزلة إلى أن هذه المقدمة استدلالية واستدلوا عليها تارة بأن أفعالنا محدثة ومحتاجة إلى الفاعل لحدوثها فكذا الجواهر المحدثة لأن علة جزم قوله : ( حيث قال لا أحب الآفلين أي لا أحبهم فضلاً عن عبادتهم لأن الأفول حادث لحدوث عارضه الدال على حدوثه أعني الأفول وما هو حادث فله محدث غيره فلا يكون مبدأ لجميع الحوادث فلا يكون صانعاً للعالم ولا يكون محبوباً للعاقل . ثم إن الأقوال أعني الغيبة وإن عم الجوهر والعرض إلا قوله عليه السلام لا أحب الآفلين نفي لربوبية الجوهر على الوجه المذكور، فلهذا قيل باختصاص طريقه عليه السلام بحدوث الجوهر وبهذا التقرير سقط ما يقال من أن اللازم من استدلاله عليه السلام عدم صلاحية الآفل رباً لا ثبوت الصانع للعالم، وإن الحدوث يقتضي المحدث هذا فإن قلت يحتمل أن يكون استدلاله عليه السلام بالإمكان بأن يكون : حاصله أن الأفول تغير من حال إلى حال والتغير يقتضي إمكان المتغير المحوج إلى علة قلت : هذا إنما يصح إذا علم اقتضاء التغير إمكان المتغير بلا ملاحظة استلزامه الحدوث وإلا فالتجاوز عنه إلى الإمكان بعد العلم به، وكونه أظهر في الدلالة على المطلوب بعيد كل البعد، والظاهر أن العلم بذلك الاقتضاء لا يتحقق بدون تلك الملاحظة.
The first observatory - the first aim: on proving the Maker. Possibility with origination as a condition or part. So these are four aspects. The first: inference by the origination of the setting ones (which is the substances). It is said this is the way of al-Khalil, may the prayers of the Merciful and His peace be upon him, when he said 'I do not love the setting ones' that the substantial world, i.e., that which is extended in essence, is originated, as passed, every originated thing has a maker, as the innate intellect testifies, for whoever sees a lofty building originated knows that it has a builder. And most of the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila held that this premise is inferential, and they argued for it sometimes by saying that our actions are originated and need an agent due to their origination, so likewise the originated substances, because the cause of certainty [؟] His saying: (where he said 'I do not love the setting ones' meaning I do not love them, let alone worship them, because the setting is originated due to the origination of its accident that indicates its origination, namely the setting, and whatever is originated has a maker other than it, so it cannot be the source of all originated things, so it cannot be the maker of the world, nor can it be loved by the rational one. Then the statements concerning the unseen, though general to substance and accident, except his saying peace be upon him 'I do not love the setting ones' is a negation of the lordship of the substance in the mentioned manner. Therefore it is said that his way is specific to the origination of substance. By this explanation, what is said falls away, namely that the necessary consequence of his inference is the unfitness of the setting thing to be a lord, not the proof of the Maker of the world, and that origination necessitates the maker. If you say: it is possible that his inference was by possibility, meaning that its gist is that setting is a change from one state to another, and change necessitates the possibility of the changing thing, which requires a cause. I say: this is only valid if one knows that change necessitates the possibility of the changing thing without observing its entailment of origination; otherwise, bypassing it to possibility after knowing it, and it being more apparent in indicating the desired, is far away. And it seems that knowledge of that entailment cannot be realized without that observation.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage presents the first of the four aspects of the proof from origination, focusing on the argument from the setting (ufool) of celestial bodies, attributed to the Prophet Abraham (al-Khalīl). It begins by restating that possibility is considered with origination as a condition. The first way is inference by the origination of the 'setting ones' (al-āfilīn), i.e., substances (jawāhir). The text cites Abraham's statement 'Lā uḥibbu al-āfilīn' (I do not love the setting ones) from the Qur'an. The reasoning is that the setting (afool) is a change that indicates the origination of the substance, and every originated thing needs a maker. This is compared to seeing a high building, which indicates a builder. The Mu'tazila added an argument from our actions: our actions are originated and need an agent, so likewise the world's substances. The commentator then explains that Abraham's statement specifically negates the lordship of the substance in that way, and this refutes the objection that the proof only shows the unsuitability of the setting thing as a lord, not the existence of a Creator. The text then addresses a possible objection: that Abraham's inference might be based on possibility (imkān) rather than origination, arguing that change implies possibility. The response is that this would only be valid if one knows that change implies possibility without also implying origination, but that is not the case. The discussion shows that the orthodox position relies on origination, not mere possibility.

Key Terms
الآفلين
الأفول
الخليل
الحدوث
الجواهر
المتحيز بالذات
بديهة العقل
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abraham's proof is based on possibility (imkān) rather than origination (ḥudūth).
Correction: The text argues that the proof relies on origination, not mere possibility, and that change implies possibility only when origination is also observed.

Checkpoint
What is the core of Abraham's (al-Khalīl) proof for the existence of the Creator according to this passage?
A) The order of the cosmos
B) The origination (ḥudūth) of the setting (ufool) of celestial bodies
C) The impossibility of an infinite regress
D) The design of living beings

Key Takeaway
Abraham's proof from the setting of celestial bodies demonstrates that everything that undergoes change is originated and thus cannot be a god, pointing to a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abraham saw the setting of stars and refused to love them because they were not the true Lord. What are the 'setting' things in your life—success, relationships, or comforts—that you have unconsciously loved as if they were permanent? Will you now reject them as he did?
?
The innate intellect tells you that every building has a builder. Do you truly live as though the universe has a Creator? When you see the order of the world, does it move your heart to worship? If not, what is blocking that natural recognition?
?
The proof from origination shows that whatever changes cannot be God. Is there any created thing you have elevated to a status that belongs only to Him? How will you remove that from your heart today?

The Intuitive Proof of Abraham and the Problem of Entailment
Discussion of the weakness of entailment proofs and the ḥadsī justification of Abraham's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difficulty in proving that the origination of an accident entails the origination of its substance, and how Abraham's argument can be understood as an intuitive proof.

Core Idea
Abraham's proof was intuitive, not demonstrative — it relied on inner certainty.

This passage addresses a subtle objection to the famous argument of Abraham (al-Khalil) from the setting of celestial bodies. Critics question whether the entailment from accident to substance is sound. The response reveals that Abraham's reasoning was intuitive (ḥadsī), rooted in his prophetic insight. This lesson unpacks that discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the objection and the response.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the text states that the proofs of entailment of accident to substance are weak. Understand the problem: Abraham used the setting (afil) to prove its subject is not a lord.
The objection is that the entailment is not necessary.
3
Understand the response
The text offers an intuitive (ḥadsī) interpretation. Abraham's strong intuition allowed him to see directly that the subject is not fit for divinity.
This bypasses the logical difficulty.
4
Reflect on ḥadsī arguments
Consider how intuition can yield certainty in some kinds of knowledge, even if formal demonstration is lacking.
This is a key epistemological point in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي هاهنا شيء وهو أن أدلة استلزام حدوث الحال لحدوث المحل مما لا يكاد كما سيجيء. فكيف استدل الخليل عليه السلام بحدوث الأفول على حدوث محله وإنه ليس رباً على أن كون الأفول أمراً موجوداً مما يمكن أن يناقش فيه . نعم كونه أمراً متجدداً مما لا خفاء فيه لكن محل المتجدد لا يلزم أن يكون حادثا، وقد يقال : إن ذلك منه عليه السلام برهان حدسي فإنه عليه السلام لقوة حدسه استفاد منه أن معروضه لا يصلح للألوهية . يصح قوله : (أي المتحيز بالذات ) فسر العالم الجوهري به لأن المجردات غير ثابتة عندنا . قوله : (بناء رفيعا) التقييد بالرفيع ليس للاحتراز بل لزيادة مشابهته للعالم الجوهري المشتمل على النظام الأحسن، فإن في هذا رفعة شأن كما أن في ذلك رفعة محسوسة . قوله : ( بأن أفعالنا محدثة ومحتاجة إلى الفاعل لحدوثها الظاهر أن المراد من الفعل نفس الحاصل بالمصدر لا المعنى المصدري أو الحاصل به من حيث هو كذلك، لأن احتياج الفعل إلى الفاعل حينئذ لكونه فعلاً وإن كان قديماً ضروري . فإن قلت : ما ذكروه تمثل يفيد الظن والمسألة المطالب العقلية التي يطلب فيها اليقين قلت : قد سبق أن العلة إذا كانت قطعية في التمثيل يرجع إلى القياس ويفيد اليقين وعليه بنو كلامهم لكن يرد عليه أن لا نسلم أن الواحد منا محدث لأفعاله، ودعوى الضرورة لا تسمع هاهنا قطعا وإن أرادوا بالفاعل مطلق المؤثر يرد المنع عليه أيضاً ، فلعل أفعالنا عند دواعينا حدثت اتفاقاً بلا مؤثر فإن بني هذا على مجرد أن كل حدث لا من
There remains here something, and it is that the proofs of the entailment of the origination of the accident for the origination of the subject are hardly [conceivable?] as will come. So how did al-Khalil, peace be upon him, infer by the origination of the setting the origination of its subject and that it is not a lord, while the fact that the setting is an existing thing can be argued about? Yes, its being a renewed thing is not hidden, but the subject of the renewed is not necessarily originated. And it may be said: that from him, peace be upon him, is an intuitive proof, for he, due to the strength of his intuition, derived from it that its subject is not fit for divinity. His saying (i.e., that which is extended in essence) explains the substantial world by it because abstract entities are not established with us. His saying (a lofty building): the restriction by 'lofty' is not for exclusion but to increase its similarity to the substantial world containing the best system, for in this there is loftiness of status just as in that there is sensible loftiness. His saying (that our actions are originated and need the agent due to their origination): it is apparent that the intended meaning of 'action' is the actual result of the infinitive, not the infinitive meaning or the result as such, because the need of the action for the agent then, due to its being an action even if it were eternal, is necessary. For if you say: what they mentioned is a representation that yields probability, while the issue is rational matters in which certainty is sought. I say: it has preceded that if the cause is certain in the representation, it reverts to syllogism and yields certainty, and upon that they built their discourse. But it is objected that we do not admit that any one of us is the originator of his actions, and the claim of necessity is not heard here at all. And if they mean by agent the absolute influence, the denial is also made against it, for perhaps our actions occur at our motives by coincidence without any agent. So if this is based on mere [the principle] that every event [does not occur without an agent?].
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage addresses remaining issues and objections to the proof from origination. First, it notes a difficulty: the proofs that the origination of an accident (like setting) entails the origination of its subject (the substance) are weak, as will be discussed later. Yet Abraham used this to prove his point. The response is that Abraham's proof may be an intuitive (ḥadsī) argument, relying on his strong intuition. The commentator then clarifies two points: (1) The term 'al-ʿālam al-jawharī' (the substantial world) is explained as that which is extended in essence (mutaḥayyiz bi-al-dhāt), because abstract entities are not accepted by us. (2) The building analogy: the qualification 'lofty' is not to exclude low buildings but to emphasize similarity to the well-ordered world. Next, the Mu'tazilī argument from our actions is examined: The action (fiʿl) is the actual result (al-ḥāṣil) of the source, not the verbal noun, because then the need for an agent would be necessary even if the act is eternal. An objection is raised: this analogy yields only probability, whereas the issue requires certainty. The response: if the cause in the analogy is certain, it becomes a syllogism yielding certainty. However, a stronger objection follows: we do not admit that we are the originators of our actions; this is not self-evident. Moreover, even if 'agent' means any influence, it can be denied that our actions are caused; they might occur by coincidence without any agent. This exposes the epistemic vulnerability of the argument from human actions.

Key Terms
الحدس
البرهان الحدسي
الاستلزام
الأفول
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Abraham's argument is a formal logical proof.
Correction: It is actually an intuitive proof based on his strong intuition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can Abraham's inference from the setting not be considered a strict logical proof?
A) The setting is not a real event
B) The entailment from accident to subject is not necessary
C) Abraham did not use reasoning
D) The passage does not address this

Key Takeaway
The entailment of origination from accident to substance is not logically necessary, so Abraham's proof must be understood as an intuitive insight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own belief in Allah, do you base it more on logical arguments or an inner certainty? How does Abraham's reliance on intuition challenge you to examine the foundation of your own faith?
?
The passage says that the entailment from an accident to its subject is weak. Are there aspects of your faith that you try to prove with arguments that may be insufficient? How can you strengthen your certainty like Abraham did?
?
Abraham's strong intuition gave him direct knowledge that the setting object is not fit for divinity. Do you have an intuitive sense of God's presence? How can you cultivate that inner ḥads in your daily life?

The Argument from Actions and Its Objections
Examination of the argument from human actions and two objections to its certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilī argument that our actions prove a creator, and the objections that challenge the certainty of that argument, including the possibility of accidental occurrence.

Core Idea
Even if actions need an agent, we cannot be certain that we are the agents — actions might occur by coincidence.

This passage examines the Mu'tazilī argument that our own originated actions prove the need for a creator. The text clarifies what is meant by 'action', then raises two powerful objections: the analogy yields only probability, and we cannot be certain that we are the originators of our actions. This lesson unpacks that critical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: first a clarification about the building analogy, then the argument from actions, then objections.
The passage continues from the previous lesson.
2
Understand the definition of 'action'
Focus on where the text says 'the intended meaning of action is the actual result of the infinitive'. This is important for the argument.
Contrast with the infinitive meaning.
3
Analyze the first objection
Find the objection that the analogy yields only probability (ẓann) while certainty (yaqīn) is required. Note the response that if the cause is certain, it becomes a syllogism yielding certainty.
4
Analyze the second objection
Identify the stronger objection: 'we do not admit that any one of us is the originator of his actions'. The text suggests actions might occur by coincidence (ittifāqan) without an agent.
This undermines the whole argument.
5
Consider the implications
Reflect on how these objections affect the proof from actions. Is it still a valid argument? What assumptions does it rely on?
This shows the epistemic vulnerability of the argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي هاهنا شيء وهو أن أدلة استلزام حدوث الحال لحدوث المحل مما لا يكاد كما سيجيء. فكيف استدل الخليل عليه السلام بحدوث الأفول على حدوث محله وإنه ليس رباً على أن كون الأفول أمراً موجوداً مما يمكن أن يناقش فيه . نعم كونه أمراً متجدداً مما لا خفاء فيه لكن محل المتجدد لا يلزم أن يكون حادثا، وقد يقال : إن ذلك منه عليه السلام برهان حدسي فإنه عليه السلام لقوة حدسه استفاد منه أن معروضه لا يصلح للألوهية . يصح قوله : (أي المتحيز بالذات ) فسر العالم الجوهري به لأن المجردات غير ثابتة عندنا . قوله : (بناء رفيعا) التقييد بالرفيع ليس للاحتراز بل لزيادة مشابهته للعالم الجوهري المشتمل على النظام الأحسن، فإن في هذا رفعة شأن كما أن في ذلك رفعة محسوسة . قوله : ( بأن أفعالنا محدثة ومحتاجة إلى الفاعل لحدوثها الظاهر أن المراد من الفعل نفس الحاصل بالمصدر لا المعنى المصدري أو الحاصل به من حيث هو كذلك، لأن احتياج الفعل إلى الفاعل حينئذ لكونه فعلاً وإن كان قديماً ضروري . فإن قلت : ما ذكروه تمثل يفيد الظن والمسألة المطالب العقلية التي يطلب فيها اليقين قلت : قد سبق أن العلة إذا كانت قطعية في التمثيل يرجع إلى القياس ويفيد اليقين وعليه بنو كلامهم لكن يرد عليه أن لا نسلم أن الواحد منا محدث لأفعاله، ودعوى الضرورة لا تسمع هاهنا قطعا وإن أرادوا بالفاعل مطلق المؤثر يرد المنع عليه أيضاً ، فلعل أفعالنا عند دواعينا حدثت اتفاقاً بلا مؤثر فإن بني هذا على مجرد أن كل حدث لا من
There remains here something, and it is that the proofs of the entailment of the origination of the accident for the origination of the subject are hardly [conceivable?] as will come. So how did al-Khalil, peace be upon him, infer by the origination of the setting the origination of its subject and that it is not a lord, while the fact that the setting is an existing thing can be argued about? Yes, its being a renewed thing is not hidden, but the subject of the renewed is not necessarily originated. And it may be said: that from him, peace be upon him, is an intuitive proof, for he, due to the strength of his intuition, derived from it that its subject is not fit for divinity. His saying (i.e., that which is extended in essence) explains the substantial world by it because abstract entities are not established with us. His saying (a lofty building): the restriction by 'lofty' is not for exclusion but to increase its similarity to the substantial world containing the best system, for in this there is loftiness of status just as in that there is sensible loftiness. His saying (that our actions are originated and need the agent due to their origination): it is apparent that the intended meaning of 'action' is the actual result of the infinitive, not the infinitive meaning or the result as such, because the need of the action for the agent then, due to its being an action even if it were eternal, is necessary. For if you say: what they mentioned is a representation that yields probability, while the issue is rational matters in which certainty is sought. I say: it has preceded that if the cause is certain in the representation, it reverts to syllogism and yields certainty, and upon that they built their discourse. But it is objected that we do not admit that any one of us is the originator of his actions, and the claim of necessity is not heard here at all. And if they mean by agent the absolute influence, the denial is also made against it, for perhaps our actions occur at our motives by coincidence without any agent. So if this is based on mere [the principle] that every event [does not occur without an agent?].
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage addresses remaining issues and objections to the proof from origination. First, it notes a difficulty: the proofs that the origination of an accident (like setting) entails the origination of its subject (the substance) are weak, as will be discussed later. Yet Abraham used this to prove his point. The response is that Abraham's proof may be an intuitive (ḥadsī) argument, relying on his strong intuition. The commentator then clarifies two points: (1) The term 'al-ʿālam al-jawharī' (the substantial world) is explained as that which is extended in essence (mutaḥayyiz bi-al-dhāt), because abstract entities are not accepted by us. (2) The building analogy: the qualification 'lofty' is not to exclude low buildings but to emphasize similarity to the well-ordered world. Next, the Mu'tazilī argument from our actions is examined: The action (fiʿl) is the actual result (al-ḥāṣil) of the source, not the verbal noun, because then the need for an agent would be necessary even if the act is eternal. An objection is raised: this analogy yields only probability, whereas the issue requires certainty. The response: if the cause in the analogy is certain, it becomes a syllogism yielding certainty. However, a stronger objection follows: we do not admit that we are the originators of our actions; this is not self-evident. Moreover, even if 'agent' means any influence, it can be denied that our actions are caused; they might occur by coincidence without any agent. This exposes the epistemic vulnerability of the argument from human actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from our actions is considered a decisive proof.
Correction: This passage shows it faces serious objections and may not yield certainty.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection raised against the argument from actions?
A) Our actions are not originated
B) We cannot be certain that we are the originators of our actions
C) The actions might be eternal
D) The agent must be external

Key Takeaway
The argument from human actions is not absolutely certain because we cannot be sure we are the agents; actions might occur without a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you comfortable with the possibility that your actions might occur by coincidence without any agent? Does that thought change how you view responsibility and intention in your daily life?
?
If you cannot be absolutely certain that you are the cause of your own actions, how does that affect your reliance on yourself versus your reliance on Allah?
?
The text challenges the self-evidence of your agency. Examine one action you performed today—can you prove with certainty that you were its sole agent? If not, what does that imply for your sense of control and accountability before Allah?

Allah's Existence
intermediate

The First Proof: Temporality of the Heavens and Ibrahim's Argument
First proof of God's existence: huduth al-aflak and Ibrahim's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first proof for God's existence based on the temporality of the heavens and the argument of Prophet Ibrahim (AS).

Core Idea
The act of setting proves temporality, and what is temporal needs a Creator.

This lesson introduces the first and most famous proof for the existence of God in Islamic theology: the argument from the temporality (huduth) of the celestial spheres. The proof is rooted in the Quranic narrative of Prophet Ibrahim, who rejected the setting heavenly bodies as lords. We will examine the logical steps of this proof and the Mu'tazilite claim that its premise is inferential.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Start by reading the Arabic passage that presents the first proof and Ibrahim's statement 'I do not love the setting ones'. Note how the proof is introduced.
The proof is called 'istidlal bi huduth al-afilin'.
2
Understand the logic of Ibrahim's argument
The passage explains that the act of setting (afool) is a temporal accident, indicating the temporality of its substrate. Therefore, the heavenly bodies cannot be eternal or divine.
This is a rational proof, not just a sensory observation.
3
Note the Mu'tazilite position
The text mentions that most Mu'tazilites considered the premise 'every temporal has a cause' as inferential, not intuitive. This will be developed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : الاستدلال بحدوث الآفلين ( وهو الجواهر ) قيل هذا طريقة الخليل صلوات الرحمن وسلامه عليه حيث قال : لا أحب الآفلين . وذهب أكثر مشايخ المعتزلة إلى أن هذه المقدمة استدلالية واستدلوا عليها تارة بأن أفعالنا محدثة ومحتاجة إلى الفاعل لحدوثها فكذا الجواهر المحدثة لأن علة الجزم . قوله : ( حيث قال لا أحب الآفلين أي لا أحبهم فضلاً عن عبادتهم لأن الأفل حادث لحدوث عارضه الدال على حدوثه أعني الأفول وما هو حادث فله محدث غيره فلا يكون مبدأ لجميع الحوادث فلا يكون صانعاً للعالم ولا يكون محبوباً للعاقل . ثم إن الأقوال أعني الغيبة وإن عم الجوهر والعرض إلا قوله عليه السلام لا أحب الآفلين نفي لربوبية الجوهر على الوجه المذكور، فلهذا قيل باختصاص طريقه عليه السلام بحدوث الجوهر وبهذا التقرير سقط ما يقال من أن اللازم من استدلاله عليه السلام عدم صلاحية الآفل رباً لا ثبوت الصانع للعالم، وإن الحدوث يقتضي المحدث هذا فإن قلت يحتمل أن يكون استدلاله عليه السلام بالإمكان بأن يكون حاصله أن الأفول تغير من حال إلى حال والتغير يقتضي إمكان المتغير المحوج إلى علة قلت : هذا إنما يصح إذا علم اقتضاء التغير إمكان المتغير بلا ملاحظة استلزامه الحدوث وإلا فالتجاوز عنه إلى الإمكان بعد العلم به، وكونه أظهر في الدلالة على المطلوب بعيد كل البعد، والظاهر أن العلم بذلك الاقتضاء لا يتحقق بدون تلك الملاحظة. بقي هاهنا شيء وهو أن أدلة استلزام حدوث الحال لحدوث المحل مما لا يكاد كما سيجيء. فكيف استدل الخليل عليه السلام بحدوث الأفول على حدوث محله وإنه ليس رباً على أن كون الأفول أمراً موجوداً مما يمكن أن يناقش فيه . نعم كونه أمراً متجدداً مما لا خفاء فيه لكن محل المتجدد لا يلزم أن يكون حادثا، وقد يقال : إن ذلك منه عليه السلام برهان حدسي فإنه عليه السلام لقوة حدسه استفاد منه أن معروضه لا يصلح للألوهية . قوله : (أي المتحيز بالذات ) فسر العالم الجوهري به لأن المجردات غير ثابتة عندنا . قوله : (بناء رفيعا) التقييد بالرفيع ليس للاحتراز بل لزيادة مشابهته للعالم الجوهري المشتمل على النظام الأحسن، فإن في هذا رفعة شأن كما أن في ذلك رفعة محسوسة . قوله : ( بأن أفعالنا محدثة ومحتاجة إلى الفاعل لحدوثها الظاهر أن المراد من الفعل نفس الحاصل بالمصدر لا المعنى المصدري أو الحاصل به من حيث هو كذلك، لأن احتياج الفعل إلى الفاعل حينئذ لكونه فعلاً وإن كان قديماً ضروري . فإن قلت : ما ذكروه تمثل يفيد الظن والمسألة المطالب العقلية التي يطلب فيها اليقين قلت : قد سبق أن العلة إذا كانت قطعية في التمثيل يرجع إلى القياس ويفيد اليقين وعليه بنو كلامهم لكن يرد عليه أن لا نسلم أن الواحد منا محدث لأفعاله، ودعوى الضرورة لا تسمع هاهنا قطعا وإن أرادوا بالفاعل مطلق المؤثر يرد المنع عليه أيضاً ، فلعل أفعالنا عند دواعينا حدثت اتفاقاً بلا مؤثر فإن بني هذا على مجرد أن كل حدث لا من الاحتياج مشتركة وأخرى بأن الحادث قد اتصف بالوجود بعد العدم فهو قابل لهما فيكون ممكناً وكل ممكن يحتاج في ترجيح وجوده على عدمه إلى مؤثر كما سلف في الأمور العامة
The first: inference by the temporality of the setting things (al-āfilīn), which are the substances. It is said this is the way of al-Khalīl (peace and blessings be upon him) where he said: 'I do not love the setting ones.' And most of the leading Mu'tazilites held that this premise is inferential, and they argued for it sometimes by [saying] that our actions are temporally originated and need the agent due to their temporality, so likewise the temporally originated substances because the cause of certainty. His saying: (where he said 'I do not love the setting ones' means I do not love them, let alone worship them, because the setting thing is temporally originated due to the temporality of its accidental attribute that indicates its temporality, namely the act of setting, and whatever is temporally originated has a temporal originator other than it, so it cannot be the source of all events, nor can it be the maker of the world, nor can it be loved by the rational person. Then the sayings, I mean the occurrence of absence [ghayba], even if it encompasses substance and accident, except his saying (peace be upon him) 'I do not love the setting ones' is a negation of the lordship of substance in the mentioned manner, so for this reason it is said that his way (peace be upon him) is specific to the temporality of substance. And by this exposition falls what is said: that the necessary consequence of his inference (peace be upon him) is the unfitness of the setting thing to be a lord, not the establishment of the maker of the world, and that temporality necessitates the temporal originator. If you say: It is possible that his inference (peace be upon him) is by possibility, such that its conclusion is that setting is a change from state to state, and change necessitates the possibility of the changing thing which requires a cause. I say: This is only valid if one knows that change necessitates the possibility of the changing thing without observing its entailment of temporality; otherwise, bypassing it to possibility after knowing it, and its being more evident in indicating the desired, is extremely far-fetched. And the apparent is that knowledge of that necessity is not realized without that observation. There remains something here: that the proofs of the entailment of the temporality of the state for the temporality of the substrate are hardly [valid] as will come. So how did al-Khalīl (peace be upon him) infer by the temporality of setting to the temporality of its substrate and that it is not a lord, given that the being of setting as an existent thing is open to discussion? Yes, its being a renewing thing is unambiguous, but the substrate of the renewing thing is not necessarily temporally originated. And it may be said: That from him (peace be upon him) is an intuitive proof (burhān ḥadsī), for he (peace be upon him) due to the strength of his intuition derived from it that its bearer is not suitable for divinity. His saying: (i.e., the self-extended) he interpreted the substantial world by it because the immaterial entities are not established according to us. His saying: (a lofty building) the restriction by 'lofty' is not for exclusion but to increase its similarity to the substantial world that contains the best system, for in this there is loftiness of rank just as in that there is sensible loftiness. His saying: (that our actions are temporally originated and need the agent due to their temporality) it is apparent that what is meant by action is the thing resulting from the verbal noun, not the verbal noun meaning or the resulting thing as such, because the need of the action for the agent then is due to its being an action, even if it is eternal, which is necessary. If you say: What they mentioned is a similitude that yields conjecture, while the issue is about rational demands in which certainty is sought. I say: It has preceded that when the cause is certain in the similitude, it reverts to analogical reasoning and yields certainty, and upon this they built their argument. But it is objected that we do not concede that each of us originates his own actions, and the claim of necessity is absolutely not heard here, and if they mean by agent the absolute efficient cause, the denial is also directed against it, for perhaps our actions occur at our motives coincidentally without an efficient cause. If this is built merely on the fact that every event does not [occur without a cause]. The need is shared, and another [argument] is that the temporal thing has been qualified by existence after non-existence, so it is receptive to both, thus it is possible, and every possible thing needs, in the preponderance of its existence over its non-existence, an efficient cause, as has preceded in the General Matters.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the existence of the Maker, namely the inference from the temporality (huduth) of the setting things (al-āfilīn), i.e., the celestial spheres and substances. It begins by stating that this is the way of Ibrahim (AS) as indicated by his words 'I do not love the setting ones' (Quran 6:76). The author explains that Ibrahim's argument is that the act of setting (afool) is a temporal accident indicating the temporality of its substrate, and whatever is temporal requires a temporal originator distinct from it, so the heavenly bodies cannot be lords or the Creator. The passage then discusses the Mu'tazilite view that the premise 'every temporal has a cause' is inferential, not intuitive. There follows a detailed objection: one might say that Ibrahim's argument actually proves the possibility (imkan) of the changing entity rather than its temporality. The answer explains that this would only be valid if change is known to necessitate possibility without requiring temporality, which is not the case. Then a further objection is raised: the temporality of the state does not necessarily entail the temporality of the substance, and setting may be a renewing state without the substrate being temporal. The reply suggests Ibrahim's proof was intuitive (hadsi). The commentary then defines the substantial world as that which is self-extended (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat), because immaterial entities are not established for the Ash'aris. The example of a lofty building is explained as a similitude for the well-ordered world. Finally, an extended objection-answer sequence discusses whether human actions are truly originated by us, and whether the need for a cause is intuitive or inferential. The passage concludes with the point that the need for a cause is shared and that the temporal thing, being possible, requires an efficient cause.

Key Terms

آفل
أفول
جوهر
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibrahim's argument is purely observational and based on the fact that the sun and moon physically set.
Correction: The argument is rational: it uses the act of setting as a sign of temporality, not the sensory observation itself. The temporality is inferred from the change inherent in setting.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the act of setting (afool) indicate?
A) The existence of a Creator
B) The temporality of its substrate
C) The divinity of the heavenly bodies
D) The eternity of the world

Key Takeaway
The first proof for God's existence uses the temporality of the heavens, derived from the act of setting, to show that they are created and need a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibrahim (AS) declared 'I do not love the setting ones' based on rational proof. Is your love for Allah built on a similar conviction, or is it merely emotional? What would it take for you to reach that level of certainty where your heart truly 'does not love' anything that is not your Creator?
?
This proof shows that the very act of a celestial body setting—something you witness daily—is a sign of its temporality and inability to be god. When you see the sun or moon set, do you consciously remind yourself that its change proves it cannot be your Lord? If not, what habit can you adopt to turn this common sight into a moment of worship?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that the principle 'every temporal has a cause' must be inferred and not taken for granted. Have you ever critically examined the foundations of your own belief in God? Or do you rely on inherited assumptions? This lesson challenges you to own your faith rationally. What step will you take this week to strengthen the rational basis of your iman?

The Objection from Possibility and the Reply
Objection that Ibrahim's argument proves possibility, and the reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that Ibrahim's argument may actually prove possibility rather than temporality, and the scholarly reply.

Core Idea
Change implies possibility, but possibility alone does not entail temporality without an additional premise.

In this lesson, we examine a sophisticated objection to Ibrahim's argument: perhaps the proof actually establishes the possibility (imkan) of the changing entity rather than its temporality (huduth). The author provides a careful response, explaining that the inference to possibility only works if one already knows that change necessitates possibility independently of temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection states that Ibrahim's inference could be based on possibility: change implies that the changing thing is possible and thus needs a cause.
This is a different reading of the same event.
2
Understand the reply
The reply argues that this only works if change is known to necessitate possibility without also implying temporality. Since change actually does imply temporality, the objection is invalid.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider why the author says that bypassing temporality in favor of possibility is 'far-fetched' and that knowledge of change's implications requires understanding its connection to temporality.

Source Text

متجدداً مما لا خفاء فيه لكن محل المتجدد لا يلزم أن يكون حادثا، وقد يقال : إن ذلك منه عليه السلام برهان حدسي فإنه عليه السلام لقوة حدسه استفاد منه أن معروضه لا يصلح للألوهية . قوله : (أي المتحيز بالذات ) فسر العالم الجوهري به لأن المجردات غير ثابتة عندنا . قوله : (بناء رفيعا) التقييد بالرفيع ليس للاحتراز بل لزيادة مشابهته للعالم الجوهري المشتمل على النظام الأحسن، فإن في هذا رفعة شأن كما أن في ذلك رفعة محسوسة . قوله : ( بأن أفعالنا محدثة ومحتاجة إلى الفاعل لحدوثها الظاهر أن المراد من الفعل نفس الحاصل بالمصدر لا المعنى المصدري أو الحاصل به من حيث هو كذلك، لأن احتياج الفعل إلى الفاعل حينئذ لكونه فعلاً وإن كان قديماً ضروري . فإن قلت : ما ذكروه تمثل يفيد الظن والمسألة المطالب العقلية التي يطلب فيها اليقين قلت : قد سبق أن العلة إذا كانت قطعية في التمثيل يرجع إلى القياس ويفيد اليقين وعليه بنو كلامهم لكن يرد عليه أن لا نسلم أن الواحد منا محدث لأفعاله، ودعوى الضرورة لا تسمع هاهنا قطعا وإن أرادوا بالفاعل مطلق المؤثر يرد المنع عليه أيضاً ، فلعل أفعالنا عند دواعينا حدثت اتفاقاً بلا مؤثر فإن بني هذا على مجرد أن كل حدث لا من الاحتياج مشتركة وأخرى بأن الحادث قد اتصف بالوجود بعد العدم فهو قابل لهما فيكون ممكناً وكل ممكن يحتاج في ترجيح وجوده على عدمه إلى مؤثر كما سلف في الأمور العامة
The first: inference by the temporality of the setting things (al-āfilīn), which are the substances. It is said this is the way of al-Khalīl (peace and blessings be upon him) where he said: 'I do not love the setting ones.' And most of the leading Mu'tazilites held that this premise is inferential, and they argued for it sometimes by [saying] that our actions are temporally originated and need the agent due to their temporality, so likewise the temporally originated substances because the cause of certainty. His saying: (where he said 'I do not love the setting ones' means I do not love them, let alone worship them, because the setting thing is temporally originated due to the temporality of its accidental attribute that indicates its temporality, namely the act of setting, and whatever is temporally originated has a temporal originator other than it, so it cannot be the source of all events, nor can it be the maker of the world, nor can it be loved by the rational person. Then the sayings, I mean the occurrence of absence [ghayba], even if it encompasses substance and accident, except his saying (peace be upon him) 'I do not love the setting ones' is a negation of the lordship of substance in the mentioned manner, so for this reason it is said that his way (peace be upon him) is specific to the temporality of substance. And by this exposition falls what is said: that the necessary consequence of his inference (peace be upon him) is the unfitness of the setting thing to be a lord, not the establishment of the maker of the world, and that temporality necessitates the temporal originator. If you say: It is possible that his inference (peace be upon him) is by possibility, such that its conclusion is that setting is a change from state to state, and change necessitates the possibility of the changing thing which requires a cause. I say: This is only valid if one knows that change necessitates the possibility of the changing thing without observing its entailment of temporality; otherwise, bypassing it to possibility after knowing it, and its being more evident in indicating the desired, is extremely far-fetched. And the apparent is that knowledge of that necessity is not realized without that observation. There remains something here: that the proofs of the entailment of the temporality of the state for the temporality of the substrate are hardly [valid] as will come. So how did al-Khalīl (peace be upon him) infer by the temporality of setting to the temporality of its substrate and that it is not a lord, given that the being of setting as an existent thing is open to discussion? Yes, its being a renewing thing is unambiguous, but the substrate of the renewing thing is not necessarily temporally originated. And it may be said: That from him (peace be upon him) is an intuitive proof (burhān ḥadsī), for he (peace be upon him) due to the strength of his intuition derived from it that its bearer is not suitable for divinity. His saying: (i.e., the self-extended) he interpreted the substantial world by it because the immaterial entities are not established according to us. His saying: (a lofty building) the restriction by 'lofty' is not for exclusion but to increase its similarity to the substantial world that contains the best system, for in this there is loftiness of rank just as in that there is sensible loftiness. His saying: (that our actions are temporally originated and need the agent due to their temporality) it is apparent that what is meant by action is the thing resulting from the verbal noun, not the verbal noun meaning or the resulting thing as such, because the need of the action for the agent then is due to its being an action, even if it is eternal, which is necessary. If you say: What they mentioned is a similitude that yields conjecture, while the issue is about rational demands in which certainty is sought. I say: It has preceded that when the cause is certain in the similitude, it reverts to analogical reasoning and yields certainty, and upon this they built their argument. But it is objected that we do not concede that each of us originates his own actions, and the claim of necessity is absolutely not heard here, and if they mean by agent the absolute efficient cause, the denial is also directed against it, for perhaps our actions occur at our motives coincidentally without an efficient cause. If this is built merely on the fact that every event does not [occur without a cause]. The need is shared, and another [argument] is that the temporal thing has been qualified by existence after non-existence, so it is receptive to both, thus it is possible, and every possible thing needs, in the preponderance of its existence over its non-existence, an efficient cause, as has preceded in the General Matters.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the existence of the Maker, namely the inference from the temporality (huduth) of the setting things (al-āfilīn), i.e., the celestial spheres and substances. It begins by stating that this is the way of Ibrahim (AS) as indicated by his words 'I do not love the setting ones' (Quran 6:76). The author explains that Ibrahim's argument is that the act of setting (afool) is a temporal accident indicating the temporality of its substrate, and whatever is temporal requires a temporal originator distinct from it, so the heavenly bodies cannot be lords or the Creator. The passage then discusses the Mu'tazilite view that the premise 'every temporal has a cause' is inferential, not intuitive. There follows a detailed objection: one might say that Ibrahim's argument actually proves the possibility (imkan) of the changing entity rather than its temporality. The answer explains that this would only be valid if change is known to necessitate possibility without requiring temporality, which is not the case. Then a further objection is raised: the temporality of the state does not necessarily entail the temporality of the substance, and setting may be a renewing state without the substrate being temporal. The reply suggests Ibrahim's proof was intuitive (hadsi). The commentary then defines the substantial world as that which is self-extended (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat), because immaterial entities are not established for the Ash'aris. The example of a lofty building is explained as a similitude for the well-ordered world. Finally, an extended objection-answer sequence discusses whether human actions are truly originated by us, and whether the need for a cause is intuitive or inferential. The passage concludes with the point that the need for a cause is shared and that the temporal thing, being possible, requires an efficient cause.

Key Terms
إمكان
تغير
علة
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reply, what is required for the objection (that Ibrahim's argument proves possibility) to be valid?
A) That change is known to necessitate possibility without also implying temporality
B) That change is a temporal accident
C) That possibility is the same as temporality
D) That Ibrahim's argument is sensory

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's argument is about temporality, not mere possibility; the objection that it proves possibility relies on an assumption that is not justified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe changes in your own life—your health, your circumstances, your relationships—do you recognize them as evidence that you are a contingent being brought into existence by God? Or do you treat them merely as natural events? What is stopping you from seeing God's hand in every change?
?
The objector tries to reduce Ibrahim's argument to a proof of possibility, avoiding the stronger conclusion of temporality. Are there areas of your life where you accept a weak explanation (e.g., 'it's possible God exists') instead of the full truth ('God is the Creator and I am accountable to Him')? Why do you settle for less?
?
The reply insists that change inherently shows temporality—that things begin to exist. Consider your own existence: you were not always here. How does that simple fact demand a response from you today, not just intellectually but in your daily choices?

The Intuitive Proof and the Nature of the Substantial World
Reply to the objection about temporality of state, and definition of the substantial world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of an intuitive proof (burhan hadsi) and how it relates to Ibrahim's argument, as well as the definition of the substantial world.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's proof was intuitive, based on the insight that a changing thing cannot be divine, and the substantial world is defined as self-extended.

This lesson addresses a further objection: that the temporality of a state does not entail the temporality of its substrate. The author responds that Ibrahim's argument was an intuitive proof (burhan hadsi) based on his strong intuition. We then examine the definition of the substantial world as 'self-extended' (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat) and the example of a lofty building.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
The objection says that the temporality of the state (setting) does not necessarily imply the temporality of the substrate. The reply suggests that Ibrahim's proof was intuitive.
This acknowledges a weakness in the purely logical deduction.
2
Understand 'burhan hadsi'
An intuitive proof is one that relies on a strong natural insight, not a fully articulated logical syllogism. Ibrahim's insight allowed him to directly see that the bearer cannot be divine.
3
Learn the definition of the substantial world
The text defines the substantial world as 'self-extended' because immaterial entities are not established in this school.
This clarifies the ontology used in the proof.
4
Consider the building analogy
The 'lofty building' is used as an example to increase similarity to the well-ordered world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : الاستدلال بحدوث الآفلين ( وهو الجواهر ) قيل هذا طريقة الخليل صلوات الرحمن وسلامه عليه حيث قال : لا أحب الآفلين . وذهب أكثر مشايخ المعتزلة إلى أن هذه المقدمة استدلالية واستدلوا عليها تارة بأن أفعالنا محدثة ومحتاجة إلى الفاعل لحدوثها فكذا الجواهر المحدثة لأن علة الجزم . قوله : ( حيث قال لا أحب الآفلين أي لا أحبهم فضلاً عن عبادتهم لأن الأفل حادث لحدوث عارضه الدال على حدوثه أعني الأفول وما هو حادث فله محدث غيره فلا يكون مبدأ لجميع الحوادث فلا يكون صانعاً للعالم ولا يكون محبوباً للعاقل . ثم إن الأقوال أعني الغيبة وإن عم الجوهر والعرض إلا قوله عليه السلام لا أحب الآفلين نفي لربوبية الجوهر على الوجه المذكور، فلهذا قيل باختصاص طريقه عليه السلام بحدوث الجوهر وبهذا التقرير سقط ما يقال من أن اللازم من استدلاله عليه السلام عدم صلاحية الآفل رباً لا ثبوت الصانع للعالم، وإن الحدوث يقتضي المحدث هذا فإن قلت يحتمل أن يكون استدلاله عليه السلام بالإمكان بأن يكون حاصله أن الأفول تغير من حال إلى حال والتغير يقتضي إمكان المتغير المحوج إلى علة قلت : هذا إنما يصح إذا علم اقتضاء التغير إمكان المتغير بلا ملاحظة استلزامه الحدوث وإلا فالتجاوز عنه إلى الإمكان بعد العلم به، وكونه أظهر في الدلالة على المطلوب بعيد كل البعد، والظاهر أن العلم بذلك الاقتضاء لا يتحقق بدون تلك الملاحظة. بقي هاهنا شيء وهو أن أدلة استلزام حدوث الحال لحدوث المحل مما لا يكاد كما سيجيء. فكيف استدل الخليل عليه السلام بحدوث الأفول على حدوث محله وإنه ليس رباً على أن كون الأفول أمراً موجوداً مما يمكن أن يناقش فيه . نعم كونه أمراً متجدداً مما لا خفاء فيه لكن محل المتجدد لا يلزم أن يكون حادثا، وقد يقال : إن ذلك منه عليه السلام برهان حدسي فإنه عليه السلام لقوة حدسه استفاد منه أن معروضه لا يصلح للألوهية . قوله : (أي المتحيز بالذات ) فسر العالم الجوهري به لأن المجردات غير ثابتة عندنا . قوله : (بناء رفيعا) التقييد بالرفيع ليس للاحتراز بل لزيادة مشابهته للعالم الجوهري المشتمل على النظام الأحسن، فإن في هذا رفعة شأن كما أن في ذلك رفعة محسوسة . قوله : ( بأن أفعالنا محدثة ومحتاجة إلى الفاعل لحدوثها الظاهر أن المراد من الفعل نفس الحاصل بالمصدر لا المعنى المصدري أو الحاصل به من حيث هو كذلك، لأن احتياج الفعل إلى الفاعل حينئذ لكونه فعلاً وإن كان قديماً ضروري . فإن قلت : ما ذكروه تمثل يفيد الظن والمسألة المطالب العقلية التي يطلب فيها اليقين قلت : قد سبق أن العلة إذا كانت قطعية في التمثيل يرجع إلى القياس ويفيد اليقين وعليه بنو كلامهم لكن يرد عليه أن لا نسلم أن الواحد منا محدث لأفعاله، ودعوى الضرورة لا تسمع هاهنا قطعا وإن أرادوا بالفاعل مطلق المؤثر يرد المنع عليه أيضاً ، فلعل أفعالنا عند دواعينا حدثت اتفاقاً بلا مؤثر فإن بني According to the passage, how does the author explain Ibrahim's inference from the temporality of setting to the temporality of its substrate?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see change everywhere in the world – your own moods, your body, the sun and moon. Have you ever paused to realize that if something changes, it cannot be the ultimate reality? When you feel your heart change from happiness to sadness, does it remind you that your ultimate reliance must be on the Unchanging? What would it take for you to truly live as if only Allah is unchanging and everything else is fleeting?
?
Ibrahim's proof was intuitive – he saw and immediately knew. Do you trust your own intuition when it comes to recognizing the divine? Or have you dulled that natural fitrah with doubts and overthinking? What is one tangible way you could cultivate that intuitive awareness of Allah today?
?
The passage says the substrate of a renewing thing is not necessarily temporally originated. How often do you assume that because something is new or changing, it must have started? How does this affect your understanding of eternal truths? Are there aspects of your faith that you treat as 'temporal' changes rather than unchanging realities?

The Need for a Cause: Human Actions and the Analogical Argument
Mu'tazilite analogy from human actions to prove the need for a cause, and subsequent objections and replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite analogy from human actions to heavenly bodies to establish the need for a cause, and the objections raised against it.

Core Idea
Every temporal event, including human actions, requires a cause, and this is used as evidence for the temporality of substances.

This lesson focuses on the Mu'tazilite argument that human actions are temporally originated and need an agent, and by analogy, the heavenly substances must also be temporally originated and need a Creator. The passage then discusses objections: are human actions truly created by us? Could they happen by coincidence? And the reply that every temporal has a cause is a shared principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite analogy
The Mu'tazilites argued that our actions are temporally originated and need an agent, so likewise the temporally originated substances need an agent.
This is an analogical argument (qiyas tamthili).
2
Understand the objection
An objector denies that we originate our own actions and suggests they may happen by coincidence without a cause.
This challenges the premise of the analogy.
3
Examine the reply
The reply asserts that the need for a cause is a necessary principle, and that the temporal thing, having come into existence, is possible and requires a preponderating cause.
This references the general principle from the 'General Matters'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : الاستدلال بحدوث الآفلين ( وهو الجواهر ) قيل هذا طريقة الخليل صلوات الرحمن وسلامه عليه حيث قال : لا أحب الآفلين . وذهب أكثر مشايخ المعتزلة إلى أن هذه المقدمة استدلالية واستدلوا عليها تارة بأن أفعالنا محدثة ومحتاجة إلى الفاعل لحدوثها فكذا الجواهر المحدثة لأن علة الجزم . قوله : ( حيث قال لا أحب الآفلين أي لا أحبهم فضلاً عن عبادتهم لأن الأفل حادث لحدوث عارضه الدال على حدوثه أعني الأفول وما هو حادث فله محدث غيره فلا يكون مبدأ لجميع الحوادث فلا يكون صانعاً للعالم ولا يكون محبوباً للعاقل . ثم إن الأقوال أعني الغيبة وإن عم الجوهر والعرض إلا قوله عليه السلام لا أحب الآفلين نفي لربوبية الجوهر على الوجه المذكور، فلهذا قيل باختصاص طريقه عليه السلام بحدوث الجوهر وبهذا التقرير سقط ما يقال من أن اللازم من استدلاله عليه السلام عدم صلاحية الآفل رباً لا ثبوت الصانع للعالم، وإن الحدوث يقتضي المحدث هذا فإن قلت يحتمل أن يكون استدلاله عليه السلام بالإمكان بأن يكون حاصله أن الأفول تغير من حال إلى حال والتغير يقتضي إمكان المتغير المحوج إلى علة قلت : هذا إنما يصح إذا علم اقتضاء التغير إمكان المتغير بلا ملاحظة استلزامه الحدوث وإلا فالتجاوز عنه إلى الإمكان بعد العلم به، وكونه أظهر في الدلالة على المطلوب بعيد كل البعد، والظاهر أن العلم بذلك الاقتضاء لا يتحقق بدون تلك الملاحظة. بقي هاهنا شيء وهو أن أدلة استلزام حدوث الحال لحدوث المحل مما لا يكاد كما سيجيء. فكيف استدل الخليل عليه السلام بحدوث الأفول على حدوث محله وإنه ليس رباً على أن كون الأفول أمراً موجوداً مما يمكن أن يناقش فيه . نعم كونه أمراً متجدداً مما لا خفاء فيه لكن محل المتجدد لا يلزم أن يكون حادثا، وقد يقال : إن ذلك منه عليه السلام برهان حدسي فإنه عليه السلام لقوة حدسه استفاد منه أن معروضه لا يصلح للألوهية . قوله : (أي المتحيز بالذات ) فسر العالم الجوهري به لأن المجردات غير ثابتة عندنا . قوله : (بناء رفيعا) التقييد بالرفيع ليس للاحتراز بل لزيادة مشابهته للعالم الجوهري المشتمل على النظام الأحسن، فإن في هذا رفعة شأن كما أن في ذلك رفعة محسوسة . قوله : ( بأن أفعالنا محدثة ومحتاجة إلى الفاعل لحدوثها الظاهر أن المراد من الفعل نفس الحاصل بالمصدر لا المعنى المصدري أو الحاصل به من حيث هو كذلك، لأن احتياج الفعل إلى الفاعل حينئذ لكونه فعلاً وإن كان قديماً ضروري . فإن قلت : ما ذكروه تمثل يفيد الظن والمسألة المطالب العقلية التي يطلب فيها اليقين قلت : قد سبق أن العلة إذا كانت قطعية في التمثيل يرجع إلى القياس ويفيد اليقين وعليه بنو كلامهم لكن يرد عليه أن لا نسلم أن الواحد منا محدث لأفعاله، ودعوى الضرورة لا تسمع هاهنا قطعا وإن أرادوا بالفاعل مطلق المؤثر يرد المنع عليه أيضاً ، فلعل أفعالنا عند دواعينا حدثت اتفاقاً بلا مؤثر فإن بني هذا على مجرد أن كل حدث لا من الاحتياج مشتركة وأخرى بأن الحادث قد اتصف بالوجود بعد العدم فهو قابل لهما فيكون ممكناً وكل ممكن يحتاج في ترجيح وجوده على عدمه إلى مؤثر كما سلف في الأمور العامة
The first: inference by the temporality of the setting things (al-āfilīn), which are the substances. It is said this is the way of al-Khalīl (peace and blessings be upon him) where he said: 'I do not love the setting ones.' And most of the leading Mu'tazilites held that this premise is inferential, and they argued for it sometimes by [saying] that our actions are temporally originated and need the agent due to their temporality, so likewise the temporally originated substances because the cause of certainty. His saying: (where he said 'I do not love the setting ones' means I do not love them, let alone worship them, because the setting thing is temporally originated due to the temporality of its accidental attribute that indicates its temporality, namely the act of setting, and whatever is temporally originated has a temporal originator other than it, so it cannot be the source of all events, nor can it be the maker of the world, nor can it be loved by the rational person. Then the sayings, I mean the occurrence of absence [ghayba], even if it encompasses substance and accident, except his saying (peace be upon him) 'I do not love the setting ones' is a negation of the lordship of substance in the mentioned manner, so for this reason it is said that his way (peace be upon him) is specific to the temporality of substance. And by this exposition falls what is said: that the necessary consequence of his inference (peace be upon him) is the unfitness of the setting thing to be a lord, not the establishment of the maker of the world, and that temporality necessitates the temporal originator. If you say: It is possible that his inference (peace be upon him) is by possibility, such that its conclusion is that setting is a change from state to state, and change necessitates the possibility of the changing thing which requires a cause. I say: This is only valid if one knows that change necessitates the possibility of the changing thing without observing its entailment of temporality; otherwise, bypassing it to possibility after knowing it, and its being more evident in indicating the desired, is extremely far-fetched. And the apparent is that knowledge of that necessity is not realized without that observation. There remains something here: that the proofs of the entailment of the temporality of the state for the temporality of the substrate are hardly [valid] as will come. So how did al-Khalīl (peace be upon him) infer by the temporality of setting to the temporality of its substrate and that it is not a lord, given that the being of setting as an existent thing is open to discussion? Yes, its being a renewing thing is unambiguous, but the substrate of the renewing thing is not necessarily temporally originated. And it may be said: That from him (peace be upon him) is an intuitive proof (burhān ḥadsī), for he (peace be upon him) due to the strength of his intuition derived from it that its bearer is not suitable for divinity. His saying: (i.e., the self-extended) he interpreted the substantial world by it because the immaterial entities are not established according to us. His saying: (a lofty building) the restriction by 'lofty' is not for exclusion but to increase its similarity to the substantial world that contains the best system, for in this there is loftiness of rank just as in that there is sensible loftiness. His saying: (that our actions are temporally originated and need the agent due to their temporality) it is apparent that what is meant by action is the thing resulting from the verbal noun, not the verbal noun meaning or the resulting thing as such, because the need of the action for the agent then is due to its being an action, even if it is eternal, which is necessary. If you say: What they mentioned is a similitude that yields conjecture, while the issue is about rational demands in which certainty is sought. I say: It has preceded that when the cause is certain in the similitude, it reverts to analogical reasoning and yields certainty, and upon this they built their argument. But it is objected that we do not concede that each of us originates his own actions, and the claim of necessity is absolutely not heard here, and if they mean by agent the absolute efficient cause, the denial is also directed against it, for perhaps our actions occur at our motives coincidentally without an efficient cause. If this is built merely on the fact that every event does not [occur without a cause]. The need is shared, and another [argument] is that the temporal thing has been qualified by existence after non-existence, so it is receptive to both, thus it is possible, and every possible thing needs, in the preponderance of its existence over its non-existence, an efficient cause, as has preceded in the General Matters.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the existence of the Maker, namely the inference from the temporality (huduth) of the setting things (al-āfilīn), i.e., the celestial spheres and substances. It begins by stating that this is the way of Ibrahim (AS) as indicated by his words 'I do not love the setting ones' (Quran 6:76). The author explains that Ibrahim's argument is that the act of setting (afool) is a temporal accident indicating the temporality of its substrate, and whatever is temporal requires a temporal originator distinct from it, so the heavenly bodies cannot be lords or the Creator. The passage then discusses the Mu'tazilite view that the premise 'every temporal has a cause' is inferential, not intuitive. There follows a detailed objection: one might say that Ibrahim's argument actually proves the possibility (imkan) of the changing entity rather than its temporality. The answer explains that this would only be valid if change is known to necessitate possibility without requiring temporality, which is not the case. Then a further objection is raised: the temporality of the state does not necessarily entail the temporality of the substance, and setting may be a renewing state without the substrate being temporal. The reply suggests Ibrahim's proof was intuitive (hadsi). The commentary then defines the substantial world as that which is self-extended (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat), because immaterial entities are not established for the Ash'aris. The example of a lofty building is explained as a similitude for the well-ordered world. Finally, an extended objection-answer sequence discusses whether human actions are truly originated by us, and whether the need for a cause is intuitive or inferential. The passage concludes with the point that the need for a cause is shared and that the temporal thing, being possible, requires an efficient cause.

Key Terms

فاعل
علة
حدوث
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human actions do not require a cause because they arise from our motives freely.
Correction: The passage argues that even if we do not originate our actions, the fact that they are temporal and possible means they require a cause, whether that cause is us or something else.

Checkpoint
What is the final reply to the objection that human actions might occur by coincidence without a cause?
A) Human actions are certainly caused by us
B) The temporal thing is possible and needs a preponderating cause
C) The analogy is only conjectural
D) The objection is invalid because it is not rational

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause is a necessary principle applicable to all temporal things, whether human actions or heavenly bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every event in your life, including your own choices, has a cause from Allah? Or do you sometimes feel that your actions are without purpose or divine oversight? How does the principle that every temporal event needs a preponderating cause challenge that feeling? What would it mean for your daily decisions if you fully acknowledged Allah as the ultimate cause?
?
This passage uses an analogy from human actions to argue for a cause in all temporal things. But what if you are the one who denies that you are the originator of your own actions, as the objector does? In what moments of your life do you act as if your actions are coincidental or without accountability? How does the lesson's reply—that every possible thing needs a preponderating cause—force you to reconsider your responsibility before Allah?
?
The author insists that rational certainty is attainable through this argument. Are you content with merely probable knowledge about Allah's existence, or do you seek certainty? What steps can you take today to move from conjecture (zann) to certainty (yaqin) in your faith, based on the rational proofs presented in this lesson? Commit to one specific action that will deepen your conviction.
