Purpose and Burdens of the Second Maslak
The commentator clarifies the aim of the philosophers' second proof and comments on its remaining burdens.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise aim of the philosophers' second proof and what difficulties it avoids.

Core Idea
The second maslak proves a necessary being, not the Creator.

The commentator begins by clarifying what the second maslak actually aims to prove. Often students think it directly proves God as Creator of the world, but here the commentator distinguishes between proving existence and proving that existence is the Creator. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the burdens that remain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's own clarifications come through.
Notice the explicit phrase 'their purpose in this approach is to prove the necessary being'.
2
Identify the two distinct aims
The passage distinguishes between proving the necessary being and proving He is the creator. Find where this distinction is made and what it implies for the rest of the proof.
3
Understand the 'burdens'
The phrase 'many burdens' is explained. Identify the burdens that remain and which one is not needed. Ask yourself: why is proving possibility unnecessary here?
The commentator says proving possibility is unnecessary because necessity is assumed.
4
Note why circularity is not addressed
The passage mentions only infinite regress, not circularity. Reflect on why the commentator considers circularity even more obvious. What does this tell you about the strength of the argument?
The commentator says circularity's invalidity is 'more apparent'.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Now, in your own words, summarize the purpose of the second maslak and what it does and does not prove. This will reinforce the key distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( المسلك الثاني للحكماء غرضهم من هذا المسلك إثبات الواجب، وأما إثبات كونه صانعاً للعالم كما هو المقصود فبمسلك آخر. قوله : ( لمؤنات كثيرة فى لفظ كثيرة إشعار بأن فيه بعض المؤنات السابقة وهو إثبات الاحتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر، وإبطال الدور والتسلسل لا إثبات نفس الإمكان كما توهم إذلا احتياج إليه قطعاً لثبوت وجوب وجود ذلك الموجود على تقدير انتفاء إمكانه وهذا ظاهر. قوله : (وإن تسلسلت إلخ ) لم يتعرض للدور لأن بطلانه ،أظهر بل قد ندعي فيه الضرورة كما سبق .
His statement: 'The second approach of the philosophers' – their purpose in this approach is to prove the necessary being. As for proving that He is the creator of the world, which is the intended purpose, it is by another approach. His statement: 'many burdens' – in the phrase 'many' there is an indication that it involves some of the previous burdens, namely proving the need of the possible for an effect and refuting circularity and infinite regress, not proving the possibility itself as imagined. For there is no need for that absolutely, due to the established necessity of that existent's existence on the assumption of its possibility, and this is apparent. His statement: 'And if they are arranged in infinite regress etc.' – he did not address circularity because its invalidity is more apparent; rather, necessity may be claimed in it as previously.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second maslak. The first part clarifies that the philosophers' proof in this maslak is only intended to establish the existence of a necessary being (wājib), not to prove that this necessary being is the creator of the world. Proving that He is the creator is a separate task requiring another approach. The second part comments on the phrase 'many burdens' (muʼanāt kathīra) in the original text, noting that it implies that this approach still retains some of the burdens of the first approach, namely proving the need of the possible for an effect and refuting circularity and infinite regress. It does not, however, require proving possibility (imkān) itself, because if the existence in question is already assumed to be necessary on the hypothesis of its possibility, then proving its possibility is unnecessary. The third part comments on the phrase 'and if they are arranged in infinite regress' (wa in tasalsalat), noting that the proof only mentions regress, not circularity, because the invalidity of circularity is even more obvious and may even be known by necessity.

صانعاً للعالم
المؤن
الاحتياج
المؤثر
الدور
التسلسل
الإمكان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' second proof directly establishes that the necessary being is the creator of the world.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that the purpose of this maslak is only to prove the existence of a necessary being; proving He is the creator is a separate task for another approach.

Checkpoint

A) To prove the world is created
B) To prove the existence of a necessary being
C) To prove that the necessary being is the creator
D) To prove the temporality of the world

Key Takeaway
The second maslak only aims to prove the necessary being; proving He is the Creator requires another approach.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The commentator mentions that some burdens remain even in the second proof. What are the 'burdens' in your own understanding of Allah's existence? Are you willing to put in the work to refute doubts, or do you expect proofs to be effortless?
?
If the philosophers' second maslak only gets us to a necessary being, what is the missing piece for you? Have you personally moved from believing in a necessary being to truly knowing Allah as your Creator? If not, what is blocking that step?

The Objection of Totality in Proving the Necessary
Commentator explains objection against the totality argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of a 'totality' (majmū‘) cannot be applied to an infinite series, and how this objection challenges a key argument for the existence of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
An infinite series has no totality, so arguments based on a totality needing a cause are invalid.

In the third maslak, the commentator presents a proof that the cause of all possible beings must be external. But an important objection arises: the very idea of a 'totality' of possible beings assumes finitude. If the series of possible beings is infinite, there is no totality to require a cause. This lesson examines this objection and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes both the original argument and the objection.
2
Understand the positive argument
The passage first argues that the cause of the totality of possible beings cannot be the totality itself, nor a part, nor all parts, so it must be external. Identify this reasoning.
This is the core of the third maslak before the objection.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection states that 'totality' (majmū‘) implies finitude; an infinite series has no totality. Ask yourself: why does this invalidate the argument?
The objection points out a hidden assumption in the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لاحتياجه إلى أجزائه ولأن كل جزء منه ممكن. المرصد الأول : المقصد الأول : في إثبات الصانع ترجح وجوده على عدمه لما عرفت من أن الإمكان محوج ( وهي لا تكون نفس ذلك المجموع إذ العلة متقدمة على المعلول ويمتنع تقدم الشيء على نفسه ، ولا جميع أجزائه لأنه عينه ( ولا تكون ) أيضاً ) جزءه ) أي بعض أجزائه ( إذ علة الكل علة لكل جزء) وذلك لأن كل جزء ممكن محتاج إلى علة فلو لم تكن علة المجموع علة لكل واحد من الأجزاء لكان بعضها معللاً بعلة أخرى فلا تكون تلك الأولى علة للمجموع بل لبعضه فقط وحينئذ فيلزم أن يكون الجزء الذي هو (علة المجموع علة لنفسه ولعلله ) . أيضاً وإذا لم تكن علة المجموع نفسه ولا أمراً داخلاً فيه ( فإذن هو أمر خارج عنه، والخارج عن جميع الممكنات واجب لذاته وهو المطلوب)، ولا بد أن يستند إليه شيء من تلك الممكنات ابتداء فتنتهي به السلسلة ( وأعترض عليه بوجوده الأول المجموع يشعر بالتناهي) لأن ما لا يتناهي ليس له كل ولا مجموع ولا جملة، بل ذلك إنما يتصور في المتناهي وتناهي الممكنات يتوقف على ثبوت الواجب ( فإِثباته ،به أي إثبات الواجب بما يدل على تناهي الممكنات
His statement: 'because of its need for its parts, and because each part is possible.' The first observatory: the first purpose: on proving the Creator. The preponderance of its existence over non-existence, because you know that possibility requires a cause. And it (the cause) is not the totality itself, because the cause is prior to the effect and it is impossible for a thing to be prior to itself. Nor all of its parts, because it is identical with it. Nor a part of it, i.e., some of its parts, because the cause of the whole is a cause for every part. That is because each part is possible needing a cause. If the cause of the totality were not a cause for every one of the parts, then some of them would be caused by another cause, so that first would not be a cause for the totality but only for some of it. Then it would follow that the part which is the cause of the totality would be a cause for itself and for its own causes. Also, if the cause of the totality is neither itself nor something internal to it, then it is something external to it. And what is external to all possible beings is necessary by itself, which is what is sought. And something from those possible beings must depend on it initially, so the chain ends with it. And objection was raised against it concerning the totality indicating finitude, because what is infinite has no whole, totality, or aggregate; rather that is only conceived in the finite. And the finitude of possible beings depends on the existence of the necessary. So proving it (the necessary) by that which indicates the finitude of possible beings...
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the third maslak, explaining the reasoning that the cause of the totality of possible beings cannot be the totality itself, nor any part, nor all parts, but must be external. It then presents an objection: the very notion of a 'totality' (majmū‘) implies finitude; an infinite series has no totality. So the argument that the totality needs a cause is invalid because there is no totality. The commentator notes that proving the finitude of possible beings depends on first establishing the existence of the necessary being, so using that finitude to prove the necessary would be circular. The text is cut off, but it hints at a response using the application (tāṭbīq) proof as an independent argument for the impossibility of infinite regress. The commentary thus highlights a subtle methodological issue: the order of knowledge in proofs of God's existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite series of causes can be treated as a whole (totality) that requires a cause outside itself.
Correction: The objection clarifies that 'whole' or 'totality' only applies to finite sets; an infinite series has no aggregate, so the argument that the totality needs a cause does not apply.

Checkpoint

A) Because an infinite series has no cause
B) Because an infinite series has no whole or aggregate
C) Because a totality is always self-caused
D) Because an infinite series is impossible

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that arguments for God's existence must avoid assuming finitude in the series of causes.

Answering the Totality Implies Finitude Objection
Al-Jurjani presents the objection that a totality implies finitude, and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani answers the objection that using a 'totality' to prove God's existence implies finitude and circular reasoning.

Core Idea
A totality of infinite items is conceivable as a notional whole.

Al-Jurjani anticipates a subtle objection: if we argue from the totality of possible things to a necessary cause, aren't we assuming that totality is finite? After all, an infinite series has no whole. He then provides a careful answer distinguishing between a detailed aggregate and a notional whole. Let's unpack this objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its basis
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the objector's claim: totality implies finitude because the infinite has no whole. Then read the translation to understand the full objection.
The objector is trying to show that the proof of the Necessary Being from the totality of possibles is circular.
2
Understand the circularity charge
The objector argues that proving the Necessary Being by what indicates finitude (the totality) is circular, because finitude of possibles depends on the existence of the Necessary Being.
This is a classic charge of مصادرة (petitio principii).
3
Consider the counter-response using burhan al-tatbiq
Notice how al-Jurjani presents a possible counter: one could claim that finitude is known independently via the 'proof of application' (برهان التطبيق), so no circularity. But al-Jurjani rejects this, saying that would make the current proof dependent on another.
This shows the subtlety of the debate: the proof must be self-sufficient.
4
Learn the answer: notional whole
Al-Jurjani's final answer redefines 'totality' as a notional whole that encompasses all possibles in a single summary glance, which is conceivable even for an infinite set. Thus the objection fails.
The key distinction is between an actual collection of infinite items (impossible) and a notional whole (possible).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأعترض عليه بوجوده الأول المجموع يشعر بالتناهي) لأن ما لا يتناهي ليس له كل ولا مجموع ولا جملة، بل ذلك إنما يتصور في المتناهي وتناهي الممكنات يتوقف على ثبوت الواجب ( فإِثباته ،به أي إثبات الواجب بما يدل على تناهي الممكنات

قوله : ( وتناهي الممكنات يتوقف على ثبوت الواجب لا يقال تناهي الممكنات يتوقف على ثبوت الواجب في نفسه، وثبوت الواجب في نفسه لا يتوقف على التناهي وما يدل عليه وإن كان يستلزمه بل إثباته يتوقف عليه حينئذ فلا مصادرة كما في الاستدلال بالأثر على المؤثر لأنا نقول العلم بتناهي الممكنات متوقف على العلم بثبوت الواجب كما دل عليه قوله، ولا بد أن يستند إلخ، فإثباته بما يدل على التناهي يكون مصادرة . فإن قلت : العلم بتناهيها لا يتوقف على العلم بثبوت الواجب لأن برهان التطبيق يدل عليه من غير ملاحظة ،ثبوته قلت : برهان التطبيق دليل مستقل على بطلان التسلسل وثبوت الواجب دليل آخر عليه مستقل أيضاً، والكلام هاهنا فيما استدل على ذلك البطلان بالدليل الثاني، وإذا استعين في الحكم بالتناهي والعلم به ببرهان التطبيق لم يكن ما ذكره دليلا مستقلا على ذلك البطلان .

مصادرة على المطلوب والجواب أن المراد به أي بالمجموع وما يرادفه في هذا المقام ( هو الممكنات ) بأسرها ( بحيث لا يخرج عنها شيء منها وذلك متصور في غير المتناهي ) إذ يكفيه ملاحظة واحدة إجمالية شاملة لجميع آحاده، إنما الممتنع أن يتصور كل واحد مما لا يتناهي مفصلا ويطلق عليه المجموع بهذا الإعتبار .
And it was objected to it (the proof) that the totality suggests finitude, because what is infinite has no whole, totality, or aggregate; rather that is only conceivable in the finite; and the finitude of possible things depends on the existence of the Necessary Being. So proving Him by it, i.e., proving the Necessary Being by what indicates the finitude of possibles. His saying: 'And the finitude of possibles depends on the existence of the Necessary Being'—one cannot say: the finitude of possibles depends on the existence of the Necessary Being in itself, and the existence of the Necessary Being in itself does not depend on finitude and what indicates it, even if it entails it, but rather His proof depends on it then, so there is no circularity, as in inferring the effect from the cause. Because we say: knowledge of the finitude of possibles depends on knowledge of the existence of the Necessary Being, as indicated by his saying 'and it is necessary that something relies on it' etc., so proving Him by what indicates finitude would be a petitio principii. If you say: knowledge of finitude does not depend on knowledge of the existence of the Necessary Being, because the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) indicates it without considering His existence, I say: the proof of application is an independent proof for the impossibility of infinite regress, and the existence of the Necessary Being is another independent proof; the discussion here is about proving that impossibility by the second proof, and if one uses the proof of application for the judgment of finitude, then what he mentioned is not an independent proof for that impossibility. A petition of the desired conclusion? And the answer: that what is intended by 'totality' and its synonyms in this context is all possible things as a whole, such that nothing from them is excluded, and that is conceivable in the infinite, because one comprehensive summary glance covering all its individuals suffices; only is impossible to conceive each individual of the infinite in detail and to call that a totality in this sense.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the proof from the totality, along with its answer. The objection states: the concept of a totality (majmu') implies finitude, because an infinite series has no whole or aggregate. Therefore, the proof that the cause must be external, which relies on the totality of possibles, presupposes that the series is finite, which itself depends on the existence of the Necessary Being. Hence the argument is circular: it uses the finitude of possibles to prove the Necessary Being, but that finitude is only known through the Necessary Being. The commentary then clarifies this objection and considers a counter-response: one might argue that the finitude of possibles can be known independently through the 'proof of application' (burhan al-tatbiq), which does not require assuming the Necessary Being. The answer is that the proof of application is a separate argument for the impossibility of infinite regress; the present argument is supposed to be another independent proof. If we rely on the proof of application to establish finitude, then the current argument is not an independent proof. The final answer resolves the circularity by redefining 'totality': what is meant here is not an actual collection of an infinite number of items, but rather an all-encompassing notional whole that can be conceived in a single summary glance, even if the individuals are infinite in number. Thus the objection based on finitude is deflected.

Correction: The 'totality' in the proof is not an actual finite collection but a notional whole, so no assumption of finitude is made.

Answering the Objection on Totality
Al-Jurjani answers the objection that the totality either means each individual in a series or a collective whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani resolves the objection regarding the meaning of 'totality' in the proof of the necessary existent.

Core Idea
The whole is identical to its parts; no separate collective entity is needed.

In the proof of the necessary existent, an objection arises: does 'totality' refer to each individual in an infinite series or to a collective whole? The objector argues that both interpretations fail. Al-Jurjani clarifies that by 'totality' we mean the whole as such, which is nothing but the individuals themselves. This distinction preserves the validity of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection's two alternatives
Read the passage carefully to see the two interpretations of 'totality' that the objector presents: each individual of the series or the collective whole. Note how each is argued to be problematic.
The objector thinks both options lead to a dead end.
2
Understand the distinction between collective whole and whole-as-such
Al-Jurjani's answer introduces a third meaning: the whole as such, which is identical to the individuals. Compare this with the collective whole that would require a separate 'social form'.
The whole as such does not depend on a notional addition; it is simply the aggregate considered as a unity.
3
See how the answer applies to the infinite series
The example of the totality of ten shows that the whole is just the ten units. Similarly, for the infinite series, the whole is the series itself. The commentary adds that the impossibility of applying 'whole' to the infinite is relative, not absolute.
The proof remains valid because the whole exists mentally even if we cannot enumerate its parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second: If you mean by 'totality' each individual of the series, then its cause is another possible thing in an infinite sequence, such that each is a cause of what follows and an effect of what precedes, without ending at a limit. And if you mean by it the collective whole (al-kull al-majmu'i), we do not grant that it exists, because there is no social form except by convention, and what is a part by convention is not an external existent. The answer: we mean by 'totality' the whole as such, and there is no need to consider the social form, because the whole here is identical to the individuals, as in the totality of ten. There is no doubt that the whole in this sense exists here. His saying: 'It is only impossible'—this restriction is relative, not absolute, because delimiting the infinite with boundaries and applying the name 'whole' and 'all' to it is impossible, even if its parts are not observed in detail, as is indicated in the discussion of knowledge. His saying: 'Exists here'—he only said 'here' because the whole in this sense does not exist where its parts are non-existent.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage deals with a second objection concerning the interpretation of 'totality'. The objection presents two alternatives: (1) If by 'totality' you mean each individual of the infinite series, then each is caused by another in an infinite regress, which does not lead to an external cause. (2) If you mean the collective whole (al-kull al-majmu'i), then such a whole is not an actual existent because it has no social form beyond the parts; it is merely notional, and what is notional cannot be a cause. The answer distinguishes: what we mean by 'totality' is the whole as such, without requiring a separate social form; the whole is nothing but the individuals themselves, just as the totality of ten is identical to the ten units. This whole is a real concept, not a mere convention, and it does exist as a mental concept even if the components are infinite. The commentary adds that the impossibility of applying 'whole' to the infinite is relative: it is only impossible if we try to delimit it by boundaries or enumerate each part; but a single global conception is possible. Also, the existence of such a whole is affirmed here because its parts (the possibles) are assumed to exist; if the parts were non-existent, the whole would not exist in this sense.

Resolving the Nature of the Cause: Complete Cause vs. Agent
Al-Jurjani refutes the third objection by distinguishing complete cause from agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani resolves the third objection regarding the nature of the cause by distinguishing between the complete cause and the agent.

Core Idea
The complete cause is a set of conditions; the agent is the independent cause of every part.

This lesson unpacks a precise objection: if the cause is either the complete cause or the agent, why cannot the effect be its own cause? Al-Jurjani's answer hinges on careful definitions of priority and agency. We will work through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Note the two interpretations of 'cause' presented.
The objection offers two possibilities: if 'cause' means complete cause, or if it means agent.
2
Understand the complete cause argument
Focus on the first part: 'If you mean by cause the complete cause...'. Al-Jurjani says the complete cause is a collection of matters each prior to the effect, but the whole is not prior; it is identical to the quiddity.
The key is that priority of parts does not imply priority of the whole.
3
Understand the agent argument
Now examine the second part: 'If you mean by it the agent...'. The objection is that a part of the totality could be the cause. The answer is that the agent is independent and must cause every part.
Al-Jurjani denies that a part could be a cause of itself; it could come from no cause or another cause.
4
Analyze the commentary's clarification
Read the last section starting with 'His saying...'. This explains why the discussion is limited to complete cause and agent, and clarifies the phrase 'without a cause'.
The commentator notes that 'without a cause' means without the specific cause under discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث إن أردت بالعلة ( العلة ( التامة فلم لا يجوز أن تكون نفسه قولك العلة متقدمة قلنا لا نسلم ذلك في ( العلة ( التامة فإنها مجموع أمور كل واحد منها مفتقر إليه)، فيكون كل واحد من تلك الأمور متقدماً على المعلول ) ولا يلزم من تقدم كل واحد تقدم الكل كما أن كل واحد من الأجزاء متقدم على الماهية ومجموعها ) ليس متقدماً بل ( هو نفس الماهية وإن أردت بها أي بالعلة (الفاعل) وحده ( فلم لا يجوز أن يكون جزؤه قولك لأنه علة لكل جزء)، فيكون علة لنفسه ولعلله ( قلنا ) ذلك ( ممنوع ولم لا يجوز أن يحصل بعض الأجزاء بلا علة أو بعلة أخرى والجواب أن المراد ) بالعلة هو ( الفاعل المستقل بالفاعلية، وهو في مجموع كل جزء منه ممكن لا بد أن يكون فاعلاً لكل) من الأجزاء على معنى أنه لا

كل واحد من
تكلف
Third: If you mean by 'cause' the complete cause (al-illa al-tamma), then why is it not permissible that it be itself? Your saying: the cause is prior, we do not grant that in the case of the complete cause, for it is a collection of matters each of which is in need of it, so each one of those matters is prior to the effect, and the priority of each does not necessitate the priority of the whole, just as each part is prior to the quiddity, but their collective is not prior, rather it is the quiddity itself. And if you mean by it (i.e., the cause) the agent (al-fa'il) alone, then why is it not permissible that a part of it be the cause? Your saying: because it is a cause of every part, so it would be a cause of itself and its own cause. We say: that is denied. And why is it not permissible that some parts occur without a cause or with another cause? The answer: that what is meant by 'cause' is the independent agent in agency, and it is such that in the totality, every part of it being possible, it must be an agent for each of the parts, meaning that... His saying: 'And if you mean by it the agent'—he did not discuss other partial causes because the discussion is about existing causes, and they are confined to the complete cause and the agent; in the complete cause, agency [is included], and he did not discuss others. His saying: 'And why is it not permissible that some parts occur without a cause?'—if you say: the discussion is about a series whose individuals are possible, so how is it permissible for some parts to occur without a cause, disregarding the specificity of the context? Or we mean by it 'without a presupposed cause' (which is the cause of the whole), and then its implication is the same as the implication of his saying 'or with another cause'—the word 'or' is for variation in expression, and this is common in al-Miftah, even though it is not free from some artificiality.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage addresses a third objection concerning the nature of the cause. The objection presents two possibilities: if by 'cause' you mean the complete cause (al-illa al-tamma), why cannot the totality itself be its own complete cause? The answer is that the complete cause is a collection of conditions each of which is prior to the effect, but the priority of each does not mean the whole is prior; the whole is identical to the quiddity. If by 'cause' you mean only the agent (al-fa'il), then why cannot a part of the totality be the agent? The objection that the part would then be a cause of itself is denied; it is possible that some parts occur without a cause or with a different cause. The answer restricts the meaning of 'cause' to the independent agent, which must be the agent of every part. The commentary clarifies that the discussion is limited to existing causes, which are of two types: the complete cause (which includes agent and conditions) and the agent alone. The phrase 'without a cause' is interpreted as 'without the specific cause under discussion' (i.e., the cause of the whole), and the alternative 'or with another cause' is presented as a variation. The commentator notes that this usage is common even if a bit artificial.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The cause must be either the complete cause or the agent, and if it is the complete cause, it must be prior to the effect, so the effect cannot be identical.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that the complete cause is a collection of prior conditions; priority of parts does not imply priority of the whole. The whole is identical to the quiddity, not prior.

Checkpoint

B) The complete cause is a collection of matters, and its priority does not imply the whole is prior

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani resolves the third objection by distinguishing the complete cause (a collection of conditions) from the agent (the independent cause of every part).

The First Objection: Conceiving the Totality of Contingent Beings
Defines first objection and answer on totality of possible things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument for the Creator avoids circularity by distinguishing between a general comprehensive notion of the infinite totality and a detailed conception of each individual.

Core Idea
The infinite can be grasped as a whole, even if not in detail.

In proving the Creator, one begins with the totality of contingent beings. An objector may claim this argument is circular, but al-Ījī's answer clarifies: when we speak of the totality, we mean a single comprehensive notion of all contingent things, not a detailed enumeration. This subtle distinction saves the argument from the false accusation of circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to get the overall flow of the objection and answer.
Notice the key terms like مجموع (totality) and إجمالية (comprehensive overview).
2
Identify the objection and its answer
The objector says the argument is circular (مصادرة على المطلوب). Find how the answer refutes this by defining 'totality' as all contingent beings conceived in a single overview.
The answer hinges on the distinction between a general concept and detailed enumeration.
3
Understand the relative impossibility
The marginal gloss (قوله) explains that the impossibility of grasping the infinite is relative, not absolute. Reflect on why this matters for the proof.
This guards against the misunderstanding that the infinite cannot be conceived at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في إثبات الصانع) مصادرة على المطلوب والجواب أن المراد به أي بالمجموع وما يرادفه في هذا المقام هو الممكنات بأسرها بحيث لا يخرج عنها شيء منها وذلك متصور في غير المتناهي إذ يكفيه ملاحظة واحدة إجمالية شاملة لجميع آحاده، إنما الممتنع أن يتصور كل واحد مما لا يتناهي مفصلا ويطلق عليه المجموع بهذا الإعتبار، قوله : (إنما الممتنع هذا الحصر إضافي لا حقيقي فإن ضبط غير المتناهي بالحدود وإطلاق إسم الكل والجميع عليه ممتنع وإن لم يلاحظ أجزاؤه مفصلاً كما يشار إليه في بحث العلم.
The First Observatory – The First Objective: On Proving the Maker. (It is) begging the question, and the answer is that what is meant by it—that is, by the totality and what is synonymous with it in this context—is the contingent things in their entirety, in such a way that nothing of them falls outside it, and that is conceivable in the infinite, because a single comprehensive overview encompassing all its individuals suffices. It is only impossible to conceive each one of the infinite in detail and to call it the totality in this sense. His statement: 'It is only impossible' – this restriction is relative, not real, for grasping the infinite by limits and applying the name 'whole' and 'all' to it is impossible even if its parts are not observed in detail, as is indicated in the discussion of knowledge.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage defines the first objection and answer concerning the proof of the Creator. The objector claims that the argument is circular (musadara). The answer clarifies that by 'totality' (majmu') is meant all contingent beings (mumkinat) without exception. Although an infinite set cannot be conceived in detail, it can be conceived in a single comprehensive overview. The marginal gloss (qawlahu) explains that the impossibility is relative (idafi) not absolute: restricting the infinite with limits and calling it a whole is impossible, but a general conception is still possible. This establishes the basis for the proof.

Key Terms
ممكنات
مجموع
لا يتناهى
إجمالية
إضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite totality cannot be conceived at all.
Correction: This passage shows that the infinite totality can be conceived in a single comprehensive overview; only detailed conception of each individual is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the answer say about conceiving the infinite totality of contingent things?
A) It is impossible both in overview and in detail.
B) It is possible in a single comprehensive overview but not in detail.
C) It is possible only if the parts are finite.
D) It is possible only through divine revelation.

Key Takeaway
The totality of infinite contingent beings is conceivable in a single comprehensive notion, even if not in detail, saving the proof from circularity.

In your relationship with Allah, do you demand detailed proofs at every turn, or have you settled for an overview that still leaves you restless? The Infinite Causal Series Objection and Answer
Second objection and answer about infinite causal chain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection regarding the infinite causal series is answered by distinguishing two meanings of 'totality'.

Core Idea
The whole of a series exists as identical to its parts, not as a separate entity.

In this passage, the author addresses a critical objection to the proof of the Creator based on the possibility of an infinite causal chain. The objection argues that the totality of such a chain cannot exist either as each individual (leading to regress) or as a collective whole (since that whole would be merely conceptual). The answer clarifies that the intended whole is simply the aggregate of all individuals, which exists as long as the individuals exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's two interpretations
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Identify the two ways the opponent interprets 'totality' (majmu).
The first interpretation is 'each individual of the series'; the second is 'the collective whole'.
2
Identify the problem with each interpretation
For the first, note the infinite regress. For the second, note the claim that the collective whole does not exist externally.
The second interpretation relies on the idea that a 'social configuration' is merely conceptual.
3
Examine the answer's distinction
The answer introduces a third meaning: the whole insofar as it is a whole, which is identical to the individuals. See how this avoids both problems.
This meaning does not require a separate configuration.
4
Understand the analogy of the number ten
The author uses the example of the totality of ten (majmu al-ashara) to illustrate that the whole is exactly the individuals.
Just as ten is nothing other than ten ones, the whole series is nothing other than its members.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني إن أردت بالمجموع كل واحد من آحاد السلسلة فعلته ممكن آخر متسلسلاً إلى غير النهاية بأن يكون كل واحد منها علة لما بعده ومعلولا لما قبله من غير أن ينتهي إلى حد يقف عنده، وإن أردت به الكل المجموعي فلا نسلم أنه موجود إذ ليس ثمة هيئة اجتماعية إلا بحسب الإعتبار وما جزؤه إعتباري لا يكون موجوداً خارجياً والجواب أنا نريد بالمجموع الكل من حيث هو كل ولا حاجة إلى اعتبار الهيئة الاجتماعية إذ الكل هاهنا عين الآحاد كما في مجموع العشرة ولا شك أن الكل بهذا المعنى موجود هاهنا، قوله : (موجود) هاهنا إنما قال هاهنا لأن الكل بهذا المعنى غير موجود فيما جزوه معدوم.
Second: If you mean by the totality each one of the individuals of the series, then its cause would be another possible [being] linked in an infinite chain – in that each one is a cause for what follows and an effect of what precedes without ending at a limit where it stops. And if you mean by it the collective whole, then we do not admit that it exists, for there is no social configuration except by consideration, and what has a part that is conceptual is not an external existent. The answer: We mean by the totality the whole insofar as it is a whole, and there is no need to consider the social configuration, for the whole here is identical with the individuals, as in the totality of ten. And there is no doubt that the whole in this sense exists here. His statement: (existent) here – he only said 'here' because the whole in this sense does not exist when its part is non-existent.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and answer regarding the proof of the Creator. Two interpretations of 'totality' (majmu') are considered. First, if each individual of an infinite causal series is meant, an infinite regress follows. Second, if a collective whole (kull majmu'i) is meant, it is not an external existent because a social configuration (hay'a ijtimaiyya) is only conceptual. The answer clarifies that the intended whole is the whole insofar as it is a whole (al-kull min haythu huwa kull), which is identical to the individuals themselves, like the number ten (majmu' al-ashara). The marginal note explains the specific context: the whole exists 'here' because its parts exist; if the parts were non-existent (as in something whose part is non-existent), the whole would not exist.

Key Terms
سلسلة
كل مجموعي
هيئة اجتماعية
إعتبار
مجموع العشرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The collective whole of an infinite series does not exist at all because it would require a separate configuration.
Correction: The answer shows that the whole exists as identical to its individuals; no separate configuration is needed. The example of the number ten clarifies that the whole is exactly the individuals.

Checkpoint
How does the answer explain the existence of the whole in the infinite series?
A) It exists as a separate entity beyond the individuals.
B) It exists only in the mind, not externally.
C) It is identical to the individuals themselves.
D) It does not exist at all.

Key Takeaway
The whole of an infinite causal series exists not as a separate entity but as identical to its individuals, just as the number ten is no more than its units.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The lesson teaches that the whole exists as identical to its individuals. Consider the 'whole' of your faith—is it merely a collection of disconnected rituals and beliefs, or do you see each individual act as integral to the whole? How does this understanding change your responsibility for even the smallest action?
?
The objection claimed that a collective whole requires a separate 'social configuration' to exist. In your own life, do you sometimes think that your identity as a Muslim requires a certain 'form' or external structure beyond the actual deeds? How does the answer—that the whole is identical to its parts—challenge you to focus on the substance of each individual act?

The analogy of the number ten shows that the whole is exactly its units. If your life were a 'totality', what would the 'units' be? Is there one unit today that you are neglecting, and how will you restore it to complete the whole?

The Complete Cause and Its Priority
Objection and answer about complete cause priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the complete cause could be itself and the response that its parts are individually prior but the whole is not prior.

Core Idea
Priority of parts does not equal priority of the whole.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the third objection concerning the complete cause. The objector wonders why the complete cause cannot be itself, given that a cause must be prior. The response clarifies that the complete cause is a composite whose parts are individually prior to the effect, but the totality is not prior—just as the essence is not prior to its parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the objector's claim and the author's response.
Notice the objector uses 'علة تامة' (complete cause).
2
Analyze the analogy
The author uses an analogy of parts and essence. Reflect on how this supports the claim that the totality is not prior.
The analogy is: each part is prior to the essence, but the whole is not prior.
3
Recognize the key distinction
Understand the difference between priority of each part and priority of the whole. This is crucial for the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث إن أردت بالعلة العلة التامة فلم لا يجوز أن تكون نفسه قولك العلة متقدمة قلنا لا نسلم ذلك في العلة التامة فإنها مجموع أمور كل واحد منها مفتقر إليه، فيكون كل واحد من تلك الأمور متقدماً على المعلول ولا يلزم من تقدم كل واحد تقدم الكل كما أن كل واحد من الأجزاء متقدم على الماهية ومجموعها ليس متقدماً بل هو نفس الماهية وإن أردت بها أي بالعلة الفاعل وحده فلم لا يجوز أن يكون جزؤه قولك لأنه علة لكل جزء، فيكون علة لنفسه ولعلله قلنا ذلك ممنوع ولم لا يجوز أن يحصل بعض الأجزاء بلا علة أو بعلة أخرى والجواب أن المراد بالعلة هو الفاعل المستقل بالفاعلية، وهو في مجموع كل جزء منه ممكن لا بد أن يكون فاعلاً لكل من الأجزاء على معنى أنه لا يستند شيء منها بالمفعولية إلا إليه أو إلى ما صدر عنه وإلا وقع بعض أجزائه بفاعل آخر لم يصدر عنه فإذا قطع النظر عنه أي عن الآخر لم تحصل الماهية المعلولة التي هي المجموع فلم يكن ذلك الفاعل فاعلاً مستقلاً بالمعنى المذكور وهو خلاف المقدر، قوله: (ولم لا يجوز أن يحصل بعض الأجزاء بلا علة) فإن قلت الكلام في السلسلة التي آحادها ممكن فكيف يجوز حصول بعض الأجزاء بلا علة مع قطع النظر عن خصوصية المقام أو نقول معناه بلا علة مفروضة وهي علة الكل وحينئذ يكون مؤداه مؤدى قوله أو بعلة أخرى يكون لفظ أو للتحيير في التعبير ومثله شائع في المفتاح وإن كان لا يخلو عن نوع تكلف، قوله : ( على معنى أنه لا يستند شيء منها بالمفعولية إلا إليه أو إلى ما صدر عنه) قد أشرنا في مباحث العلة والمعلول إلى الاعتراض على هذا الكلام بأن المعلوم لنا هو أن كل ممكن مركب ممكنات لا بد له من فاعل مستقل على معنى أن لا يكون المركب محتاجاً إلى فاعل خارج عنه وأما الاستقلال بالمعنى الذي أشار إليه الشارح فهو إنما يجب في مركب من آحاد متناهية يستند بعضها إلى بعض، وأما في الآحاد الغير المتناهية المستندة بعضها إلى بعض على ما هو المفروض في السلسلة التي كلا منها فيها، فلزومه ممنوع فالواجب حينئذ أن يكون موجد الكل موجود الكل جزء منه إما بنفسه أو بجزئه ولا محذور حينئذ في كون ما قبل المعلول الأخير علة ما قبلها بمرتبة وهلم جرا فليتأمل.
Third: If you mean by the cause the complete cause, then why may it not be itself? Your statement 'the cause is prior' – we do not admit that in the complete cause, for it is a composite of matters each of which is needed by it; thus each of those matters is prior to the effect, but the priority of each does not entail the priority of the whole, just as each part is prior to the essence but their totality is not prior but is the essence itself. And if you mean by it the agent alone, then why may not its part be [uncaused]? Your statement 'because it is a cause for every part' – then it would be a cause for itself and for its cause. We say: that is denied. And why may some parts occur without a cause or with another cause? The answer: What is meant by the cause is the independent agent in agency, and in a totality every part of which is possible, it must be an agent for each of the parts, in the sense that nothing of them is dependent in effecthood except on it or on what proceeds from it. Otherwise some of its parts would be caused by another agent not proceeding from it. Then if consideration is cut off from that other, the caused essence which is the totality would not be realized, so that agent would not be independent in the mentioned sense, contrary to the supposition. His statement: (And why may some parts occur without a cause) – then you say: the discussion concerns the series whose individuals are possible, so how can some parts occur without a cause, disregarding the specificity of the context? Or we say: its meaning is without a presumed cause which is the cause of the whole, and then its import is the same as his statement 'or with another cause'. The word 'or' is for confusion in expression, and its like is common in al-Miftah, even if it is not free from some affectation. His statement: (on the meaning that nothing of them is dependent in effecthood except on it or on what proceeds from it) – we have pointed out in the discussion of cause and effect the objection to this statement: what is known to us is that every possible composite of possibles necessarily has an independent agent in the sense that the composite does not need an agent external to it. As for independence in the sense the commentator indicates, it is only necessary in a composite of finite individuals that depend on one another. As for infinite individuals that depend on one another, as supposed in the series, its necessity is denied. So then the creator of the whole must be the existence of the whole [as a] part of it, either by itself or by its part, and there is no difficulty then in that what precedes the last effect is the cause of what precedes it by one rank, and so on. So let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and answer concerning the nature of the cause (illa). Two interpretations are considered: the complete cause (illa tamma) and the agent alone (fa'il). For the complete cause, the objection that a cause must be prior is answered by noting that the complete cause is composite and its components are prior individually, but the totality is not prior (like parts and essence). For the agent alone, the objection that it might cause only some parts is answered by defining the agent as independent (mustaqill) and requiring it to cause every part of the composite, otherwise the composite would depend on another agent. The marginal glosses further examine the possibility of parts occurring without a cause and challenge the necessity of independence for infinite series, suggesting an alternative view.

Key Terms
علة تامة
ماهية
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does the complete cause not need to be prior to itself?
A) Because the complete cause is not composite.
B) Because only the parts are prior, not the whole.
C) Because the complete cause is identical to the effect.
D) Because priority is not required for causes.

Key Takeaway
The complete cause as a whole is not prior to its effect; only its components are prior individually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider God as the ultimate cause of everything, do you unknowingly treat His attributes as separate 'parts' that come before Him? How does this lesson's distinction between parts and whole challenge you to see Him as one complete cause without division?
?
If the complete cause is not prior to its effect but identical to it in essence, what does that mean for your personal reliance on God? Does your dependence on Him feel like relying on a whole or on many disconnected factors?
?
Reflect on a time when you thought God’s action was delayed because you focused on individual conditions. How can this teaching free you from impatience, knowing that God as the complete cause is not bound by the priority of parts?

Independent Agent and Causal Parts
Objection and answer about agent alone and causal independence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection regarding the agent alone and the requirement that an independent agent must cause every part of the composite either directly or indirectly.

Core Idea
An independent agent must be the source of every part's effecthood.

This lesson covers the second part of the third objection, which targets the agent alone. The objector asks why a part of the agent could be uncaused, and the answer defines the independent agent as one from which every part of the composite depends for its effecthood—either directly or through something that proceeds from it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the two objections within this section and the overarching answer.
The objector first says 'why may its part be uncaused?' and then 'why may some parts occur without a cause or with another cause?'
2
Understand the definition of independent agent
The answer defines the independent agent as one that causes every part either directly or through what proceeds from it. Reflect on why this definition is necessary.
This definition ensures the agent is truly independent.
3
Examine the gloss on 'without a cause'
The marginal gloss clarifies the phrase 'without a cause' in the context of the series. Note its interpretation.
It means without a presumed cause that is the cause of the whole.

Third: If you mean by the cause the complete cause, then why may it not be itself? Your statement 'the cause is prior' – we do not admit that in the complete cause, for it is a composite of matters each of which is needed by it; thus each of those matters is prior to the effect, but the priority of each does not entail the priority of the whole, just as each part is prior to the essence but their totality is not prior but is the essence itself. Then if consideration is cut off from that other, the caused essence which is the totality would not be realized, so that agent would not be independent in the mentioned sense, contrary to the supposition. As for independence in the sense the commentator indicates, it is only necessary in a composite of finite individuals that depend on one another. As for infinite individuals that depend on one another, as supposed in the series, its necessity is denied. So then the creator of the whole must be the existence of the whole [as a] part of it, either by itself or by its part, and there is no difficulty then in that what precedes the last effect is the cause of what precedes it by one rank, and so on. So let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and answer concerning the nature of the cause (illa). Two interpretations are considered: the complete cause (illa tamma) and the agent alone (fa'il). For the complete cause, the objection that a cause must be prior is answered by noting that the complete cause is composite and its components are prior individually, but the totality is not prior (like parts and essence). For the agent alone, the objection that it might cause only some parts is answered by defining the agent as independent (mustaqill) and requiring it to cause every part of the composite, otherwise the composite would depend on another agent. The marginal glosses further examine the possibility of parts occurring without a cause and challenge the necessity of independence for infinite series, suggesting an alternative view.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what makes an agent 'independent' (mustaqill)?
A) Causing the whole without needing to cause each part.
B) Every part of the composite depends for its effecthood on it or on what proceeds from it.
C) Being a part of the composite itself.
D) Being the only cause in the universe.

Key Takeaway
An independent agent must be the source of effecthood for every part of the composite, either directly or through its effects.

?
When you consider the 'parts' of your life—your health, wealth, relationships—do you acknowledge that none of them could have come from any source other than Allah or what He has caused? Objection to Independence for Infinite Series
Marginal gloss objecting to independence requirement for infinite series

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique that the independence requirement for an agent does not necessarily apply to infinite causal series.

Core Idea
Infinite series allow parts to depend on each other without an external independent agent.

This lesson examines a marginal gloss that challenges the necessity of the independence condition for infinite causal chains. The gloss argues that the independence requirement is only necessary for finite composites, not for infinite series where each part depends on a prior part.

Guided Path
1
Read the marginal gloss
Read the Arabic and translation of the gloss. It begins by quoting the earlier statement about effecthood depending on the agent or its effect.
The gloss is responding to the definition of independent agent.
2
Understand the distinction between finite and infinite
The gloss distinguishes between a finite composite of individuals and an infinite series. Reflect on why this matters for independence.
In an infinite series, each part can be caused by a prior part, not necessarily by an independent external agent.
3
Grasp the alternative view
The gloss suggests that for an infinite series, the creator of the whole might be the existence of the whole itself, and parts cause each other in a chain. Consider this alternative.
This challenges the earlier requirement of a single independent agent.

Third: If you mean by the cause the complete cause, then why may it not be itself? Your statement 'the cause is prior' – we do not admit that in the complete cause, for it is a composite of matters each of which is needed by it; thus each of those matters is prior to the effect, but the priority of each does not entail the priority of the whole, just as each part is prior to the essence but their totality is not prior but is the essence itself. And if you mean by it the agent alone, then why may not its part be [uncaused]? Your statement 'because it is a cause for every part' – then it would be a cause for itself and for its cause. We say: that is denied. And why may some parts occur without a cause or with another cause? The answer: What is meant by the cause is the independent agent in agency, and in a totality every part of which is possible, it must be an agent for each of the parts, in the sense that nothing of them is dependent in effecthood except on it or on what proceeds from it. Otherwise some of its parts would be caused by another agent not proceeding from it. Then if consideration is cut off from that other, the caused essence which is the totality would not be realized, so that agent would not be independent in the mentioned sense, contrary to the supposition. His statement: (And why may some parts occur without a cause) – then you say: the discussion concerns the series whose individuals are possible, so how can some parts occur without a cause, disregarding the specificity of the context? Or we say: its meaning is without a presumed cause which is the cause of the whole, and then its import is the same as his statement 'or with another cause'. The word 'or' is for confusion in expression, and its like is common in al-Miftah, even if it is not free from some affectation. His statement: (on the meaning that nothing of them is dependent in effecthood except on it or on what proceeds from it) – we have pointed out in the discussion of cause and effect the objection to this statement: what is known to us is that every possible composite of possibles necessarily has an independent agent in the sense that the composite does not need an agent external to it. As for independence in the sense the commentator indicates, it is only necessary in a composite of finite individuals that depend on one another. As for infinite individuals that depend on one another, as supposed in the series, its necessity is denied. So then the creator of the whole must be the existence of the whole [as a] part of it, either by itself or by its part, and there is no difficulty then in that what precedes the last effect is the cause of what precedes it by one rank, and so on. So let it be pondered.

Objection: Composite of Necessary and Contingent
Objection about composite of necessary and contingent and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the proof from composition involving a mix of necessary and contingent beings, and the answer that qualifies the whole as having every part possible.

Core Idea
The proof only applies when every part is possible; mixed composites do not refute it.

In this passage, the author anticipates a possible objection to the earlier proof: what if a whole is composed of both a necessary being and contingent beings? The objector argues that such a whole is still possible, yet its agent does not cause each part. The author responds by reiterating that the proof only applies to wholes where every part is possible. This refinement shows the precision of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is raised. Notice it has two sub-arguments: one about the composite of necessary and contingent, and another about temporal order in composites like a bed.
The objector uses the example of a bed to illustrate temporal priority and posteriority issues.
2
Identify the two parts of the objection
Distinguish the first objection (composite of necessary and contingent) from the second (temporal order). The second objection argues that if the agent is independent, it leads to either the effect preceding its cause or lagging behind.
3
Read the answer and the gloss
Read the answer starting with 'I say: The answer to the first...'. Note that the answer only addresses the first objection directly; the second objection is discussed in the following gloss.
The gloss further analyzes why the composite of necessary and contingent is not a valid refutation.
4
Understand the qualification
The key move is that the whole was qualified as having every part possible. Therefore, a composite that includes a necessary being (which is not possible) falls outside the scope of the proof.
This qualification prevents the objection from undermining the original argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل هذا الذي ذكرتموه منقوض بالمركب من الواجب والممكن فإن مجموعهما من حيث هو مجموع لا شك أنه ممكن لاحتياجه إلى جزءه الذي هو غيره مع أن فاعله ليس فاعلاً لكل واحد من أجزائه وأيضاً لو كان فاعل الكل بالاستقلال فاعلاً لكل جزء منه كذلك للزم في مركب في أجزائه ترتب زماني كالسرير مثلاً إما تقدم المعلول على علته أو تخلف المعلول عن علته المستقلة إذ عند وجود الجزء المتقدم كالخشب إن وجدت العلة المستقلة للكل لزم الأمر الثاني، وإن لم توجد لزم الأمر الأول وكلاهما محال قلت الجواب عن الأول وهو النقض أنا قيدناه أي الكل بما كل جزء منه ممكن كما مر آنفاً فاندفع النقض، فإن قيل نحن نمنع كون فاعل الكل فاعلاً من، قوله : (فإن قيل هذا منقوض إلخ) أي قولهم علة الكل يجب أن يكون علة لكل جزء منه كما يدل عليه قوله مع أن فاعله ليس فاعلاً لكل فاعل من أجزائه، وهذا السؤال بعد ما صرح في الجواب السابق بقوله وهو في مجموع كل جزء منه ممكن غير موجد، وكأن قول الشارح في أثناء جوابه كما مر آنفاً يومي إليه وقد يقال : النقض لقولهم علة الجملة لا يكون جزؤها . قيل لا جهة للنقضين لأن الواجب ليس بعلة مستقلة للكل بل لما سواه من الممكنات ولو سلم عليته للكل أن ماهية الواجب توجب وجود نفسه وغيره فلا نقض أيضاً إذ حينئذ يكون علة للكل فلم يحتج إلى تقييد الكل بإمكان كل جزء منه لدفعه، ولا يخفى عليك أن إمكان الكل يستلزم احتياجه إلى علة مستقلة قطعاً، فلا وجه لمنعه بعد الاعتراف باللزوم على ما أطبقوا عليه نعم لو استلزم احتياج كل جزء منه إليها لكان له وجه لكن ذلك الاستلزام فيما كل جزء منه ممكن، وأما في المركب من الواجب والممكن فلا ولهذا لم يلزم صدور أثرين منه عندهم بيناه في بحث العلة والمعلول.
For it is said: This which you have mentioned is refuted by the composite of the necessary and the contingent, for their composite insofar as it is a composite is undoubtedly possible due to its need for its part which is other than it, even though its agent is not an agent for each of its parts. And also, if the agent of the whole independently were an agent for each part in the same way, it would require in a composite whose parts have a temporal order – like a bed – either the effect preceding its cause or the effect lagging behind its independent cause. For when the prior part (like wood) exists, if the independent cause of the whole exists, the second consequence follows; if it does not exist, the first follows, and both are impossible. I say: The answer to the first (the refutation) is: We qualified the whole – i.e., the totality – as that every part of it is possible, as passed just now, so the refutation is repelled. Then it is said: We deny that the agent of the whole is an agent of... His statement: (For it is said this is refuted etc.) That is, their statement that the cause of the whole must be a cause for every part of it, as his statement indicates, although its agent is not an agent for each of its agents. This question after he stated in the previous answer his statement 'and it is in a totality every part of which is possible' – and it is as if the commentator's statement in the course of his answer, as just passed, points to it. And it may be said: The refutation of their statement that the cause of the whole is not its part. It is said: There is no ground for the two refutations, because the Necessary is not an independent cause of the whole, but only of other contingent things. Even if one concedes its causality for the whole, then the essence of the Necessary necessitates its own existence and other things, so there is no refutation; for then it would be a cause of the whole, and there would be no need to restrict the whole to the possibility of every part of it to repel it. And it is not hidden from you that the possibility of the whole necessarily entails its need for an independent cause; so there is no ground for denying it after acknowledging the entailment, according to what they have agreed upon. Indeed, if it entailed the need of every part of it for it, there would be a ground, but that entailment applies only when every part of it is possible. As for the composite of the Necessary and the contingent, it does not, and for this reason the occurrence of two effects from Him is not necessary according to them, as we explained in the discussion of cause and effect.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents two new objections to the proof. First, a composite of the Necessary Being (Wajib) and a contingent being (mumkin) is itself possible, yet its agent does not cause every part. Second, in composites with temporal order (like a bed), independence of the agent leads to paradoxes of priority and posteriority. The answer to the first objection is a reassertion of the qualification that the whole in question has every part possible, so the composite of necessary and contingent is not a counterexample. The second objection is not directly answered in the main text but is discussed in the marginal gloss. The gloss further analyzes the objection, arguing that the Necessary is not an independent cause of the whole but only of other things, and that the entailment of need for an independent cause only holds when every part is possible.

Correction: The proof only applies to wholes where every part is possible; a composite including the Necessary does not qualify, so the objection fails.

The Independent Cause Must Cause Every Part
Proof of independent cause must cause each part, with objections and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that an independent cause of a composite whole must cause each part individually, and how it answers objections about composites involving necessary parts.

Core Idea
An independent cause of a whole must cause every part individually.

This passage presents a key proof in kalam about causality and independent agency. The author argues that if something is the independent cause of a composite whole, it must cause each part; otherwise the whole would not be fully caused. Two objections are raised—concerning composites of necessary and possible beings, and temporal order—and the author answers by restricting the argument to composites where every part is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the first part of the passage where the author states the proof: the independent cause of the whole must cause each part. Notice the reasoning about what would happen if a part were caused by another agent.
The proof is brief but logically tight.
2
Examine the first objection
The objection about a composite of necessary and possible beings: the composite is possible but the necessary part needs no cause. Ask yourself why this seems to challenge the proof.
3
Examine the second objection
The temporal order objection using a bed as example. Understand the two impossibilities that arise if the cause of the whole were the cause of each part with parts in time.
4
Understand the answer
The author answers by restricting the claim to composites where every part is possible. See how this resolves both objections—the necessary part is excluded, and temporal parts are all possible so the cause must be present throughout.
5
Reflect on significance
Consider why this argument matters for proving a single Creator: the universe is a composite of possible parts, so its cause must be the cause of every part, implying one independent agent.
This argument is a foundation for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول : المقصد الأول : في إثبات الصانع يستند شيء منها بالمفعولية إلا إليه أو إلى ما صدر عنه ( وإلا وقع بعض أجزائه بفاعل آخر) لم يصدر عنه ( فإذا قطع النظر عنه ( أي عن الآخر (لم تحصل الماهية ) المعلولة التي هي المجموع فلم يكن ذلك الفاعل ( فاعلاً مستقلاً) بالمعنى المذكور وهو خلاف المقدر، فإن قيل هذا الذي ذكرتموه ( منقوض بالمركب من الواجب والممكن) فإن مجموعهما من حيث هو مجموع لا شك أنه ممكن لاحتياجه إلى جزءه الذي هو غيره مع أن فاعله ليس فاعلا لكل واحد من أجزائه ( وأيضاً لو كان فاعل (الكل) بالاستقلال ( فاعلاً لكل جزء) منه كذلك (للزم في مركب في أجزائه ترتب زماني ( كالسرير مثلاً (إما تقدم المعلول على علته أو تخلف المعلول عن علته المستقلة إذ عند وجود الجزء المتقدم كالخشب إن وجدت العلة المستقلة للكل لزم الأمر الثاني، وإن لم توجد لزم الأمر الأول وكلاهما محال ( قلت الجواب عن الأول ) وهو النقض ( أنا قيدناه ) أي الكل ( بما كل جزء منه ممكن كما مر آنفاً ) ( فاندفع النقض
The first observatory: the first objective: on proving the Maker. Nothing among them is attributed in effect except to Him or to what proceeds from Him (otherwise some of its parts would occur by another agent) not proceeding from Him (so if one disregards it (i.e., the other), then the essence that is the effect, which is the composite, would not occur, and that agent would not be an independent agent in the aforementioned sense, contrary to what is supposed). For it is said: This that you have mentioned is refuted by the composite of the necessary and the possible, for their composite as such is undoubtedly possible because of its need for a part that is other than it, while its agent is not an agent for each one of its parts. (Also, if the agent of the whole independently were the agent of each part similarly, then in a composite whose parts have a temporal order, like a bed for example, there would follow either the precedence of the effect over its cause or the delay of the effect from its independent cause, for when the prior part like wood is present, if the independent cause of the whole is present, the second [impossibility] follows, and if it is not present, the first [impossibility] follows, and both are impossible. I said: The answer to the first (i.e., the refutation) is that we restricted it (i.e., the whole) to that in which every part is possible, as has just passed, so the refutation is repelled.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a proof followed by an objection and its answer. The author begins by stating the proof that the independent cause (الفاعل المستقل) of a composite whole must be the cause of every part individually, because otherwise the whole would not be produced by that cause alone. Then an objection is raised: the proof is refuted by the case of a composite of the necessary (واجب) and the possible (ممكن), since the composite is possible (needs a cause) but the agent of that composite is not the agent of each part (e.g., the necessary part does not need a cause). A further objection is added concerning temporal order (like a bed): if the cause of the whole were the cause of each part, then with temporally ordered parts, either the effect would precede its cause or the effect would be delayed from its cause, both absurd. The answer restricts the claim to composites where every part is possible; the composite of necessary and possible is excluded, so the objection does not apply. The temporal objection is implicitly handled by the restriction since the bed's parts are all possible.

Key Terms
فاعل مستقل
علة الكل
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection about the composite of necessary and possible shows that an independent cause does not need to cause every part.
Correction: The argument only applies when every part is possible; the necessary part is not caused, so the objection does not refute the proof for composites of purely possible parts.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what restriction was placed on the composite to answer the first objection?
A) That the whole must be possible.
B) That every part of the whole must be possible.
C) That the whole must be necessary.
D) That the parts must be temporally ordered.

Key Takeaway
The independent cause of a composite whole must cause each part individually, provided every part is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every part of your existence is caused directly by the Independent Cause, then your very heartbeat and every breath is not truly your own. Are you living as if you own your life, or as if it is held in His grasp every moment? What would change today if you truly internalized that you are not the independent cause of any part of your own being?
?
The composite of the necessary and possible was an exception that saved the argument. In your life, do you sometimes mix necessary Islamic obligations with optional desires, treating them as one composite that you can control? How might disentangling what is wajib from what is mumkin transform your accountability before Allah?
?
The temporal objection was about the timing of causes. Consider a sin you committed in the past: do you think you were the independent cause of that action? How does the truth that every part of that action was caused by Allah affect your understanding of repentance and reliance on Him to avoid future sins?
