The Second Proof and the Philosophers' Objection
Presents the second proof and the philosophers' objection based on perpetual motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for divine causality based on the daily event and the philosophers' objection that invokes perpetual motion with two aspects.

Core Idea
The daily event requires an uncaused cause; perpetual motion with continuity and renewal is a philosopher's attempted objection.

This lesson presents the second of two proofs concerning the daily originated event. The proof argues that the daily event cannot depend on an infinite chain of prior events, while the philosophers respond by appealing to perpetual motion as an intermediary that combines continuity (allowing dependence on the Eternal) and renewal (allowing origination). We will see the proof's structure and the objection that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's claim
The proof states the daily event does not depend on an infinite series of prior events; if it did, it could be explained by perpetual motion.
Note the phrase 'لا يستند إلى حادث مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية'.
2
Identify the philosophers' position
The philosophers allow infinite regress in ordered but non-simultaneous things, citing perpetual motion as having two aspects: continuity and renewal.
They claim that by its continuity it can depend on the Eternal, and by its renewal it mediates events.
3
Understand the objection
The objector says the proof only works for things other than God's essence and attributes, and that infinite successions require a universal shared matter.
The objection ends with 'ففيه تأمل' (but there is reflection upon it).
4
Reflect on the open-ended nature
The objection is not fully answered here; the commentator will later show the proof's insufficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني من الطريقين أن نبين في الحادث اليومي أنه لا يستند الى حادث مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية محفوظاً استناده كذلك بحركة دائمة، إذ على تقدير هذا الاستناد جاز أن يكون المبدأ الأول موجباً مفيضاً لوجود الحادث اليومي على مادة قديمة بواسطة استعدادات متعاقبة مستندة إلى تلك الحركة السرمدية كما ذهبت إليه الفلاسفة حيث جوزوا التسلسل في الأمور المترتبة إذا لم تكن مجتمعة وزعموا أن الحركة الدائمة الواسطة بين عالمي القدم والحدوث فإنها ذات جهتين استمرار وتجدد فباعتبار استمرارها جاز استنادها إلى القديم وباعتبار تجددها صارت واسطة في صدور الحوادث عن المبدأ القديم وإذا لم يجز هذا الاستناد فلو كان الباري تعالى موجباً لكان الحادث اليومي المستند إليه بواسطة أو بغير واسطة قديماً هذا خلف فقد تم هذا الاستدلال بهذا الطريق أيضاً، ولقائل أن يقول ذلك البرهان البديع لا أيضاً إلا
حينئذ فيما سوى ذاته تعالى وصفاته مع أنه غير حادث على الفرض ولا يمكن حوادث متعاقبة إلى غير النهاية بدون أن يشترك في أمر كلي لئلا يلزم قدم المطلق فيصح الاشتراط ففيه تأمل. قوله: (فإنها ذات جهتين استمرار وتجدد قيل مرادهم بجهة استمرار الحركة استمراراً لحركة البسيطة أعني الحركة بمعنى التوسط فإنها في كل فلك أمر واحد شخصي مستمر من الأزل إلى الأبد عند الفلاسفة وبجهة حدوثها حدوث ما يلزمها بواسطة عدم استقرارها من الأوضاع الجزئية لأن الحركة بمعنى القطع لا تحقق لها ولا لأفرادها لتكون مستمرة أو حادثة فلا حاجة لحمل مرادهم بجهة الاستمرار على استمرار ماهية الحركة بل يجب أن يحمل على استمرار ماهية الحركة بالحقيقة أعني تلك الحالة البسيطة وتأويل العبارات المشعرة بإرادة الأولى بعد وضوح الحق أمر هين وحينئذ يبطل كلامهم بإجراء برهان التطبيق في الأوضاع لا في الحركات كما يدل عليه كلام الشارح.
قوله : ( فقد تم هذا الاستدلال بهذا الطريق أيضاً لا شك أن الطريق الثاني غير واف إذ لو لم يجوز ما أشير إليه في هذا، وجوز ما أشير إليه في الطريق الأول لم يلزم محذور وكان إقحام الشارح لفظ أيضاً، وحصر التمام في كونه بالطريق الأول حيث قال ولقائل أن يقول ذلك البرهان البديع أيضاً لا يتم إلا بالطريق الأول إيماء إلى هذا، اللهم إلا أن يكون القول بأن الاستدلال يتم بالطريق الأول، إذ لو جاز قدم ما سوى ذاته تعالى وصفاته أو جاز تعاقب صفاته التي لا تتناهى لم يلزم الأمر الرابع أعني التخلف عن المؤثر التام أما على الأول فلأنه جاز أن يكون ذلك القديم مختاراً كما مر وأما على الثاني فلجواز استناد الحادث إلى الموجب بتعاقب حوادث لا تتناهى وليس يلزم على شيء من هذين تخلف الأثر عن مؤثره الموجب التام لأم مؤثره إما مختار مع كون الباري تعالى موجباً، إما غير تام في المؤثرية لتوقف تأثيره فيه على شرائط حادثة غير متناهية قائمة بذاته تعالى
The second of the two paths: that we demonstrate concerning the daily originated event that it does not depend upon an event preceded by another without end, its dependence being preserved likewise by perpetual motion. For on the assumption of this dependence, it would be possible that the first principle is a necessitating cause emanating the existence of the daily event upon an eternal matter by means of successive dispositions dependent upon that eternal motion, as the philosophers held, for they allowed infinite regress in ordered things if they are not simultaneous. They claimed that perpetual motion, the intermediary between the worlds of eternity and origination, has two aspects: continuity and renewal. By virtue of its continuity, its dependence on the Eternal is permissible; by virtue of its renewal, it becomes an intermediary in the emanation of events from the eternal principle. If this dependence is not permissible, then if the Exalted Creator were a necessitating cause, the daily event depending on Him, whether mediated or not, would be eternal—a contradiction. Thus this proof is completed by this path as well. And one may say: that brilliant proof (al-burhān al-badīʿ) is not complete except then in what is other than His essence and attributes, while it is not originated by hypothesis, and it is not possible to have successive events to infinity without sharing in a universal matter, lest the eternal absolute be implied, so the condition is valid—but there is reflection upon it. He said: (For it has two aspects: continuity and renewal). It was said: Their intention by the aspect of continuity of motion is the continuity of simple motion, meaning motion in the sense of intermediate state (al-ḥaraka bi-maʿnā al-tawassuṭ), for it is in every sphere a single individual thing continuous from eternity to perpetuity according to the philosophers. And by the aspect of its origination is the origination of what accompanies it due to its instability from partial positions. Because motion in the sense of cutting (al-ḥaraka bi-maʿnā al-qaṭʿ) has no reality nor individuals to be continuous or originated. So there is no need to interpret their intention by the aspect of continuity as the continuity of the essence of motion; rather, it must be interpreted as the continuity of the essence of motion in reality, meaning that simple state. And interpreting expressions that indicate intending the first after the truth is clear is an easy matter. Then their statement is invalidated by applying the proof of correlation (burhān al-taṭbīq) to positions, not to motions, as indicated by the words of the commentator. He said: (So this proof is completed by this path as well). No doubt the second path is insufficient, for if what was indicated in this [first path] is not allowed, and what was indicated in the first path is allowed, no absurdity follows. And the insertion by the commentator of the word 'also' and his limiting completion to its being by the first path—where he said 'and one may say that brilliant proof is not complete except by the first path'—is an allusion to this. Unless it is said that the proof is completed by the first path: for if the eternity of what is other than His essence and attributes were allowed, or the succession of His attributes to infinity were allowed, then the fourth matter—namely, the delay of the effect from the complete cause—would not follow. As for the first, because it is possible for that eternal being to be free (mukhtār) as previously mentioned. As for the second, because it is possible for the event to depend on the necessitating cause by the succession of infinite events. And from neither of these does the delay of the effect from its complete necessitating cause follow, for its cause is either free while the Exalted Creator is a necessitating cause, or not complete in causality because its effect depends on infinite originated conditions subsisting in His essence.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-ṭarīq al-thānī) concerning the daily originated event (al-ḥādith al-yawmī) and then addresses an objection. The proof argues that the daily event cannot depend on an infinite chain of prior events; if it did, it could be explained by philosophers as depending on perpetual motion which has two aspects: continuity (allowing dependence on the Eternal) and renewal (allowing it to mediate origination). If this dependence is not allowed, then if God were a necessitating cause, the daily event would be eternal—a contradiction. The objector says that the brilliant proof (al-burhān al-badīʿ) is only complete for things other than God's essence and attributes, but the daily event is not originated by hypothesis, and infinite succession of events requires a universal shared matter. The commentator then explains the meaning of the two aspects of motion, distinguishing between motion as intermediate state (continuous) and motion as cutting (momentary). He concludes that the philosophers' view is invalid by applying the proof of correlation (burhān al-taṭbīq) to positions. Finally, he states that the second path is insufficient on its own; it only works if the first path (which denies the possibility of an eternal free intermediary or infinite succession of attributes) is accepted. Without that, the delay of the effect from the complete cause does not necessarily follow.

التسلسل

Key Takeaway
The second proof argues that the daily event cannot depend on an infinite chain, but the philosophers object by invoking perpetual motion with two aspects, leaving the issue unresolved for now.

Motion's Two Aspects and the Need for the First Proof
Explains the two aspects of motion and argues the second proof requires the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second proof is insufficient on its own and depends on the first proof's denial of an eternal free intermediary or infinite regress of attributes.

Core Idea
The second proof fails unless the first proof already rules out an eternal free intermediary and infinite attributes.

This lesson unpacks the commentator's clarification of the two aspects of motion—continuity and renewal—and his argument that the second proof is not independently valid. The commentator shows that the philosophers' view is refuted by applying the proof of correlation to positions, and then demonstrates that the second proof only works if the first proof's conclusions are presupposed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two aspects of motion
The commentator explains that by 'continuity' the philosophers mean motion as intermediate state (continuous), and by 'renewal' they mean the origination of accompanying partial positions.
Motion as cutting (الحركة بمعنى القطع) has no reality to be continuous.
2
See how burhān al-taṭbīq refutes them
The proof of correlation is applied to positions (not motions), invalidating the philosophers' argument.
This shows that the philosophers' solution fails.
3
Grasp the insufficiency of the second path
The commentator states that the second path is insufficient; it only works if the first path is accepted, because otherwise no absurdity follows.
The insertion of 'also' (أيضاً) hints at this dependency.
4
Recognize the two scenarios
If an eternal free intermediary is allowed or if infinite attributes are allowed, the delay of effect from the complete cause does not occur.
Thus the second proof requires the first proof to block these possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني من الطريقين أن نبين في الحادث اليومي أنه لا يستند الى حادث مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية محفوظاً استناده كذلك بحركة دائمة، إذ على تقدير هذا الاستناد جاز أن يكون المبدأ الأول موجباً مفيضاً لوجود الحادث اليومي على مادة قديمة بواسطة استعدادات متعاقبة مستندة إلى تلك الحركة السرمدية كما ذهبت إليه الفلاسفة حيث جوزوا التسلسل في الأمور المترتبة إذا لم تكن مجتمعة وزعموا أن الحركة الدائمة الواسطة بين عالمي القدم والحدوث فإنها ذات جهتين استمرار وتجدد فباعتبار استمرارها جاز استنادها إلى القديم وباعتبار تجددها صارت واسطة في صدور الحوادث عن المبدأ القديم وإذا لم يجز هذا الاستناد فلو كان الباري تعالى موجباً لكان الحادث اليومي المستند إليه بواسطة أو بغير واسطة قديماً هذا خلف فقد تم هذا الاستدلال بهذا الطريق أيضاً، ولقائل أن يقول ذلك البرهان البديع لا أيضاً إلا
حينئذ فيما سوى ذاته تعالى وصفاته مع أنه غير حادث على الفرض ولا يمكن حوادث متعاقبة إلى غير النهاية بدون أن يشترك في أمر كلي لئلا يلزم قدم المطلق فيصح الاشتراط ففيه تأمل. قوله: (فإنها ذات جهتين استمرار وتجدد قيل مرادهم بجهة استمرار الحركة استمراراً لحركة البسيطة أعني الحركة بمعنى التوسط فإنها في كل فلك أمر واحد شخصي مستمر من الأزل إلى الأبد عند الفلاسفة وبجهة حدوثها حدوث ما يلزمها بواسطة عدم استقرارها من الأوضاع الجزئية لأن الحركة بمعنى القطع لا تحقق لها ولا لأفرادها لتكون مستمرة أو حادثة فلا حاجة لحمل مرادهم بجهة الاستمرار على استمرار ماهية الحركة بل يجب أن يحمل على استمرار ماهية الحركة بالحقيقة أعني تلك الحالة البسيطة وتأويل العبارات المشعرة بإرادة الأولى بعد وضوح الحق أمر هين وحينئذ يبطل كلامهم بإجراء برهان التطبيق في الأوضاع لا في الحركات كما يدل عليه كلام الشارح.
قوله : ( فقد تم هذا الاستدلال بهذا الطريق أيضاً لا شك أن الطريق الثاني غير واف إذ لو لم يجوز ما أشير إليه في هذا، وجوز ما أشير إليه في الطريق الأول لم يلزم محذور وكان إقحام الشارح لفظ أيضاً، وحصر التمام في كونه بالطريق الأول حيث قال ولقائل أن يقول ذلك البرهان البديع أيضاً لا يتم إلا بالطريق الأول إيماء إلى هذا، اللهم إلا أن يكون القول بأن الاستدلال يتم بالطريق الأول، إذ لو جاز قدم ما سوى ذاته تعالى وصفاته أو جاز تعاقب صفاته التي لا تتناهى لم يلزم الأمر الرابع أعني التخلف عن المؤثر التام أما على الأول فلأنه جاز أن يكون ذلك القديم مختاراً كما مر وأما على الثاني فلجواز استناد الحادث إلى الموجب بتعاقب حوادث لا تتناهى وليس يلزم على شيء من هذين تخلف الأثر عن مؤثره الموجب التام لأم مؤثره إما مختار مع كون الباري تعالى موجباً، إما غير تام في المؤثرية لتوقف تأثيره فيه على شرائط حادثة غير متناهية قائمة بذاته تعالى
The second of the two paths: that we demonstrate concerning the daily originated event that it does not depend upon an event preceded by another without end, its dependence being preserved likewise by perpetual motion. For on the assumption of this dependence, it would be possible that the first principle is a necessitating cause emanating the existence of the daily event upon an eternal matter by means of successive dispositions dependent upon that eternal motion, as the philosophers held, for they allowed infinite regress in ordered things if they are not simultaneous. They claimed that perpetual motion, the intermediary between the worlds of eternity and origination, has two aspects: continuity and renewal. By virtue of its continuity, its dependence on the Eternal is permissible; by virtue of its renewal, it becomes an intermediary in the emanation of events from the eternal principle. If this dependence is not permissible, then if the Exalted Creator were a necessitating cause, the daily event depending on Him, whether mediated or not, would be eternal—a contradiction. Thus this proof is completed by this path as well. And one may say: that brilliant proof (al-burhān al-badīʿ) is not complete except then in what is other than His essence and attributes, while it is not originated by hypothesis, and it is not possible to have successive events to infinity without sharing in a universal matter, lest the eternal absolute be implied, so the condition is valid—but there is reflection upon it. He said: (For it has two aspects: continuity and renewal). It was said: Their intention by the aspect of continuity of motion is the continuity of simple motion, meaning motion in the sense of intermediate state (al-ḥaraka bi-maʿnā al-tawassuṭ), for it is in every sphere a single individual thing continuous from eternity to perpetuity according to the philosophers. And by the aspect of its origination is the origination of what accompanies it due to its instability from partial positions. Because motion in the sense of cutting (al-ḥaraka bi-maʿnā al-qaṭʿ) has no reality nor individuals to be continuous or originated. So there is no need to interpret their intention by the aspect of continuity as the continuity of the essence of motion; rather, it must be interpreted as the continuity of the essence of motion in reality, meaning that simple state. And interpreting expressions that indicate intending the first after the truth is clear is an easy matter. Then their statement is invalidated by applying the proof of correlation (burhān al-taṭbīq) to positions, not to motions, as indicated by the words of the commentator. He said: (So this proof is completed by this path as well). No doubt the second path is insufficient, for if what was indicated in this [first path] is not allowed, and what was indicated in the first path is allowed, no absurdity follows. And the insertion by the commentator of the word 'also' and his limiting completion to its being by the first path—where he said 'and one may say that brilliant proof is not complete except by the first path'—is an allusion to this. Unless it is said that the proof is completed by the first path: for if the eternity of what is other than His essence and attributes were allowed, or the succession of His attributes to infinity were allowed, then the fourth matter—namely, the delay of the effect from the complete cause—would not follow. As for the first, because it is possible for that eternal being to be free (mukhtār) as previously mentioned. As for the second, because it is possible for the event to depend on the necessitating cause by the succession of infinite events. And from neither of these does the delay of the effect from its complete necessitating cause follow, for its cause is either free while the Exalted Creator is a necessitating cause, or not complete in causality because its effect depends on infinite originated conditions subsisting in His essence.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-ṭarīq al-thānī) concerning the daily originated event (al-ḥādith al-yawmī) and then addresses an objection. The proof argues that the daily event cannot depend on an infinite chain of prior events; if it did, it could be explained by philosophers as depending on perpetual motion which has two aspects: continuity (allowing dependence on the Eternal) and renewal (allowing it to mediate origination). If this dependence is not allowed, then if God were a necessitating cause, the daily event would be eternal—a contradiction. The objector says that the brilliant proof (al-burhān al-badīʿ) is only complete for things other than God's essence and attributes, but the daily event is not originated by hypothesis, and infinite succession of events requires a universal shared matter. The commentator then explains the meaning of the two aspects of motion, distinguishing between motion as intermediate state (continuous) and motion as cutting (momentary). He concludes that the philosophers' view is invalid by applying the proof of correlation (burhān al-taṭbīq) to positions. Finally, he states that the second path is insufficient on its own; it only works if the first path (which denies the possibility of an eternal free intermediary or infinite succession of attributes) is accepted. Without that, the delay of the effect from the complete cause does not necessarily follow.

Key Terms
الحركة بمعنى التوسط
الحركة بمعنى القطع
برهان التطبيق
الموجب التام
التخلف عن المؤثر التام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the second proof is independently valid.
Correction: The commentator shows that without the first proof's denial of an eternal free intermediary and infinite regress of attributes, the second proof does not lead to a contradiction, so it is insufficient alone.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the second proof is insufficient on its own?
A) Because the daily event is not really originated
B) Because it relies on the first proof to deny the possibility of an eternal free intermediary or infinite regress of attributes
C) Because perpetual motion does not exist
D) Because the objection is unanswerable

Key Takeaway
The second proof is not a standalone argument; it only succeeds if the first proof has already ruled out an eternal free intermediary and infinite regress of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a secondary argument for God's existence without verifying its foundational premises? How might this lesson urge you to re-examine the foundations of your own conviction?
?
In your daily life, do you tend to settle for superficial reasoning (like 'cutting' motion) rather than seeking the deeper, continuous reality that sustains your faith? What would it mean for you to prioritize the 'first proof'?
?
The passage shows that an incomplete proof can lead to false comfort. Are there areas in your spiritual understanding where you rely on half-truths? Will you commit to seeking a complete, integrated understanding of tawḥīd, even if it requires more effort?

The Philosophers' Strongest Proof for God's Necessitation
Presents the strongest proof for God's necessitation and its argumentative basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the philosophers' strongest proof for God's necessitation and how it relies on the opponent's concessions.

Core Idea
The philosophers' strongest proof relies on the opponent's own admissions.

After summarizing earlier proofs for the contingency of the world and the impossibility of infinite succession, the author presents the most relied-upon argument for God's necessitation. This proof is not built on first principles but on what the opponent already accepts. This lesson unpacks that argument and its formulation.

Guided Path
1
Review the preceding proofs
Begin by noticing that the author mentions two earlier demonstrations: the path of possibility for contingency and the proof of correlation for the impossibility of infinite succession. These set the stage.
This shows the broader context of the philosophers' case.
2
Identify the strongest argument
Read the statement that the philosophers have many arguments, the strongest being what the author expressed as 'the first'. Understand that this argument is the one they rely on and prevail with.
3
Analyze the formulation
Examine how the proof is formulated: it uses the second path in an argumentative (ilzāmī) manner, relying on the opponent's agreement to two points: absence of an eternal free intermediary and the impermissibility of infinite successive events in God's essence.
The proof is not demonstrative from first principles but forces the opponent to accept the conclusion based on their own admissions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنت بعد إحاطتك بما تقدم من المباحث خليق بأن يسهل عليك ذلك أي بيان الأمور المذكورة، أما بيان حدوث ما سوى الله سبحانه وتعالى فيما مر من المسلك العام في حدوث العالم مطلقاً، أعني مسلك الإمكان أو المسلك الخاص بالأجسام مع نفي المجردات، وأما بيان امتناع تعاقب الصفات والحركات إلى غير النهاية فبالبرهان التطبيقي ( احتج الحكماء ) على إيجابه تعالى ( بوجوه كثيرة أقواها ما صرح به المصنف وعبر عنه بقوله ( الأول ) لأنه الذي عليه يعولون وبه يصولون وتقريره أن يقال : بالطريق الثاني إلزامياً حيث وافق الخصم على عدم قديم مختار متوسط يستند إليه الحوادث وعلى عدم جواز قيام حوادث متعاقبة لا إلى نهاية بذاته تعالى . قوله : ( أو جاز تعاقب صفاته تعالى إلخ قيل عليه توقف البرهان المذكور على امتناع قديم سواه تعالى ،مسلم وأما توقفه على بيان امتناع تعاقب صفاته التي لا تتناهى فممنوع لأنه لو تعاقب صفاته تعالى كذلك لزم التسلسل فتندرج في الأمر الثالث وأنت خبير بأن المذكور في البرهان البديع هو التسلسل المحال بالاتفاق وهو التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة المترتبة المجتمعة في الوجود فلا يندرج تعاقب الصفات فيه على أن المصنف قد أشار إلى بطلان مطلق التسلسل في الدليل الثاني أعني قوله وإن شئت قلت مع أنه ذكر ثانياً أن تمام الدليل موقوف على بيان بطلانه فغرض الشارح أن البرهان البديع يشاركه في هذا التوقف وهذا معنى صحيح يرد عليه ما ذكر فإن قلت : إذا كان بيان لزوم التخلف في البرهان البديع ولزوم قدم الحادث في قوله وإن شئت قلت : إلخ يتضمن الإشارة إلى بطلان التسلسل مطلقاً كان عدم جواز تعاقب الصفات والحوادث الغير المتناهيتين معلوماً منه فأي حاجة إلى التصريح بأن الدليل الثاني أو الأول إنما يتم بكذا قلت : لما كان نزاع الخصم في بطلان التسلسل قويا صرح بأن تمام الدليل موقوف على إبطاله وأنه قد أبطل فيما سبق اهتماما بشأنه ولا ضير في ذلك . قوله : ( لأنه الذي عليه يعولون وبه يصولون) توجيه لكلام المصنف فإن التعبير عن الوجه بالأول سيما بعد قوله بوجوه يستدعي يورد بعده الثاني والثالث بهذين العنوانين ولذا قال الكرماني في شرحه : أن المصنف خط القلم على قوله بوجوه إذ لا تعدد فيما ذكر في الكتاب من جانبهم فالنسخة هكذا احتج الحكماء بأن تعلق إلخ . ظاهراً أن هذا
And after your comprehensive knowledge of what has preceded from the investigations, you are worthy that this—i.e., the explanation of the mentioned matters—should be easy for you. As for the demonstration of the contingency of everything other than God—may He be glorified and exalted—it is in what has passed of the general path in the contingency of the world absolutely, namely the path of possibility (maslak al-imkān) or the specific path concerning bodies with the denial of spiritual substances. As for the demonstration of the impossibility of the succession of attributes and motions to infinity, it is by the proof of correlation (al-burhān al-taṭbīqī). The philosophers argued for His necessitation (ījābuhu taʿālā) with many arguments, the strongest of which is what the author clearly stated and expressed by his saying 'the first', because it is what they rely on and with it they prevail. Its formulation is that it is said: by the second path in an argumentative manner, since the opponent has agreed to the absence of an eternal free intermediary to which events depend, and to the impermissibility of the existence of successive events without end in His essence. He said: (Or if the succession of His attributes be allowed, etc.). It was said against this: the dependence of the mentioned proof on the denial of something eternal other than Him is conceded; but its dependence on the demonstration of the impossibility of the succession of His attributes to infinity is denied. For if His attributes succeeded in that manner, infinite regress would follow, and thus it would fall under the third matter. And you are an expert that what is mentioned in the brilliant proof (al-burhān al-badīʿ) is the infinite regress that is impossible by consensus, which is infinite regress in existing ordered things simultaneous in existence. So the succession of attributes does not fall under it. Moreover, the author pointed to the invalidity of absolute infinite regress in the second proof, namely his saying 'And if you wish, you may say', while he mentioned secondly that the completion of the proof depends on demonstrating its invalidity. So the purpose of the commentator is that the brilliant proof shares in this dependence. This is a correct meaning that is objected to as mentioned. For if you say: If the demonstration of the necessity of delay in the brilliant proof and the necessity of the eternity of the event in his saying 'And if you wish, you may say' etc. include an indication of the invalidity of absolute infinite regress, then the impermissibility of the succession of infinite attributes and events is known from it. So what need is there to state explicitly that the second proof or the first is only completed by such? I say: Because the opponent's dispute concerning the invalidity of infinite regress was strong, he stated explicitly that the completion of the proof depends on refuting it, and that he has refuted it in what preceded, out of concern for its importance. And there is no harm in that. He said: (Because it is what they rely on and with it they prevail). This is a clarification of the author's words. For the expression of the argument as 'the first' especially after his saying 'with many arguments' requires that he should mention after it the second and third with these titles. Therefore al-Kirmānī said in his commentary: The author crossed out his saying 'with many arguments' since there is no multiplicity in what is mentioned in the book from their side. Thus the copy is: 'The philosophers argued that it depends, etc.' Apparently this is.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage begins with a transitional summary, stating that after having studied the previous investigations, explaining the matters mentioned should be easy. The contingency of everything other than God is demonstrated by the general path of possibility or the specific path concerning bodies with denial of spiritual substances. The impossibility of the succession of attributes and motions to infinity is shown by the proof of correlation. Then the passage presents the philosophers' strongest argument for God's necessitation (ījāb). The argument is formulated in a second-path argumentative manner, relying on the opponent's admission that there is no eternal free intermediary and no infinite regress of events in God's essence. The discussion then addresses an objection: the dependence of the proof on the denial of an eternal being other than God is conceded, but its dependence on the denial of infinite succession of attributes is denied. The objector argues that the succession of attributes would lead to infinite regress of the kind that is impossible by consensus (i.e., simultaneous ordered things), and that the author himself indicated the invalidity of absolute infinite regress in his second proof. The commentator responds by noting that the author explicitly stated the proof's dependence on refuting infinite regress because the opponent's dispute was strong. Finally, there is a textual note: al-Kirmānī says the author crossed out 'with many arguments' because there is no multiplicity; the correct reading is simply 'The philosophers argued that it depends...'.

مسلك الإمكان
البرهان التطبيقي

What two concessions from the opponent does the philosophers' strongest proof rely on?
A) The existence of an eternal free intermediary and the possibility of infinite succession
B) The absence of an eternal free intermediary and the impermissibility of infinite successive events in God's essence
C) The contingency of the world and the impossibility of infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' strongest proof for God's necessitation is an argumentative proof that forces the conclusion by relying on the opponent's acceptance that there is no eternal free intermediary and that infinite successive events cannot occur in God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers' argument forces the opponent to accept the truth based on their own admissions. When you reflect on your own beliefs, are there concessions you have made intellectually that you have not fully applied to your life? For example, do you truly accept that nothing in your life happens without God's permission, yet still worry as if events are random? Today, commit to aligning your actions with one such admission you already hold.
?
The opponent conceded that there is no eternal free intermediary other than God. In your daily life, do you treat anything—money, status, relationships—as an intermediary that gives you security or happiness independent of God? Identify one such false intermediary and consciously rely on God alone for it this week.
?
If you were in a debate about God's existence, would you be willing to accept the premises of this argument honestly? What about your own intellectual pride might prevent you from conceding a point that leads to a truth you resist? Ask yourself: What truth about God am I avoiding because it would require me to change?

Objection to the Proof and Textual Clarification
Presents an objection and response on the proof's dependence, and a textual note on the number of arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against the philosophers' proof and the commentator's response, as well as a textual note about the number of arguments.

Core Idea
The proof does not require the impossibility of infinite succession of attributes; a distinction is made between kinds of infinite regress.

After presenting the strongest proof, the commentary addresses an objection: the proof seems to depend on the impossibility of infinite succession of attributes in God's essence. The response shows that this concerns a different type of infinite regress from the one universally rejected. The lesson also includes a textual clarification about the phrase 'with many arguments'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: the proof depends on denying an eternal being other than God (conceded) and also on demonstrating the impossibility of infinite succession of His attributes (denied). The objector argues that if attributes succeed infinitely, infinite regress would follow, which falls under the third matter (the impossible regress).
The objector is trying to show that the proof's requirement is already included in the third matter.
2
Examine the commentator's response
The commentator counters that the infinite regress mentioned in the 'brilliant proof' (al-burhān al-badīʿ) is the one impossible by consensus: simultaneous, ordered, existing things. The succession of attributes does not fall under that kind.
Thus the proof does not depend on a separate demonstration.
3
Address the author's explicit statement
The commentator notes that the author himself indicated the invalidity of absolute infinite regress in the second proof, and explicitly stated that the completion of the proof depends on refuting it. This is because the opponent's denial of regress was strong, so the author emphasizes that point.
4
Consider the textual note
Finally, a textual issue: al-Kirmānī claimed that the author crossed out 'with many arguments' because there is no multiplicity in the book; the correct reading is simply 'the philosophers argued that it depends...'.
This explains why only one argument is given in the margin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنت بعد إحاطتك بما تقدم من المباحث خليق بأن يسهل عليك ذلك أي بيان الأمور المذكورة، أما بيان حدوث ما سوى الله سبحانه وتعالى فيما مر من المسلك العام في حدوث العالم مطلقاً، أعني مسلك الإمكان أو المسلك الخاص بالأجسام مع نفي المجردات، وأما بيان امتناع تعاقب الصفات والحركات إلى غير النهاية فبالبرهان التطبيقي ( احتج الحكماء ) على إيجابه تعالى ( بوجوه كثيرة أقواها ما صرح به المصنف وعبر عنه بقوله ( الأول ) لأنه الذي عليه يعولون وبه يصولون وتقريره أن يقال : بالطريق الثاني إلزامياً حيث وافق الخصم على عدم قديم مختار متوسط يستند إليه الحوادث وعلى عدم جواز قيام حوادث متعاقبة لا إلى نهاية بذاته تعالى . قوله : ( أو جاز تعاقب صفاته تعالى إلخ قيل عليه توقف البرهان المذكور على امتناع قديم سواه تعالى ،مسلم وأما توقفه على بيان امتناع تعاقب صفاته التي لا تتناهى فممنوع لأنه لو تعاقب صفاته تعالى كذلك لزم التسلسل فتندرج في الأمر الثالث وأنت خبير بأن المذكور في البرهان البديع هو التسلسل المحال بالاتفاق وهو التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة المترتبة المجتمعة في الوجود فلا يندرج تعاقب الصفات فيه على أن المصنف قد أشار إلى بطلان مطلق التسلسل في الدليل الثاني أعني قوله وإن شئت قلت مع أنه ذكر ثانياً أن تمام الدليل موقوف على بيان بطلانه فغرض الشارح أن البرهان البديع يشاركه في هذا التوقف وهذا معنى صحيح يرد عليه ما ذكر فإن قلت : إذا كان بيان لزوم التخلف في البرهان البديع ولزوم قدم الحادث في قوله وإن شئت قلت : إلخ يتضمن الإشارة إلى بطلان التسلسل مطلقاً كان عدم جواز تعاقب الصفات والحوادث الغير المتناهيتين معلوماً منه فأي حاجة إلى التصريح بأن الدليل الثاني أو الأول إنما يتم بكذا قلت : لما كان نزاع الخصم في بطلان التسلسل قويا صرح بأن تمام الدليل موقوف على إبطاله وأنه قد أبطل فيما سبق اهتماما بشأنه ولا ضير في ذلك . قوله : ( لأنه الذي عليه يعولون وبه يصولون) توجيه لكلام المصنف فإن التعبير عن الوجه بالأول سيما بعد قوله بوجوه يستدعي يورد بعده الثاني والثالث بهذين العنوانين ولذا قال الكرماني في شرحه : أن المصنف خط القلم على قوله بوجوه إذ لا تعدد فيما ذكر في الكتاب من جانبهم فالنسخة هكذا احتج الحكماء بأن تعلق إلخ . ظاهراً أن هذا
And after your comprehensive knowledge of what has preceded from the investigations, you are worthy that this—i.e., the explanation of the mentioned matters—should be easy for you. As for the demonstration of the contingency of everything other than God—may He be glorified and exalted—it is in what has passed of the general path in the contingency of the world absolutely, namely the path of possibility (maslak al-imkān) or the specific path concerning bodies with the denial of spiritual substances. As for the demonstration of the impossibility of the succession of attributes and motions to infinity, it is by the proof of correlation (al-burhān al-taṭbīqī). The philosophers argued for His necessitation (ījābuhu taʿālā) with many arguments, the strongest of which is what the author clearly stated and expressed by his saying 'the first', because it is what they rely on and with it they prevail. Its formulation is that it is said: by the second path in an argumentative manner, since the opponent has agreed to the absence of an eternal free intermediary to which events depend, and to the impermissibility of the existence of successive events without end in His essence. He said: (Or if the succession of His attributes be allowed, etc.). It was said against this: the dependence of the mentioned proof on the denial of something eternal other than Him is conceded; but its dependence on the demonstration of the impossibility of the succession of His attributes to infinity is denied. For if His attributes succeeded in that manner, infinite regress would follow, and thus it would fall under the third matter. And you are an expert that what is mentioned in the brilliant proof (al-burhān al-badīʿ) is the infinite regress that is impossible by consensus, which is infinite regress in existing ordered things simultaneous in existence. So the succession of attributes does not fall under it. Moreover, the author pointed to the invalidity of absolute infinite regress in the second proof, namely his saying 'And if you wish, you may say', while he mentioned secondly that the completion of the proof depends on demonstrating its invalidity. So the purpose of the commentator is that the brilliant proof shares in this dependence. This is a correct meaning that is objected to as mentioned. For if you say: If the demonstration of the necessity of delay in the brilliant proof and the necessity of the eternity of the event in his saying 'And if you wish, you may say' etc. include an indication of the invalidity of absolute infinite regress, then the impermissibility of the succession of infinite attributes and events is known from it. So what need is there to state explicitly that the second proof or the first is only completed by such? I say: Because the opponent's dispute concerning the invalidity of infinite regress was strong, he stated explicitly that the completion of the proof depends on refuting it, and that he has refuted it in what preceded, out of concern for its importance. And there is no harm in that. He said: (Because it is what they rely on and with it they prevail). This is a clarification of the author's words. For the expression of the argument as 'the first' especially after his saying 'with many arguments' requires that he should mention after it the second and third with these titles. Therefore al-Kirmānī said in his commentary: The author crossed out his saying 'with many arguments' since there is no multiplicity in what is mentioned in the book from their side. Thus the copy is: 'The philosophers argued that it depends, etc.' Apparently this is.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage begins with a transitional summary, stating that after having studied the previous investigations, explaining the matters mentioned should be easy. The contingency of everything other than God is demonstrated by the general path of possibility or the specific path concerning bodies with denial of spiritual substances. The impossibility of the succession of attributes and motions to infinity is shown by the proof of correlation. Then the passage presents the philosophers' strongest argument for God's necessitation (ījāb). The argument is formulated in a second-path argumentative manner, relying on the opponent's admission that there is no eternal free intermediary and no infinite regress of events in God's essence. The discussion then addresses an objection: the dependence of the proof on the denial of an eternal being other than God is conceded, but its dependence on the denial of infinite succession of attributes is denied. The objector argues that the succession of attributes would lead to infinite regress of the kind that is impossible by consensus (i.e., simultaneous ordered things), and that the author himself indicated the invalidity of absolute infinite regress in his second proof. The commentator responds by noting that the author explicitly stated the proof's dependence on refuting infinite regress because the opponent's dispute was strong. Finally, there is a textual note: al-Kirmānī says the author crossed out 'with many arguments' because there is no multiplicity; the correct reading is simply 'The philosophers argued that it depends...'.

Key Terms
التسلسل المطلق
البرهان البديع
التعاقب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the philosophers' proof requires demonstrating the impossibility of any kind of infinite succession of attributes.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the proof only relates to the impossible regress by consensus—simultaneous ordered existence—not to temporal succession.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what kind of infinite regress is impossible by consensus and referred to in the brilliant proof?
A) The succession of events over time
B) The succession of attributes in God
C) Infinite regress in existing ordered things simultaneous in existence
D) Any sequential regress without beginning

Key Takeaway
The proof does not require a separate demonstration of the impossibility of successive attributes because this does not fall under the agreed-upon impossible regress; the author's explicit statement on regress is to counter a strong opponent objection.

?
The commentator explicitly repeats a point to counter a strong opponent. Is there a truth you have avoided articulating clearly out of fear of repetition? Identify one and resolve to state it boldly to someone who needs to hear it.

The Philosophers' Strongest Proof for God's Necessitation
The commentator presents the philosophers' strongest proof for God's necessitating action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the philosophers' strongest proof for God's necessitating action and why it is considered a compulsory argument.

Core Idea
The proof uses the opponent's own premises to force the conclusion.

This lesson examines the most forceful proof offered by the philosophers (falāsifa) to demonstrate that God must act by necessity. The commentator presents it as a compulsory argument (ilzāmī) that builds on premises the opponent already accepts. By understanding this argument, you will see how classical kalam engages with philosophical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'إيجابه', 'الطريق الثاني', 'إلزامياً', 'قديم مختار متوسط', 'حوادث متعاقبة'.
2
Identify the proof structure
The passage describes the proof as a 'compulsory argument' (ilzāmī). What are the two premises that the opponent must agree to? Find them in the text.
The two premises are: (1) no eternal free intermediate, (2) no infinite succession of temporal events in God's essence.
3
Understand why it is 'compulsory'
Reflect on why this argument is called 'compulsory' (ilzāmī). It does not prove God's necessitation directly from first principles; instead, it forces the conclusion using premises the opponent already concedes.
This shows a dialectical strategy in kalam.
4
Consider the significance
Think about why the commentator says this proof is 'the strongest' and why the philosophers rely on it. How does it differ from other proofs?
The commentary mentions it is the one 'upon which they rely and with which they triumph.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الحكماء على إيجابه تعالى بوجوه كثيرة أقواها ما صرح به المصنف وعبر عنه بقوله ( الأول ) لأنه الذي عليه يعولون وبه يصولون وتقريره أن يقال : بالطريق الثاني إلزامياً حيث وافق الخصم على عدم قديم مختار متوسط يستند إليه الحوادث وعلى عدم جواز قيام حوادث متعاقبة لا إلى نهاية بذاته تعالى
The philosophers argued for His necessitation (ījāb), exalted is He, with many proofs, the strongest of which is what the author stated and expressed by his saying 'the first', because upon it they rely and with it they triumph. Its formulation is to say: by the second way, as a compulsory argument (ilzāmiyyan), since the opponent has agreed to the non-existence of an eternal free intermediate upon which the temporal events depend, and to the non-permissibility of the occurrence of successive temporal events without end in His essence.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' strongest proof for God's necessitating action. The commentator notes that the author refers to it as 'the first' proof. The proof is formulated as a compulsory argument (ilzāmī) because it takes premises that the opponent already accepts. The two premises are: (1) that there is no eternal free intermediate agent on which temporal events depend; (2) that it is not permissible for an infinite succession of temporal events to occur in God's essence. With these agreed premises, the argument forces the conclusion that God must be a necessitator.

Key Terms
إيجابه
الطريق الثاني
إلزامياً
قديم مختار متوسط
حوادث متعاقبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the philosophers' proof relies on assuming God's nature as a necessitator from the start.
Correction: In fact, the argument is a compulsory one (ilzāmī) that uses premises the opponent already accepts—namely, the denial of an eternal free intermediate and the impossibility of infinite accidents in God's essence—to force the conclusion.

Checkpoint
What are the two premises that the opponent must accept for the compulsory argument?
A) That there is no eternal free intermediate and no infinite succession of accidents in God's essence
B) That God is a free agent and the world is created
C) That the world is eternal and there is no creator
D) That God's attributes are eternal and not subject to change

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' strongest proof for God's necessitation is a compulsory argument that uses the opponent's own premises to force the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just considered a proof that forces you to accept that God's action is necessary if you deny an eternal free intermediate and an infinite series of events in His essence. Do you hold any premises about God that lead to conclusions you are uncomfortable accepting? Where in your life are you inconsistent in following the logic of your own beliefs?
?
This argument relies on what you already concede. Examine your own faith: are there areas where you accept one thing about God (e.g., His power) but live as if another is true (e.g., reliance on secondary causes)? How should the reality of God as the Necessitator change your daily trust in Him rather than in means?

Refuting the Dualists: God Creates Both Good and Evil
Refutes dualists and explains divine naming principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualists' objection against God's power over evil, the refutation that God creates all things, and the reasons why the term 'evil-doer' is not applied to Him.

Core Idea
God creates all good and evil, but 'evil-doer' is not His name.

The dualists, including the Magians, argued that if God had power over evil, He would be both good and evil, so they posited two deities. This passage presents the orthodox response: God creates everything, good and evil, but the name 'evil-doer' is not used for Him due to linguistic implication and divine naming conventions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the complete Arabic passage first without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: sect claim, answer, and clarifying notes.
The passage includes marginal comments (sharh) that expand on the main text.
2
Identify the dualists' claim
The dualists (al-Thanawiyya) assert that God cannot have power over evil, otherwise He would be both good and evil. Understand why this leads them to believe in two gods.
The claim is that a good being cannot create evil without becoming evil.
3
Understand the main answer
The response accepts the consequence: God creates all goods and evils, but the term 'evil-doer' is not applied to Him. Focus on the two reasons given for this.
First reason: calling Him evil would imply evil predominates. Second: divine names are based on revelation.
4
Examine the clarifying notes
The commentary points out that the dualists' proof only negates the act of evil, not the power. Also, the answer shifts if 'good' means 'predominance of good' rather than 'creator of good'.
These notes strengthen the refutation and prevent misunderstandings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثالثة: الثنوية ومنهم المجوس فإنهم قالوا: إنه تعالى لا يقدر على الشر وإلا لكان خيراً شريراً معاً فلذلك أثبتوا إلهين كما مر تفصيله والجواب: أنا نلتزم التالي فإنه تعالى خالق للخيرات والشرور كلها وإنما لا يطلق لفظ الشرير عليه كما لا يطلق عليه لفظ خالق القردة والخنازير مع أنه خالق لهما لأحد الأمرين، إما لأنه يوهم أن يكون الشر غالباً في فعله كما يقال: فلان شرير أي ذلك مقتضى نحيزته أي طبيعته والغالب على هجيراه أي دأبه وعادته، وإما لعدم التوقيف من الشرع وأسماء الله تعالى توقيفية.

قوله: (وإلا لكان خيراً وشريراً معاً) لا يخفى أن دليلهم على تقدير تمامه لا يثبت مدعاهم لأنه إنما ينفي فعل الشر والمدعى نفي القدرة عليه ومن البين أن القادر على الشر الغير الفاعل إياه لا يكون شريرا.

قوله: (والجواب إنا نلتزم التالي) هذا على تقدير أن يراد بالخير خالق الخير، وبالشرير خالق الشر، وأما إن أريد بالخير من يغلب خيره على شره وبالشر من يغلب شره على خيره فالجواب منع الملازمة ولما سبق فيه التفصيل اكتفى هاهنا بما ذكره.

قوله: (مقتضى نحيزته والغالب على هجيراه) النحيزة بالنون المفتوحة والحاء المهملة والزاي المعجمة على وزن فعيلة والهجير بكسر الهاء والجيم المشددة وفتح الراء المهملة وفيه لغات أخرى وهي الهجير على وزن الفسيق والإهجيري بكسر الهمزة وفتح الراء وهجرياً بكسر الهمزة والراء وتشديد الياء.
The Third Sect: The Dualists (al-Thanawiyya), among them the Magians, for they said: He, exalted, does not have power over evil, otherwise He would be good and evil together. Therefore they affirmed two gods, as its detail has passed. And the answer: We accept the consequence, for He, exalted, is the creator of all goods and evils, but the term 'evil-doer' is not applied to Him just as the term 'creator of apes and pigs' is not applied to Him despite His being their creator, for one of two reasons: either because it would imply that evil predominates in His actions, as it is said: 'So-and-so is evil' meaning that is the requirement of his nature (nakhizatihi), and the predominance of his habit (hijiraahu), i.e., his custom; or for lack of authorization (tawqif) from the Law, and the names of God are authorized (tawqifiyya).

His saying: ('otherwise He would be good and evil together') — it is not hidden that their proof, if complete, does not prove their claim, because it only negates the doing of evil, while the claim is the negation of power over it, and it is clear that one who has power over evil but does not do it is not evil.

His saying: ('and the answer: we accept the consequence') — this is on the supposition that by 'good' is meant the creator of good, and by 'evil' is meant the creator of evil. But if by 'good' is meant one whose good predominates over his evil, and by 'evil' one whose evil predominates over his good, then the answer is denial of the implication; and since the detail has preceded, he suffices here with what he mentioned.

His saying: ('the requirement of his nature and the predominance of his habit') — al-nakhizah is with an open noon, unpointed ha, and dotted zal, on the pattern of fa‘ilah; and al-hijirah is with a kasra on the ha, shadda on the jim, and fatha on the ra; and it has other dialects: al-hijir on the pattern of fasīq, al-ihjīrī with kasra on the hamza and fatha on the ra, and hijriyyan with kasra on the hamza and ra and tashdid on the ya.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualists (specifically the Magians) who denied that God has power over evil, because if He did, He would be both good and evil. The response accepts the consequence: God creates both goods and evils, but the name 'evil' is not applied to Him for two reasons: first, because it would imply evil predominates in His actions (like calling a person evil due to his nature); second, because divine names are based on revelation (tawqif) and are not applied arbitrarily. The marginal notes clarify: the dualists' argument only negates the act of evil, not the power to do it; a powerful being who does not actually do evil is not called evil. Another note explains that the response about accepting the consequence assumes 'good' means creator of good and 'evil' means creator of evil; if interpreted as 'one whose good predominates' then the implication is denied. The third note provides lexical details for the words 'nakhizah' and 'hijirah'.

Key Terms
ثنوية
مجوس
شر
خير
توقيف
نحيزة
هجيراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates evil, then He must be evil.
Correction: God creates all things, good and evil, but the name 'evil-doer' is not applied to Him because it would imply evil predominates in His actions, and because divine names are based on revelation, not human logic.

Checkpoint
What are the two reasons given for not applying the term 'evil-doer' to God despite His creating evil?
A) Because evil does not come from God, and because names are conventional.
B) Because it would imply evil predominates in His actions, and because divine names are based on revelation.
C) Because God does not create all evils, and because the term is inappropriate.
D) Because the dualists do not affirm God's power, and because God is good.

Key Takeaway
God is the creator of all good and evil, but He is not called evil because that would imply a flawed nature, and because His names are derived from revelation alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience evil in your life, do you ever find yourself questioning God's goodness? How does this lesson's distinction between God being the creator of evil and being called 'evil-doer' help you respond to that doubt? Write down one specific intention to remind yourself of this truth when facing hardship.
?
The lesson says God's names are tawqifiyya—based only on revelation. Are there any names or descriptions you have used for God that are not clearly from the Quran or Sunnah? What will you do to align your speech about God with this principle? Commit to one practical step.
?
The dualists' error was to judge God by human standards of fairness. In what areas of your life do you still judge God by your own sense of what is fair? How can you surrender your understanding to revelation today? Write a short prayer asking for trust in Allah's wisdom.

Refuting al-Nazzam: God's Absolute Power Over All Acts
Sanusi refutes al-Nazzam by asserting ugliness is relative and that deterrent does not negate power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to al-Nazzam's claim that God cannot perform ugly acts, based on the relativity of ugliness and the distinction between power and actual performance.

Core Idea
Ugliness is relative; God owns all, so He can act. A deterrent doesn't negate power.

Al-Nazzam asserted that God cannot do ugly acts because doing so would imply foolishness or ignorance. The answer here is twofold: first, ugliness is not absolute but relative to God as owner; second, even if an act is ugly relative to God, refraining from it does not mean inability. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of al-Nazzam's claim and the response. Notice the structure: claim, then answer in two parts.
The passage uses a typical kalam format of objection and reply.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
First, ugliness is relative to God as owner. Second, even if ugliness is granted, the deterrent does not negate power. Understand each part's logical role.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for God's omnipotence. God's power remains absolute even if He never performs certain acts due to His wisdom.
This connects to broader discussions of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الرابعة: النظام ومتبعوه قالوا: لا يقدر على الفعل القبيح لأنه مع العلم بقبحه سفه ودونه جهل وكلاهما نقص يجب تنزيهه تعالى عنه والجواب: أنه لا قبيح بالنسبة إليه فإن الكل ملكه فله أن يتصرف فيه على أي وجه أراد وإن سلم قبح الفعل بالقياس إليه فغايته عدم الفعل لوجود الصارف عنه وهو القبح وذلك لا ينفي القدرة عليه.

قوله: (لأنه مع العلم بقبحه سفه) قيل: إنما يلزم السفه على تقدير تسليم قبح الفعل بالنسبة إليه تعالى إذا لم تكن جهة الحسن راجحة كثيرة إذ الحكمة حينئذ تقتضي وجود ذلك الفعل ليحصل حسنه الكثير وإن لزم القبح القليل وهذا كما قال الحكماء: الشر داخل في القضاء على سبيل التبع. وهذا إنما يرد عليهم إذا لم يكن كلامهم في القبح المطلق أو الذي جهة قبحه كثيرة راجحة.

قوله: (وذلك لا ينفي القدرة عليه) فإن قلت: بل ينفيها إذ المقدور عندنا ما يصح فعله و تركه فلو كان القبيح مقدورا على تقدير تسليم القبح بالقياس إليه تعالى لزم جواز النقص، تعالى ذلك علواً كبيراً قلت: المحذور هاهنا إنما لزم من الامتناع الغيري أعني من وصف القبح الخارج عن ذات الفعل لا منه نفسه فلا ينافي الإمكان الذاتي الكافي في المقدورية.
The Fourth Sect: al-Nazzam and his followers said: He does not have power over the ugly act, because with knowledge of its ugliness it is foolishness (safah), and without knowledge it is ignorance, and both are defects from which He, exalted, must be declared free. The answer: There is nothing ugly relative to Him, for everything is His property, so He has the right to dispose of it in any way He wills. And if the ugliness of the act relative to Him is granted, then the utmost of it is non-performance due to the presence of the deterrent (al-sarif) which is the ugliness, and that does not negate power over it.

His saying: ('because with knowledge of its ugliness it is foolishness') — it is said: The charge of foolishness only follows on the assumption that the ugliness of the act relative to Him, exalted, is granted, if there is no preponderant aspect of goodness, because wisdom then requires the existence of that act to achieve its great goodness even if a little ugliness follows. This is as the philosophers said: Evil enters into decree by way of concomitance. And this only refutes them if their speech is not about absolute ugliness or ugliness whose aspect of ugliness is great and preponderant.

His saying: ('and that does not negate power over it') — If you say: Rather it negates it, because the thing that is empowered (al-maqdur) in our view is what is valid to do and leave; so if the ugly act were empowered on the assumption of granting ugliness relative to Him, exalted, it would necessitate the possibility of deficiency—exalted is He far above that! I say: The difficulty here follows only from extrinsic impossibility (al-imtina‘ al-ghayri), i.e., from the attribute of ugliness external to the act itself, not from the act itself; so it does not conflict with intrinsic possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) which suffices for being empowered.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Nazzam and his followers who argued that God cannot do ugly acts because doing so would be either foolish (if done knowingly) or ignorant (if done unknowingly), both being defects. The answer has two parts. First, ugliness is relative: everything belongs to God, so He can dispose of it as He wills. Second, even if we grant ugliness relative to God, the worst that follows is that He refrains from the act due to a deterrent (the ugliness), which does not negate the power itself. The marginal notes elaborate: the charge of foolishness only applies if there is no preponderant good; wisdom might require the act despite a little ugliness, as philosophers say evil enters by way of concomitance. This refutation works unless the ugliness is absolute or its aspect is overwhelmingly preponderant. The second marginal note addresses an objection: if the thing is not actually done, its power is negated because power requires both doing and leaving. The reply distinguishes intrinsic possibility from extrinsic impossibility: the ugliness is an external attribute that prevents the act, not the act's own nature; thus intrinsic possibility (sufficient for being empowered) remains.

Key Terms
نظام
قبيح
سفه
جهل
صارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God never does an ugly act, He must lack the power to do it.
Correction: The text explains that He refrains from such acts due to the deterrent of ugliness, not due to lack of power. Power includes the ability to do even if not done.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the most fundamental reason why al-Nazzam's argument fails?
A) God never does ugly acts.
B) Ugliness is relative to God as owner.
C) God cannot do foolish acts.
D) The philosophers said so.

Key Takeaway
God's power is absolute; the non-occurrence of an ugly act is due to an external deterrent, not to any incapacity inherent in Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that feels unjust or ‘ugly’ in your life, do you truly believe that God has power over it and could have prevented it? How does the truth that ugliness is relative to Him change your perspective on that situation? Will you commit to trusting His wisdom even when you don't understand?
?
This lesson shows that God refrains from ugly acts due to a deterrent—not because He cannot. Are there sins in your life that you think are ‘too much’ for God to forgive? How does the distinction between power and deterrent affect your view of God's mercy? What step will you take today to ask for forgiveness with full confidence?
?
If you accept that God owns everything and can do anything, does that change how you react to His commands and prohibitions? Are you sometimes tempted to think a command is ‘ugly’ or unreasonable? How will you remind yourself that His wisdom is greater? Write one practical commitment to obey without questioning His right to command.

Intrinsic Possibility vs. Extrinsic Impossibility
Sanusi explains the conditional charge of foolishness and distinguishes intrinsic possibility from extrinsic impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the charge of foolishness is conditional and how intrinsic possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) is sufficient for being empowered, even if extrinsic factors prevent actualization.

Core Idea
An act can be intrinsically possible for God yet never occur due to external reasons like ugliness.

Having established the basic refutation, the text now delves into two important nuances. First, the charge that God's doing an ugly act would be foolishness only applies if there is no outweighing good. Second, even if we accept that an act is ugly relative to God, the fact that it is not performed does not negate God's power over it, because the impossibility is extrinsic to the act's essence. Let's unpack these points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the two marginal notes. Notice the conditional logic and the distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic impossibility.
This passage is more advanced and builds on the previous lesson.
2
Analyze the conditional nature of foolishness
Understand that the charge of foolishness only holds if there is no outweighing good; wisdom may require an act with minor evil for a greater good, as philosophers recognize.
This mirrors the principle that evil enters creation by way of concomitance.
3
Understand intrinsic vs. extrinsic impossibility
Examine the objection that power requires ability to both do and leave, and the reply that the ugliness is an external attribute causing extrinsic impossibility, which does not negate intrinsic possibility.
Intrinsic possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) is sufficient for an act to be within God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الرابعة: النظام ومتبعوه قالوا: لا يقدر على الفعل القبيح لأنه مع العلم بقبحه سفه ودونه جهل وكلاهما نقص يجب تنزيهه تعالى عنه والجواب: أنه لا قبيح بالنسبة إليه فإن الكل ملكه فله أن يتصرف فيه على أي وجه أراد وإن سلم قبح الفعل بالقياس إليه فغايته عدم الفعل لوجود الصارف عنه وهو القبح وذلك لا ينفي القدرة عليه.

Answering the Objection from Bees and Spiders
Objection from bee and spider craftsmanship and the answer clarifying 'متقن'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from bee and spider craftsmanship is answered by clarifying the meaning of 'متقن' (skillfully made) and why it does not require absolute perfection.

Core Idea
Amazing craftsmanship, not absolute perfection, points to the Maker.

This lesson examines a classic objection to the argument from design: how can intricate actions by bees and spiders, which lack knowledge, indicate a divine Maker? The objection is answered by clarifying that the term 'متقن' refers to wondrous workmanship that bewilders minds, not to flawless perfection. The analogy of writing and speech shows that even imperfect works indicate a knowing author.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the examples of bees and spiders. Note that the objection claims these actions occur without knowledge, thus refuting the proof of God's knowledge from design.
The objection uses precise geometric details about bee honeycombs and spider webs.
2
Identify the core challenge
The objection argues that since bees and spiders produce intricate designs without apparent knowledge, the argument that intricate design proves a knowing Maker is refuted. Pinpoint this logical move.
3
Study the answer's key clarification
Read the answer: what does 'المتقن' mean? It is defined as 'الصنيع الغريب والترتيب العجيب' (wondrous workmanship and amazing arrangement). Notice that absolute perfection is not required.
This definition reframes the evidence: it's not about flawlessness but about astonishing skill.
4
Understand the analogy with writing and speech
The answer uses an analogy: even if a piece of writing could be improved, it still indicates a writer with knowledge. Similarly, the bee's and spider's works indicate a Maker despite any possible improvements or the agents' ignorance.
This shows that the objection misunderstands the kind of evidence required.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why the objection fails: the design argument does not claim that the design is maximally perfect, only that it exhibits marvelous order that points to a knowing cause. The answer successfully preserves the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( و ) نقول أيضاً : (إنه ) أي دليلك على إثبات علمه ( منقوض بفعل النحل لتلك البيوت المسدسة المتساوية بلا فرجار ومسطر واختيارها للمسدس لأنه أوسع من المثلث والمربع والمخمس ولا يقع بينها أي بين المسدسات فرج كما يقع بين المدورات وما سواها من المضلعات وهذا الذي ذكرناه لا يعرفه إلا الحذاق من أهل الهندسة وكذلك العنكبوت تلك تنسج البيوت وتجعل لها سدى ولحمة على تناسب هندسي بلا آلة مع أنه لا علم لهما بما يصدر عنهما وما يتضمنه من الحكم. ) والجواب عن الأول : أن المراد بالمتقن ما تشاهده من الصنيع الغريب والترتيب العجيب) الذي تتحير فيه العقول ولا تهتدي إلى كمال ما فيه من المصالح والمنافع ولا شك في دلالته على علم الصانع ) وتوضيحه ما ذكرنا في مثال الكتابة والخطاب إذ لا يشترط في الدلالة على العلم خلوه عن كل خلل واشتماله على كل كمال ) حتى لو أمكن أن يكتب أحسن ( أو يتكلم بأفصح منه لم يدل على علم .
And we also say: Indeed it—i.e., your proof for establishing His knowledge—is refuted by the act of the bees for those hexagonal, equal-sided houses without a compass and ruler, and their choice of the hexagon because it is more spacious than the triangle, square, and pentagon, and no gaps occur between them—i.e., between the hexagons—as occur between circles and other polygons. And this that we have mentioned is known only by the expert geometricians. And likewise the spider: it weaves houses and makes for them warp and weft with geometric proportion without a tool, while it has no knowledge of what issues from it and what it contains of wisdom. And the answer to the first: that what is meant by 'skillfully made' is what you observe of the wondrous workmanship and amazing arrangement at which minds are bewildered and cannot attain the perfection of the benefits and advantages in it, and there is no doubt that it indicates the Maker's knowledge. And its clarification is what we mentioned in the example of writing and speech, for it is not required for the indication of knowledge that it be free from every flaw and include every perfection, such that even if one could write better or speak more eloquently, it would not indicate knowledge.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the proof of God's knowledge based on the intricate actions of bees and spiders, which display remarkable design without apparent knowledge. The objection argues that if such design can occur without knowledge, then the argument from design fails. The answer clarifies that the term 'متقن' (skillfully made) refers to the amazing craftsmanship that points to a Maker, not to absolute perfection. The analogy of writing and speech shows that even if a piece of writing could be improved, it still indicates a knowing author. Thus the objection is refuted.

Correction: The passage clarifies that 'متقن' means wondrous workmanship and amazing arrangement, not absolute perfection. Even if the product could be improved, it still indicates a knowing Maker.

Key Takeaway

?

?
The bee and the spider act without knowledge, yet produce wonders that baffle experts. Does that humble you to realize that much of what you think you 'achieve' is actually God working through you? Will you consciously attribute your next success to Him rather than your own intelligence?
?

Are there objections you quietly hold against God—perhaps about unanswered prayers, injustice, or suffering—that might stem from misunderstanding His criteria? What would it cost you to surrender that objection today and trust His wisdom?

The Second Answer: God Creates Knowledge in Bees and Spiders
Author states the second answer and gives two possible mechanisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second answer to the objection: that it is possible for God to create knowledge in bees and spiders, refuting the assumption of ignorance.

Core Idea
Divine inspiration suffices to refute the charge of animal ignorance.

After conceding the skillfulness of bees' and spiders' actions, the opponent argues they lack knowledge. The second answer refuses to grant that premise, asserting that God could create knowledge in them or inspire them moment by moment. This shows that the objection's assumption of impossibility is not proven.

Guided Path
1
Read the core answer
Read the Arabic passage that states the answer: 'We do not concede that bees and spiders lack knowledge… because it is possible that God creates knowledge in them or inspires them.'
Notice the structure: rejection of the premise followed by a positive claim of possibility.
2
Identify the two mechanisms
The passage mentions two ways God could give knowledge: either by directly creating knowledge (خلق العلم) or by inspiring them moment by moment (إلهام حالاً فحالاً).
Both are possible, not actual—this is about possibility.
3
Understand the logical strategy
The answer does not prove that animals have knowledge; it only shows that their lack of knowledge is not necessary. Since the objection assumes impossibility, showing possibility is enough to refute it.
This connects to the broader method in kalam of rebutting a claim by undermining its basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثاني : أنا لا نسلم عدم علم النحل والعنكبوت بما يفعله لجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى فيهما علماً بذلك الفعل الصادر عنهما أو يلهمهما حالاً فحالاً ما هو مبدأ لذلك الفعل الصادر منهما. قوله : ( والجواب عن الثاني إلخ) وفي أبكار الأفكار أن هذا الجواب عند من يعترف بكون الحيوانات هي الفاعلة لأفعالها الاختيارية ومن قال : إنها مخلوقة لله تعالى فالجواب عنده أن الإحكام والإتقان ليس مستنداً إلى تلك الحيوانات بل إلى الله سبحانه فيجب أن تكون معلومة لله تعالى لا لها. ولعل المصنف إنما لم يتعرض له لأن الكسب أيضاً يقتضي العلم بالمكسوب القصدي كما دل عليه مثال الكتاب والخطاب أيضاً . قوله : ( لجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى إنما ذكر في التعليل الجواز مع أن ظاهره يدل على أن الإتقان يفيد جواز علم المتقن لا القطع به كما هو المدعى لأن مبنى النقض توهم استحالة علمها وإلا فلا وجه للقطع بعدمه بعد تسليم إمكانه فأشار إلى منع المبنى وادعاء علمهما أيضاً لإتقان فعلهما .
And the answer to the second: that we do not concede that the bees and spiders lack knowledge of what they do, because it is possible that God Most High creates in them knowledge of that action that issues from them, or inspires them moment by moment with what is the principle of that action that issues from them. His saying: 'And the answer to the second, etc.'—and in Abkar al-Afkar, that this answer is for one who acknowledges that animals are the agents of their voluntary actions. But if one says that they are created by God, then the answer is that the skillful craftsmanship is not attributed to those animals but to God, so it must be known to God, not to them. Perhaps the author did not address this because the act of acquisition (kasb) also requires knowledge of the intended acquired act, as indicated by the example of writing and speech. His saying: 'because it is possible that God creates'—he only mentioned possibility in the reasoning, although its outward meaning indicates that this skillful craftsmanship yields the possibility of the creator's knowledge, not certainty, as claimed, because the basis of the objection is the assumption of the impossibility of their knowledge; otherwise, there is no reason to be certain of its absence after admitting its possibility. So he pointed to refuting the basis and claiming that they have knowledge due to the skillfulness of their action.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage provides a second answer to the objection. It refuses to concede that bees and spiders lack knowledge, arguing that God could create knowledge in them or inspire them. The commentary from Abkar al-Afkar notes that this answer works for those who hold animals as agents; for those who say all actions are created by God, the skillfulness points directly to God's knowledge. The commentator also discusses why the author's approach uses possibility rather than certainty, explaining that the objection assumes impossibility, so showing possibility suffices to refute it.

B) By teaching them through prophets or angels
C) By instinct or habit
D) By making them learn from experience

Key Takeaway
The second answer refutes the objection by showing that animals' knowledge is possible through divine action, without needing to prove its actual existence.

?

?
