Nuance: Two Views on Animal Actions and Logical Strategy
Commentator explains scope of answer and logic of 'possibility'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the answer applies differently based on whether animals are agents or God creates their actions, and why the author uses 'possibility' to target the objection's assumption.

Core Idea
The answer's validity does not depend on animal agency; it refutes impossibility regardless.

The commentator Abkar al-Afkar explains that the second answer works from two perspectives: if animals are agents, God gives them knowledge; if God creates all actions, the skill points to His knowledge. Further, the author's choice of 'possibility' (جواز) is deliberate: it undermines the objection's assumption that animal knowledge is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'قوله : ( والجواب عن الثاني إلخ) وفي أبكار الأفكار...' and continues to the end. This contains the nuanced discussion.
This passage is from the commentary, not the original matn.
2
Distinguish the two perspectives
The passage explains that if animals are the agents of their own voluntary actions, the answer works by giving them knowledge. If instead God creates all actions, the skillfulness is attributed to God, so knowledge must be in God, not the animals.
Both perspectives ultimately defend the answer's coherence.
3
Understand the logical use of 'جواز'
The passage notes that the author mentions 'جواز' (possibility) even though the skillfulness might suggest certainty. This is because the objection was based on assuming impossibility (استحالة), so showing possibility is enough to refute that basis.
This highlights a common kalam method: refuting the premise rather than proving the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثاني : أنا لا نسلم عدم علم النحل والعنكبوت بما يفعله لجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى فيهما علماً بذلك الفعل الصادر عنهما أو يلهمهما حالاً فحالاً ما هو مبدأ لذلك الفعل الصادر منهما. قوله : ( والجواب عن الثاني إلخ) وفي أبكار الأفكار أن هذا الجواب عند من يعترف بكون الحيوانات هي الفاعلة لأفعالها الاختيارية ومن قال : إنها مخلوقة لله تعالى فالجواب عنده أن الإحكام والإتقان ليس مستنداً إلى تلك الحيوانات بل إلى الله سبحانه فيجب أن تكون معلومة لله تعالى لا لها. ولعل المصنف إنما لم يتعرض له لأن الكسب أيضاً يقتضي العلم بالمكسوب القصدي كما دل عليه مثال الكتاب والخطاب أيضاً . قوله : ( لجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى إنما ذكر في التعليل الجواز مع أن ظاهره يدل على أن الإتقان يفيد جواز علم المتقن لا القطع به كما هو المدعى لأن مبنى النقض توهم استحالة علمها وإلا فلا وجه للقطع بعدمه بعد تسليم إمكانه فأشار إلى منع المبنى وادعاء علمهما أيضاً لإتقان فعلهما .
And the answer to the second: that we do not concede that the bees and spiders lack knowledge of what they do, because it is possible that God Most High creates in them knowledge of that action that issues from them, or inspires them moment by moment with what is the principle of that action that issues from them. His saying: 'And the answer to the second, etc.'—and in Abkar al-Afkar, that this answer is for one who acknowledges that animals are the agents of their voluntary actions. But if one says that they are created by God, then the answer is that the skillful craftsmanship is not attributed to those animals but to God, so it must be known to God, not to them. Perhaps the author did not address this because the act of acquisition (kasb) also requires knowledge of the intended acquired act, as indicated by the example of writing and speech. His saying: 'because it is possible that God creates'—he only mentioned possibility in the reasoning, although its outward meaning indicates that this skillful craftsmanship yields the possibility of the creator's knowledge, not certainty, as claimed, because the basis of the objection is the assumption of the impossibility of their knowledge; otherwise, there is no reason to be certain of its absence after admitting its possibility. So he pointed to refuting the basis and claiming that they have knowledge due to the skillfulness of their action.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage provides a second answer to the objection. It refuses to concede that bees and spiders lack knowledge, arguing that God could create knowledge in them or inspire them. The commentary from Abkar al-Afkar notes that this answer works for those who hold animals as agents; for those who say all actions are created by God, the skillfulness points directly to God's knowledge. The commentator also discusses why the author's approach uses possibility rather than certainty, explaining that the objection assumes impossibility, so showing possibility suffices to refute it.

عنكبوت
جواز
كسب
إتقان
إحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the answer only works if animals are agents, but the commentary shows it also works if God is the direct creator.
Correction: The two perspectives are both valid; each leads to a coherent answer.

Checkpoint
Why did the author use 'جواز' (possibility) in the reasoning?
A) Because he was unsure
B) Because the objection assumes impossibility, so showing possibility refutes it
C) Because animals might not actually have knowledge
D) Because the skillfulness is not evidence

Key Takeaway
The second answer is flexible: it works whether animals are agents or not, and its use of possibility strategically undermines the objection's assumption of impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth about God because you thought it was impossible, without considering that it might simply be beyond your understanding? How can you train yourself to say 'it is possible' before rejecting something?
?
The author adapts his answer to different views of agency. In your own life, do you insist on a single way God must act, or are you open to multiple models of divine action? Which approach brings you closer to trust?
?
You often want certainty, but this lesson shows that refuting false assumptions can be more powerful than proving your own view. Can you identify a false assumption about God that you hold onto? What would it cost you to let it go?

The First Approach: Proof from Non-Materiality
Presentation of the first approach: non-materiality implies knowledge of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's non-materiality serves as a proof for His knowledge of all universals.

Core Idea
Non-material beings inherently know all universals.

The first approach to proving God's knowledge starts with a point already established: God is not a body, nor corporeal. From this non-materiality, the argument moves to a general principle: every non-material being is cognizant of all universals. Let's examine this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Recall God's non-materiality
Recall that earlier in the text, God was established as non-material (not a body, not corporeal). This is the foundation of the first proof.
Non-materiality here means being free from matter and its properties.
2
Identify the general principle
The passage states: 'Every non-material is cognizant of all universals.' This is the key premise linking non-materiality to knowledge.
Universals refer to general concepts, not particular instances.
3
Understand the logical structure
The proof combines God's non-materiality with the general principle. Since God is non-material, He must be cognizant of all universals.
The passage says the two premises were proven earlier; here they are applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : في علمه تعالى إثبات علمه تعالى أيضاً مسلكان : المسلك الأول أنه مجرد أي ليس جسماً ولا جسمانياً كما مر في التنزيهات . وكل مجرد فهو عاقل لجميع الكليات وقد برهنا فيما سلف على المقدمتين . المسلك الثاني : أنه تعالى يعقل ذاته وإذا عقل ذاته عقل ما عداه، أما الأول فلأن التعقل حضور الماهية المجردة عن العلائق المادية للشيء المجرد القائم بذاته . وهو حاصل في شأنه لأن ذاته مجردة غير غائبة عن ذاته فيكون عالماً بذاته. وأما الثاني : فلانه مبدأ لما سواه أي لجميعه إما بواسطة أو بدونها والعلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول فيكون عالماً بذاته ومعلولاته.
The Fourth Watch - Third Objective: On His Knowledge, exalted be He. Proving His knowledge, exalted be He, also (has) two approaches: The first approach is that He is non-material, i.e., neither a body nor corporeal, as passed in the transcendences. And every non-material is cognizant of all universals, and we have proven the two premises previously. The second approach: that He, exalted be He, cognizes His essence, and when He cognizes His essence, He cognizes what is other than it. As for the first (part): because cognition is the presence of the abstract quiddity, free from material relations, to the abstract thing subsisting by itself. And it is realized in His case because His essence is abstract, not absent from itself, so He is knowing of His essence. And as for the second (part): because He is the principle of what is other than Him, i.e., of all of it, either through an intermediary or without it, and knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, so He is knowing of His essence and of His effects.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the two approaches (مسلكان) for proving God's knowledge. The first approach uses God's non-materiality (being مجرد): since He is not a body, He is non-material, and every non-material being is cognizant of all universals. The second approach argues that God cognizes His essence, and through that He cognizes everything else. Cognition is defined as the presence of an abstract quiddity to a self-subsisting abstract entity. Because God's essence is abstract and not absent from itself, He knows Himself. Also, as the cause of all things, knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, so God knows all His effects.

Key Terms
مجرد
تعقل
كليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that non-materiality alone is insufficient for knowledge, but the argument posits a necessary connection.
Correction: The argument treats non-materiality as a sufficient condition for cognizing universals, a principle that was established earlier.

Checkpoint
What is the first approach (المسلك الأول) for proving God's knowledge?
A) God's non-materiality implies He knows all universals.
B) God's essence is simple, so He knows Himself.
C) God is the cause of all things, so He knows effects.
D) God's knowledge is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The first approach proves God's knowledge by linking His non-materiality to the general principle that non-material beings know all universals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God knows every universal without any need for learning, how does that challenge the way you seek knowledge—do you rely more on your limited intellect or on His infinite wisdom?
?
If God's knowledge is inherent and complete, why do you still doubt His plan in areas where you lack understanding? What step will you take today to surrender your ignorance to His omniscience?
?
You are a material being, limited to knowing through senses and study. How does the reality of God’s non-material, all-encompassing knowledge make you reconsider what you prioritize in your daily search for truth?

The Second Approach: Proof from Self-Knowledge and Causality
Presentation of the second approach: self-knowledge and causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's knowledge of His essence leads to knowledge of all other things through causality.

Core Idea
Knowing oneself as the cause yields knowledge of all effects.

The second approach to proving God's knowledge is more intricate. It argues that God knows His own essence, and because He is the cause of everything else, that self-knowledge extends to all His effects. Let's unpack this argument.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of cognition
The passage defines cognition (التعقل) as 'the presence of the abstract quiddity to the abstract thing subsisting by itself.' This definition is key to the first part of the argument.
Abstract quiddity (ماهية مجردة) means the essence stripped of matter.
2
Apply definition to God
Since God's essence is abstract and not absent from itself, His essence is present to Himself. Therefore, He knows His essence.
God's self-knowledge is immediate and direct.
3
Understand causality principle
The second part invokes a principle: knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect. As the cause of all things, God knows His effects.
This principle is taken as axiomatic in the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And every non-material is cognizant of all universals, and we have proven the two premises previously. The second approach: that He, exalted be He, cognizes His essence, and when He cognizes His essence, He cognizes what is other than it. And it is realized in His case because His essence is abstract, not absent from itself, so He is knowing of His essence. And as for the second (part): because He is the principle of what is other than Him, i.e., of all of it, either through an intermediary or without it, and knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, so He is knowing of His essence and of His effects.

Commentary

The first approach uses God's non-materiality (being مجرد): since He is not a body, He is non-material, and every non-material being is cognizant of all universals. Misconception: Some might think the argument is circular: God knows Himself because He knows effects, or vice versa.

Checkpoint

?

?

Allah's Necessary Self-Knowledge
Author proves Allah's necessary self-knowledge and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves that Allah necessarily knows Himself and responds to the objection that it could be merely possible.

Core Idea
Allah's self-knowledge is not merely possible—it is necessary.

This lesson examines a key argument from the author on why Allah must know Himself. The passage presents a proof from impossibility of the contrary and then responds to a potential objection. We will follow the reasoning step by step to see how the author defends divine self-knowledge as a necessary perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage once
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noting the key terms. Then read the English translation to get the overall flow.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إمكان المحال' which is central to the argument.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage begins by asserting that Allah knows Himself necessarily. Find this claim and note the proof that follows.
3
Understand the proof by absurdity
The proof shows that if Allah did not know Himself necessarily, it would be possible for a human to know complex topics without being aware of that knowledge—which is absurd.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objector says that if self-knowledge is merely possible, it need not be actual. The author responds by denying the implication and also by stating that any possible perfection for the Necessary Being must be actual.
Notice how the author uses the principle that 'the possibility of the impossible is impossible'.
5
Connect to broader principle
The passage concludes that whatever perfection is possible for the Necessary Being is actually established, or else ignorance and deficiency would follow. Reflect on how this upholds Allah's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عالم به بالضرورة وإلا جاز أن يكون أحدنا عالماً بالمجسطي والمخروطات وسائر العلوم الدقيقة الكثيرة المباحث المثبتة بالدلائل القطعية ولكن لا يمكنه أن يعلم أنه عالم به وإن التفت إلى ذلك وبالغ في الاجتهاد وذلك سفسطة ظاهرة وإذا لزم الإمكان ثبت المدعى لأن إمكان المحال محال والجواب أنه إن امتنع منه تعالى علمه بنفسه منعنا الملازمة وقلنا الضرورة التي ذكرتموها إنما هي فيمن يمكنه العلم بنفسه وإن أمكن له علمه بنفسه منعنا بطلان التالي المتضمن لهذا العلم الممكن بالفرض. وأيضاً قد مر بطلان ما ذكروه في إثبات أنه لا يعلم نفسه. قوله: (لأن إمكان المحال محال) وأيضاً قد ثبت أن ما يمكن للواجب من الكمالات فهو ثابت له بالفعل وإلا لزم الجهل والنقص تعالى عن ذلك فيلزم وقوع المحال.
He knows it necessarily, otherwise it would be possible that one of us is knowledgeable about the Almagest and cones and other precise sciences with many investigations established by definitive proofs, but cannot know that he knows it even if he attends to it and strives earnestly—that is manifest sophistry. And if possibility follows, the claim is established because the possibility of the impossible is impossible. The answer: if knowledge of Himself is impossible for Him, we deny the implication and say the necessity you mentioned only applies to one who can know himself; and if knowledge of Himself is possible for Him, we deny the falsity of the consequent which includes this knowledge assumed to be possible. Also, it has already passed that what they mentioned in proving that He does not know Himself is false. His statement: (because the possibility of the impossible is impossible) and also it has been established that whatever is possible for the Necessary from perfections is actually established for Him, otherwise ignorance and deficiency would follow—exalted is He above that—so the occurrence of the impossible follows.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents a proof and its defense concerning Allah's knowledge of Himself. The argument begins by asserting that Allah necessarily knows Himself, otherwise it would be possible for a human to know a complex science without being aware of that knowledge, which is absurd. The objectors claim that if Allah's self-knowledge is merely possible, it need not be actual. The author refutes this by stating that if the knowledge is possible, it must be actual for the Necessary Being because withholding a possible perfection implies ignorance and deficiency. The response also distinguishes that the necessity of self-knowledge applies only to one who can know himself—and Allah can. The sharh reinforces that the possibility of the impossible is itself impossible, and whatever perfection is possible for the Necessary is actual.

Key Terms
بالضرورة
إمكان المحال
الواجب
الكمالات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah's self-knowledge is merely possible, it need not be actual.
Correction: For the Necessary Being, any possible perfection must be actual, because withholding it would imply deficiency.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives that Allah's self-knowledge must be actual?
A) Because Allah would otherwise not be perfect.
B) Because the possibility of the impossible is impossible.
C) Because any human can know himself necessarily.
D) Because a human cannot know a complex science without being aware of it.

Key Takeaway
Allah must actually know Himself because any possible perfection for the Necessary Being is necessarily actual, and the contrary leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's knowledge of you is necessary and perfect, or do you sometimes act as if He might be unaware of your circumstances? How does the author's reductio argument expose the absurdity of doubting His self-knowledge?

?
When you reflect on your own ignorance of what you know, does the sophistry the author describes ever describe your spiritual state—knowing about Allah but failing to realize His intimate knowledge of you? How will you change that today?

Proof that God is Living from Knowledge and Power
Al-Iji states the proof that God is Living based on knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God is Living, based on the already-established attributes of knowledge and power.

Core Idea
A being that knows and acts with power must be alive — that is rational necessity.

In this passage, al-Iji establishes a foundational divine attribute — that God is Living (al-Hayy). Rather than beginning with an abstract definition, he points to a rational inference from two already-proven attributes: knowledge and power. The argument is simple but powerful, relying on a necessary connection known to all rational minds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the mention of consensus and the logical connection at the end: 'every knower and powerful being is necessarily living.'
The passage is concise — every word matters.
2
Identify the premises
Identify the two key premises: (1) God is Knowing and Powerful (from earlier proofs), and (2) Every being that is knowing and powerful is necessarily living. The second premise is presented as a self-evident truth.
3
Examine the consensus
Al-Iji says 'all have agreed' on God being Living, including people of various religions and even those without religion. This shows the universality of the belief.
He also notes consensus on God being Knowing except for an insignificant handful.
4
Understand the logical necessity
The conclusion 'God is Living' follows necessarily from the two premises. This is not an arbitrary statement but a rational deduction: if God knows and exercises power, He must be alive.
The term 'bi-ḍ-ḍarūra' (necessarily) emphasizes the logical force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أنه تعالى حي هذا مما اتفق عليه الكل من أهل الملل وغيرهم لأنه عالم قادر لما مر من الدلائل وقد أطبقوا أيضاً عليه أي على أنه عالم إلا شرذمة لا يعبأ بهم كما عرفته وكل عالم قادر فهو حي بالضرورة
Regarding that He, exalted, is Living: This is something upon which all have agreed, from people of religions and others, because He is Knowing, Powerful, due to the proofs that have passed. And they have also unanimously agreed upon it, i.e., that He is Knowing, except for a handful who are not taken into consideration as you have known. And every knower and powerful being is necessarily living.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a proof and statement of consensus regarding God being Living. The author (al-Iji) asserts that all people—from various religious traditions and even atheists—agree that God is Living. The reason given is that it has been established by previous proofs that God is Knowing and Powerful. There is consensus on His being Knowing except for an insignificant minority. Then he states a necessary truth: every being that is knowing and powerful is necessarily living. This means that, since we have already proven God's knowledge and power, it follows necessarily that He is Living. The argument is straightforward: major premise: every knower and powerful being is living; minor premise: God is knower and powerful; conclusion: God is living.

Key Terms
حي (Living)
عالم (Knowing)
قادر (Powerful)
الضرورة (necessity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'living' for God implies a physical body or biological life, but the passage does not define it here; the definition will come later.
Correction: The attribute 'Living' is affirmed rationally; its precise meaning (e.g., not requiring a body) is addressed in subsequent texts.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the proof that God is Living?
A) Scriptural evidence
B) Consensus of all people
C) The fact that He is Knowing and Powerful, and every knower and powerful being is necessarily living
D) The definition of life

Key Takeaway
God is proven to be Living by a rational necessity: every being that knows and exercises power must be alive, and God is both knowing and powerful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is truly Living – aware and responsive – do your prayers reflect a conversation with a living being or a mere ritual? What would change if you spoke to Him as someone who is actually listening right now?
?
You have just learned that knowledge and power necessitate life. In your own life, are you living as if you are accountable to a Living God who knows your every thought and can act on His will? Or do you act as if He is distant and unaware?
?
The passage says every knower and powerful being is necessarily living. God knows you completely and has absolute power over you. Does this truth make you tremble with awe, or do you feel indifferent? What is blocking your heart from responding to this reality?

The Three Stations and the Priority of Proving Possibility
Introduction to three stations and defense of the priority of possibility proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari scholars first prove the rational possibility of seeing God before addressing its occurrence in the hereafter.

Core Idea
Proving possibility is the logical foundation for accepting scriptural proofs of occurrence.

In this section, al-Amidi outlines the three stations of the debate on ru'ya: validity, occurrence, and refuting doubts. He then explains a key methodological point: why the Ash'aris prioritize proving possibility over occurrence. The discussion is not merely doctrinal but also about how to structure rational and scriptural arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three stations
Read the Arabic and translation once to get an overview. Notice that the author lists three stations: validity (sihha), occurrence (wuqu'), and answering the deniers.
The three stations structure the entire discussion.
2
Understand the objection and response
Pay attention to the hypothetical objection: why not just prove occurrence? The response clarifies that scriptural proofs can be rejected if possibility is denied, so possibility must be established first.
This shows a sophisticated awareness of argumentation strategy.
3
Reflect on the nature of the proofs
The commentator notes that even the proof of pure possibility is scriptural, but it is decisive. Compare this with the proofs of occurrence which are less definitive.
This highlights that some scriptural proofs are stronger than others.
4
Consider the grammatical note
At the end, there is a minor grammatical point about the word 'maqamat'. This is incidental but shows attention to precise wording.
Do not get distracted; focus on the main methodological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس فيما يجوز عليه تعالى ( أي يجوز أن يتعلق به كالرؤية والعلم بالكنه ) وفيه المقصد الأول : في الرؤية والكلام في الرؤية والكلام في الصحة وفي الوقوع وفي شبه المنكرين، فهاهنا ثلاثة مقامات المقام الأول : في صحة الرؤية وقد طال نزاع المنتمين إلى الملة فيها فذهبت الأشاعرة إلى أنه تعالى يصح أن يرى ومنعه الأكثرون ) . قال الآمدي : اجتمعت الأئمة أصحابنا على أن رؤيته تعالى في الدنيا والأخرى جائزة عقلا واختلفوا فيه جوازها سمعا في الدنيا فأثبته بعضهم ونفاه آخرون. وهل يجوز أن يرى في المنام، فقيل : لا . وقيل : نعم . والحق أنه لا مانع من هذه الرؤيا وإن لم تكن رؤية حقيقة ولا خلاف بيننا في أنه تعالى يرى ذاته والمعتزلة حكموا بامتناع رؤيته عقلاً لذي الحواس واختلفوا في رؤيته لذاته ولا بد أولاً من تحرير محل النزاع فنقول: إذا نظرنا إلى الشمس فرأيناها ثم غمضنا العين فعند التغميض نعلم الشمس علماً جلياً وهذه الحالة مغايرة نعم لكنه قوله : ( والكلام في الصحة وفي الوقوع فإن قلت : لم لم يقتصروا على أدلة الوقوع مع أنها تفيد الإمكان أيضاً؟ قلت لأنها كلها سمعيات ربما يدفعها الخصم بمنع إمكان المطلوب فاحتاجوا إلى بيان الإمكان أولاً والوقوع ثانياً، فإن قلت : المعول عليه من أدلة الإمكان المحض أيضاً سمعني إذ لا وثوق على الدليل العقلي هاهنا كما ستقف عليه، قلت: قطعي وليس في أدلة الوقوع المشهورة دليل بهذه الحيثية اللهم إلا أن يتشبث بالإجماع قبل ظهور المخالف، وبالجملة في الطريق الذي سلكوه اهتمام بإثبات الوقوع الذي هو المقصد الأصلي. قوله : فهاهنا ثلاث مقامات المقام الأول) في العبارة مساهلة لأنها لا تخلو إما أن تكون مقامة أو مقام فعلى الأول ينبغي أن يقول : المقامة الأولى بالتأنيث وعلى الثاني ينبغي أن يقول ثلاثة مقامات .
The fifth observation concerning what is permissible for Him, exalted (i.e., what can be attributed to Him, like vision and knowledge of essence). It contains the first objective: on vision (ru'ya) and speech (kalam). Discussion on vision and speech concerns validity (sihha), occurrence (wuqu'), and the doubts of the deniers. So here are three stations: the first station is on the validity of vision. The dispute among those belonging to the [Islamic] community has been prolonged. The Ash'aris held that it is valid to see Him, exalted, while the majority prevented it. Al-Amidi said: The imams of our companions have agreed that seeing Him, exalted, in this world and the hereafter is intellectually possible, but they differed on its permissibility according to revelation in this world: some affirmed it and others negated it. And is it permissible to see Him in a dream? It was said: no. And it was said: yes. The truth is that there is no impediment to this vision, even if it is not a real vision. And there is no disagreement among us that He, exalted, sees His own essence. The Mu'tazila ruled that it is intellectually impossible for Him to be seen by the senses, but they differed on His seeing Himself. It is necessary first to clarify the locus of dispute. So we say: When we look at the sun and see it, then close our eyes, at the moment of closing we know the sun with clear knowledge, and this state is different. Yes, but... His saying: 'Discussion on validity and occurrence. If you say: Why did they not confine themselves to proofs of occurrence, since they also imply possibility? I say: Because they are all scriptural (sam'iyyat), and the opponent might refute them by denying the possibility of the claimed object. So they needed to first demonstrate possibility, then occurrence. If you say: The relied-upon proofs of pure possibility are also scriptural, since there is no certainty in the rational proof here, as you will see. I say: It is decisive, and among the well-known proofs of occurrence there is no proof with this quality, unless one clings to consensus before the appearance of the opponent. In short, in the path they took, there is concern to prove occurrence, which is the main objective.' His saying: 'So here are three stations, the first station' – in the expression there is laxity, because it is either 'maqama' or 'maqam'. On the first, he should say: 'al-maqama al-ula' with feminine gender, and on the second, he should say: 'thalathatu maqamat'.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an outline of the discussion on the permissibility of seeing God (ru'ya). The commentator begins by introducing the fifth observation, which deals with what is possible for God, specifically vision and knowledge of the essence. The first objective within this is on ru'ya and kalam. The discussion is divided into three stations: validity (sihha), occurrence (wuqu'), and refuting the doubts of deniers. The first station is validity. The commentator notes the prolonged debate, with Ash'aris affirming that it is valid to see God, while the majority (including Mu'tazila) deny it. He then quotes al-Amidi, stating that Ash'ari imams agree on intellectual possibility of seeing God in this world and the hereafter, but differ on its scriptural permissibility in this world. The commentator then addresses seeing God in dreams: some negate, some affirm; the truth is it is not forbidden but it is not a real vision. He notes that there is no disagreement among Ash'aris that God sees Himself, while Mu'tazila deny that He can be seen by senses but differ on His self-vision. The commentator then explains the need to clarify the locus of dispute with an example of seeing the sun and then knowing it with eyes closed. Next, he presents an objection-response regarding why they start with possibility (imkan) rather than occurrence: the proofs for occurrence are scriptural and can be rejected if possibility is denied, so they first establish possibility. The commentator counters that even the proof of pure possibility is scriptural, but it is decisive, and the well-known proofs of occurrence lack that decisiveness, except perhaps consensus before the appearance of opponents. Finally, he notes a grammatical laxity: the phrase 'three stations' should be either 'thalathu maqamat' if 'maqam' is masculine, or 'thalathu maqamat'? He corrects that if it is 'maqama' (feminine), it should be 'al-maqama al-ula', and if 'maqam', then 'thalathatu maqamat'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that proving the occurrence of seeing God is sufficient and that possibility does not need separate proof.
Correction: The commentator emphasizes that if possibility is not established, an opponent can reject scriptural proofs of occurrence by simply denying that seeing God is possible. Hence, proving possibility is a necessary first step.

Checkpoint
Why do Ash'ari scholars first prove the possibility (imkan) of seeing God before proving its occurrence (wuqu')?
A) Possibility is easier to prove
B) Proofs of occurrence are scriptural and might be rejected if possibility is not established
C) Occurrence is impossible
D) They want to avoid rational proofs

Key Takeaway
Before accepting scriptural proofs for the occurrence of seeing God, one must first establish its rational possibility to prevent rejection based on impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a theological claim that challenges you, do you instinctively dismiss it as impossible without first honestly examining whether it could be possible? This lesson teaches that possibility must be addressed before occurrence. How will you cultivate the discipline of first asking 'Is it possible?' before judging a scriptural claim?
?
You have just read that even the proof of possibility is scriptural and decisive. Does that unsettle your assumption that reason and revelation are separate? In which areas of your faith have you perhaps relied too much on your own rational assessment, ignoring that the very possibility of a matter may only be established through scripture?
?
The scholars prioritized proving possibility over occurrence to safeguard the scriptural proofs. Is there a matter in your life—perhaps a command or a promise from Allah—that you have set aside because it seemed too difficult or unlikely? Will you now reconsider its possibility before concluding it cannot happen?

Seeing God in Dreams: Likeness and Reality
Refutation of those denying dream visions and distinction between mithal and mithl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that seeing God in a dream is a vision by likeness (mithal), not a literal vision of His essence, and the theological nuance involved.

Core Idea
Dream visions of God use a likeness, not the reality — nothing is like Him.

This passage from the commentary addresses a delicate question: Can believers see God in dreams? The answer is nuanced — it is possible, but not a literal vision. The scholars (muhaqqiqun) make a crucial distinction between an example (mithal) and the thing itself (mithl). We will explore this distinction, its application to dream visions, and a critique of an analogy used by al-Ghazali.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English side by side to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the key terms mithal and mithl.
2
Understand the refutation
The passage begins by refuting those who deny dream visions of God. Identify the basis of the refutation: the scriptural proof mentioned later.
3
Grasp the mithal vs mithl distinction
The researchers say: 'The example is not the thing itself.' This is the core conceptual tool. Understand why it matters — because nothing is like God.
4
Analyze al-Ghazali's analogy and the objection
Al-Ghazali compares seeing God in a dream to seeing Gabriel in human form. But the commentator raises a problem: if Gabriel appears as someone else, he is not literally Gabriel at that moment? Consider the implication.
5
Reflect on the takeaway
What did you learn about the nature of dream visions? How does this protect against anthropomorphism?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ونفاه آخرون يرده أن الدليل السمعي الذي سيذكره يدل على جوازها في الدنيا. قوله : ( والحق أنه لا مانع من هذه (الرؤيا وإن لم تكن رؤية حقيقية قال المحققون : المثال غير المثل ورؤية الحق في المنام بمثاله لا بمثله إذ لا مثل له :قال الغزالي : وكذا رؤية جبرائيل عليه السلام في صورة دحية الكلبي وغيره، وفيه نظر إذ لا يكون الآتي بالوحي نفس جبرائيل عليه السلام حينئذ وفيه من المقاصد مالا يخفى، وقد أشار إليه الشيخ في مفتاح الغيب .
His saying: '(And others negated it) is refuted by the fact that the scriptural proof which he will mention indicates its permissibility in this world.' His saying: '(And the truth is that there is no impediment to this vision, even if it is not a real vision). The researchers (muhaqqiqun) said: The example (mithal) is not the same as the thing (mithl). And seeing the Truth in a dream is by its likeness (bi-mithalihi), not by itself (bi-mithlihi), since there is nothing like Him. Al-Ghazali said: Similarly, the vision of Gabriel, peace be upon him, in the form of Dihya al-Kalbi and others. There is a problem (nazar) in this, because then the one bringing revelation would not be Gabriel himself at that time. And in it there are objectives that are not hidden. The Shaykh alluded to it in Miftah al-Ghayb.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on seeing God in dreams. It begins with a refutation: those who negate seeing God in this world are refuted because the scriptural proof indicates its permissibility. Then the commentator addresses the truth about dream vision: there is no impediment, but it is not a real vision. The researchers (muhaqqiqun) distinguish between an example (mithal) and the thing itself (mithl). Seeing God in a dream is by a likeness (bi-mithalihi), not by His own essence (bi-mithlihi), because nothing is like Him. The commentator then quotes al-Ghazali, who compares it to seeing Gabriel in the form of Dihya al-Kalbi. However, the commentator notes a problem (nazar): if Gabriel appeared in another form, then the one bringing revelation would not be Gabriel himself. He remarks that there are subtle objectives in this discussion, and the Shaykh (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) alluded to them in his work Miftah al-Ghayb. This passage thus clarifies the nature of dream vision as a symbolic representation, not a literal perception of God, and also raises a theological nuance about angelic apparitions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing God in a dream is a literal vision of His essence.
Correction: The researchers clarify that dream visions are by a likeness (mithal), not by the essence (mithl), because nothing is like God.

Checkpoint
According to the researchers (muhaqqiqun), how is seeing God in a dream understood?
A) It is a real vision of God's essence
B) It is a vision by a likeness (mithal), not by the thing itself (mithl)
C) It is impossible
D) It occurs only for prophets

Key Takeaway
Seeing God in a dream is a vision by a likeness (mithal), not a literal vision of His essence, preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine seeing God in a dream, do you unconsciously picture a form or shape? How does this lesson's insistence on 'likeness, not the thing itself' force you to let go of that image? Will you actually train your heart to seek only His attributes, not a mental picture?
?
If every vision of God in a dream is only a mithal, then what do you truly desire when you long to see Him? Are you willing to accept that in this life you may only receive signs and symbols? How will you respond when such a dream comes — will you be satisfied, or will you demand more?
?
The companion's critique of al-Ghazali shows that even scholars struggled with the limits of analogy in divine matters. What hidden assumptions do you hold about how God reveals Himself? Will you submit to the principle 'nothing is like Him' even when your heart aches for closeness? What concrete change in your spiritual practice will this truth demand starting today?
